Rational Judgments
intermediate
10 min
The Self-Evidence of Non-Corporeal Existence: Razi's First Proof
Razi introduces the dispute and presents the first proof based on disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the impossibility of a non-corporeal existent is not self-evident, using disagreement among rational people as evidence.

Core Idea
Disagreement among rational thinkers proves a claim is not self-evident.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi begins by presenting the view of some who claim that a non-corporeal being is self-evidently impossible. He then counters that this is not self-evident, offering the first of several proofs: if it were self-evident, there would be no disagreement about it. Yet history shows widespread disagreement, even among Muslims and philosophers. This lesson explores that argument and its supporting evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opposing claim
Read the first part of the passage where Razi states that some people claim every existent must either interpenetrate or be separated in one of six directions, making a non-corporeal being impossible, and that this is self-evident.
This sets up the view Razi will refute.
2
Identify Razi's position
Notice Razi says the majority of rational people hold that this impossibility is not self-evident, and the matter requires proof. This is the view he adopts.
Razi aligns with the majority, including philosophers and many Muslim scholars.
3
Follow the logical structure of the proof
The passage presents a conditional: if self-evident, no disagreement. Then Razi shows that disagreement exists, so it cannot be self-evident. This is a simple modus tollens argument.
Focus on the logical move: disagreement → not self-evident.
4
Examine the evidence for disagreement
Razi lists examples: philosophers affirm non-embodied intellects and souls; our own self-awareness points to a non-corporeal self; prominent Muslims (Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Sunnis like al-Ghazali) affirm a transcendent God not confined to space.
These examples show that denial of self-evidence is widespread among rational people.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that since there is clear disagreement, the proposition cannot be self-evident. This does not prove the proposition false, only that it requires proof.
Avoid the misconception that disagreement equals falsity; it only removes self-evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في بيان أن إثبات موجود ليس بجسم ولا حالا في الجسم ليس ممتنع الوجود في بديهة العقل اعلم أن كثيرا من الناس يزعمون أن كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر أو مباينا عنه في جهة من الجهات الست وزعموا أن إثبات موجود لا يكون حالا في هذا العالم الجسماني ولا مباينا عنه بحسب شيء من الجهات ممتنع الوجود ثم زعموا أن العلم بهذا الامتناع علم بديهي ضروري غني عن الحجة والدليل وأما الجمهور الأعظم من العقلاء فإنهم اتفقوا على أن إثبات موجود ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز وليس في العالم ولا في خارج العالم ليس معلوم الامتناع في بديهة العقل بل الأمر في إثباته ونفيه موقوف على الدليل فإن دل الدليل على إثباته وجب القضاء به وإلا وجب التوقف في إثباته ونفيه وهذا القول هو الذي نذهب إليه ونقول به والذي يدل على أن الأمر كذلك وجوه الحجة الأولى أن نقول لو كانت هذه القضية بديهة لامتنع وقوع الاختلاف فيها بين العقلاء لكن الاختلاف واقع فيها فوجب أن لا تكون بديهية بيان الملازمة أن الجمع العظيم من العقلاء لا يجوز إطباقهم على إنكار الضروريات وبيان أن هذا الاختلاف واقع هو أن الفلاسفة اتفقوا على إثبات موجودات ليست بمتحيزة ولا حالة في المتحيز مثل العقول والنفوس والهيولي واتفقوا على أن الشيء الذي يشير إليه كل إنسان بقوله أنا موجود وليس بجسم ولا بجسماني ولم يقل أحد من العقلاء إنهم في هذا القول منكرون للبديهات بل نقول إن جمعا من أكابر المسلمين اختاروا هذا المذهب مثل معمر بن عباد السلمي من المعتزلة ومثل أبي سهل النوبختي ومحمد بن النعمان من الرافضة ومثل أبي القاسم الراغب وأبي حامد الغزالي من أهل السنة والجماعة وأيضا فأكثر العقلاء من أرباب الملل والنحل المختلفة أطبقوا على تنزيه الله تعالى عن كونه متحيزا وعن كونه حالا في المتحيز وعن كونه داخلا في العالم وخارجا عنه فيثبت بما ذكرنا أن هذه القضية لو كانت بديهة لامتنع وقوع الاختلاف فيها ولما ثبت أن الاختلاف واقع فيها بل الأكثرون ينكرون تلك القضية ويقطعون بفسادها لزم القطع بأنها ليست من البديهيات
Chapter One on showing that affirming an existent that is not a body and not inhering in a body is not impossible in the self-evidence of the intellect. Know that many people claim that every two existents must necessarily be either one of them permeating the other or separated from it in one of the six directions. And they claimed that affirming an existent that neither inheres in this corporeal world nor is separate from it with respect to any of the directions is impossible of existence. Then they claimed that knowledge of this impossibility is self-evident necessary knowledge, independent of proof and evidence. As for the vast majority of rational people, they agreed that affirming an existent that is not a spatial particular (mutahayyiz), nor inhering in a spatial particular, and neither in the world nor outside the world, is not known to be impossible in the self-evidence of the intellect. Rather, the matter of affirming or denying it depends on proof. If proof indicates its affirmation, then judgment must be made accordingly; otherwise, one must suspend judgment on its affirmation and denial. This is the view we hold and affirm. That which indicates that the matter is as such are arguments. The first argument: we say: if this proposition were self-evident, disagreement about it among rational people would be impossible, but disagreement about it occurs, so it must not be self-evident. Explanation of the implication: a great multitude of rational people cannot agree upon denying self-evident truths. And proof that this disagreement occurs is that the philosophers agreed on affirming existents that are not spatial particulars and not inhering in spatial particulars, such as the intellects, souls, and prime matter. And they agreed on the thing that every human points to by saying 'I' exists, and it is not a body nor corporeal. And no rational person said that they are, in this statement, deniers of self-evident truths. Rather, we say that a group of prominent Muslims adopted this doctrine, such as Muammar ibn Abbad al-Sulami from the Mu'tazila, and Abu Sahl al-Nawbakhti and Muhammad ibn al-Numan from the Rawafid, and Abu al-Qasim al-Raghib and Abu Hamid al-Ghazali from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah. Moreover, most rational people from various religious communities and sects agreed on declaring God transcendent above being a spatial particular, above inhering in a spatial particular, and above being inside or outside the world. Thus it is established from what we have mentioned: if this proposition were self-evident, disagreement about it would be impossible, and since it is established that disagreement about it occurs—indeed, the majority deny that proposition and are certain of its falsity—it necessarily follows that it is not among self-evident propositions.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage introduces the first of two arguments against the claim that the existence of a non-corporeal, non-embodied being is self-evidently impossible. The author first states the position of some people: that every pair of existents must either interpenetrate or be separated in one of the six directions, and therefore a being that neither inheres in the world nor is separate is impossible, and this impossibility is self-evident. The author then presents the position of the majority of rational people: that this impossibility is not self-evident; rather, proof is required. The author adopts this latter view. The first proof is presented: if the proposition were self-evident, there would be no disagreement among rational people about it. However, disagreement exists. The author explains the implication: rational people cannot agree to deny self-evident truths. Evidence of disagreement is given: philosophers affirm non-embodied existents (intellects, souls, prime matter), and everyone's self-awareness is of a non-corporeal self. Many prominent Muslims (Mu'tazila, Rafida, and even some Sunnis like al-Ghazali) along with most rational people from various religions affirm a transcendent God. Therefore, the proposition is not self-evident.

Key Terms
بديهة
بديهي
ضروري
متحيز
حال
جهات ست
تقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Disagreement proves that a proposition is false
Correction: Razi uses disagreement only to deny self-evidence, not to deny truth. The proposition may still be true, but it requires proof.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what would be the consequence if the proposition that a non-embodied existent is impossible were self-evident?
A) All rational people would agree on it.
B) Disagreement about it would be impossible.
C) It would require proof.
D) The philosophers would accept it.

Key Takeaway
Widespread disagreement among rational thinkers disproves the claim that a non-corporeal existent is self-evidently impossible, but does not disprove its truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that even great scholars disagreed about whether non-corporeal existence is self-evident. What is one belief you hold that you treat as 'obvious,' but which you have never actually examined with evidence? Will you test it today, or let it remain unchallenged?
?
He lists scholars from different schools whom he disagrees with, yet respects their intellect. When you encounter someone whose religious view differs from yours, do you genuinely consider their argument, or do you dismiss it? What would it cost you to listen—and what might you lose by refusing?
?
Razi's argument forces you to ask: 'Is my certainty based on evidence or on familiarity?' If you removed all external supports and had only your reason, which of your core beliefs would survive? Are you willing to face that question?

The Second Proof: Division of Existence Necessitates a Third Category
Razi presents the second proof based on the division of existence and refutes the objection of self-evident impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second proof that existence must be divided into three categories and why the third category is not self-evidently impossible.

Core Idea
Self-evidence requires immediate denial, which the third category lacks.

Razi presents a second argument for the necessary existent by analyzing the correct division of existence. He shows that any complete division must include a third category that is neither a body nor an accident. When an objector claims this third category is impossible by self-evidence, Razi demonstrates that true self-evidence must produce immediate rejection, unlike the case at hand.

Guided Path
1
Understand the correct division
Read the passage to see how Razi divides existence into three categories: spatial particular, inhering in a spatial particular, or neither. This division is considered self-evidently correct.
The third category is the one that will be defended.
2
Recognize the objection
An objector argues that even if the division is logically complete, the third category is known by self-evidence to be impossible. Identify this objection in the text.
This objection is a common challenge to arguments for the necessary existent.
3
See the refutation
Razi refutes the objection by comparing two statements: 'the third category is impossible' and 'one is half of two'. He shows the intellect immediately rejects the second but not the first, so it is not self-evident. Follow his reasoning carefully.
This refutation hinges on the concept of self-evidence as immediate intellectual denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية إن صريح العقل شاهد بأن التقسيم الصحيح أن يقال الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزا أو حالا في المتحيز أو لا يكون متحيزا ولا حالا في المتحيز ولو أنا قلنا الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزا أو حالا في المتحيز واقتصرنا على هذا القدر فإن بدائه العقول قاطعة بأن هذا التقسيم غير تام بل لا بد وأن نضم إليه القسم الثالث وهو الذي لا يكون متحيزا ولا حالا في المتحيز حتى يتم ذلك التقسيم ولو أن قائلا قال هب أن هذا القسم معتبر بحسب التقسيم إلا أنه معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة فنقول ليس الأمر كذلك لأنا إذا عرضنا على بديهة العقل قولنا إن هذا القسم ممتنع الوجود وعرضنا أيضا على البديهة أن الواحد ضعف الاثنين فإنا نجد البديهة جازمة بكذب هذه الثانية وغير جازمة بالأولى ومن أنكر التفاوت بين الصورتين كان مكابرا في أجل البديهيات وأقواها فيثبت بما ذكرنا إن إثبات موجود ليس
The second argument: indeed, clear reason testifies that the correct division is to say: the existent is either a spatial particular (mutahayyiz) or inhering in a spatial particular, or it is not a spatial particular nor inhering in a spatial particular. If we were to say: the existent is either a spatial particular or inhering in a spatial particular, and we restrict ourselves to this extent, then the self-evident principles of the intellects are categorical that this division is incomplete. Rather, we must append to it the third category, which is that which is not a spatial particular nor inhering in a spatial particular, so that the division is completed. If someone were to say: suppose this category is considered according to the division, but it is known to be impossible by self-evidence, we say: the matter is not so, because when we present to the self-evidence of the intellect our statement that this category is impossible of existence, and we also present to the self-evidence that one is half of two, we find that the self-evidence is decisive in rejecting the second and not decisive regarding the first. Whoever denies the difference between the two cases is being obstinate regarding the most manifest and strongest self-evident truths. Thus it is established from what we have mentioned that affirming an existent which is not... [text breaks]
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument. The author states that clear reason shows the correct division of existence is threefold: (1) spatial particular (mutahayyiz), (2) inhering in a spatial particular, or (3) neither spatial particular nor inhering in a spatial particular. If one omits the third category, the division is incomplete. An objector then says: even if we accept the third category logically, we know by self-evidence that it is impossible. The author refutes this by comparing two propositions: 'this category is impossible' and 'one is half of two'. The intellect immediately knows the second is false, but it is not immediate with the first. Therefore, the first is not self-evident. Anyone who claims equal obviousness is obstinate. The conclusion (cut off) is that affirming such an existent is not self-evidently impossible.

Key Terms
بديهة
بديهي
متحيز
حال
تقسيم صحيح
مكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third category (neither body nor accident) is self-evidently impossible.
Correction: Self-evidence requires immediate intellectual denial. Since the intellect does not immediately reject the claim that the third category is impossible, it is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that the third category is known by self-evidence to be impossible?
A) By appealing to the consensus of philosophers.
B) By showing that the intellect does not judge it impossible with the same immediacy as a clear falsehood like 'one is half of two'.
C) By citing Quranic verses.
D) By arguing that the third category is actually possible.

Key Takeaway
The third category of existence (neither body nor accident) is not self-evidently impossible because the intellect does not reject it with the same immediacy as a clear falsehood, leaving open the possibility of a necessary existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the division of existence—bodies, accidents, and that which is neither—do you ever feel in your heart that what is beyond the physical world is unreal or impossible? Has that feeling subtly influenced your belief in a non-physical Creator? Razi’s argument shows that your intellect does not immediately reject the third category. Will you today honestly examine whether you have been rejecting the possibility of the Necessary Existent due to lack of experience rather than genuine self-evidence? Commit to reconsidering one area where you have dismissed the unseen too quickly.
?
The difference between immediate denial and mere unfamiliarity is crucial. In your spiritual life, have you ever brushed aside a truth about Allah because it seemed too abstract or unfamiliar, without honestly testing whether your intellect truly rejects it? Self-evidence is a high bar. Are you willing to pause and let your intellect speak before you dismiss any of Allah’s attributes as impossible? What is one belief about Allah that you have not fully accepted, and will you reflect on it with an open mind today?
?
Razi calls those who deny the difference between the two propositions obstinate. Think of a time when you were obstinate in rejecting something about Allah or His creation because it didn’t fit your assumptions. Can you see how obstinacy blocks you from deeper faith? Today, will you ask Allah to remove the obstinacy from your heart regarding one specific truth that is not self-evident but that revelation or reason points to? Write that down and resolve to act on it.

Rational Judgments

First Proof: Disagreement Shows Non-Badihi Status
Argument that disagreement proves non-self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the existence of disagreement among rational thinkers proves that the proposition 'immaterial existents exist' is not self-evident.

Core Idea
Disagreement proves non-self-evidence: if a truth were self-evident, all would agree.

This passage introduces the first proof against the claim that immaterial existence is self-evident. The author points out that if it were self-evident, universal agreement would be required. But in fact, many rational thinkers—including philosophers and theologians—hold this position, yet many others deny it is self-evident. Thus, the proposition is not badīhī.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Get a sense of the author's argument flow.
Focus on the logical structure: premise, evidence, conclusion.
2
Identify the evidence presented
Notice that the author lists groups who affirm immaterial existents: philosophers, and specific Muslim scholars. This shows that disagreement exists.
The list includes Muʿtazila, Shia, and Sunni figures to show broad consensus despite disagreement.
3
Understand the logical argument
The author argues: if the proposition were self-evident (badīhī), disagreement would be impossible. Since disagreement occurs (many deny it is self-evident), it cannot be badīhī.
This is a modus tollens argument: if P then Q; not Q, therefore not P.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that the proposition is not self-evident; it requires proof. This sets up the need for the subsequent theological arguments.
This is a foundational move in Ilm al-Kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضروريات. وبيان أن هذا الاختلاف واقع : هو أن الفلاسفة اتفقوا على إثبات موجودات ليست بمتحيزة ، ولا حالة في المتحيز مثل : العقول والنفوس والهيولي . واتفقوا على أن الشيء الذي يشير إليه كل إنسان بقوله : أنا موجود ، وليس بجسم ولا بجسماني. ولم يقل أحد من العقلاء : إنهم في هذا القول منكرون للبديهات ، بل نقول . إن جمعا من أكابر المسلمين اختاروا هذا المذهب مثل معمر بن عباد السلمي من المعتزلة ، ومثل أبي سهل النوبختي ، ومحمد بن النعمان من الرافضة . ومثل أبي القاسم الراغب ، وأبي حامد الغزالي من أهل السنة والجماعة وأيضا : فأكثر العقلاء من أرباب الملل والنحل المختلفة أطبقوا على تنزيه الله تعالى عن كونه متحيزا ، وعن كونه حالا في المتحيز ، وعن كونه داخلا في العالم ، وخارجا عنه . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن هذه القضية لو كانت بديهة لامتنع وقوع الاختلاف فيها ، ولما ثبت أن الاختلاف واقع فيها ، بل الأكثرون ينكرون تلك القضية ، ويقطعون بفسادها ، لزم القطع بأنها ليست من البديهيات .
The necessary things. And an explanation that this disagreement is real: it is that the philosophers agreed on affirming existents that are not located [mutaḥayyiz] nor inherent in a located thing, such as the intellects, souls, and matter. And they agreed that the thing which every person points to when they say 'I' exists, and it is not a body nor corporeal. And none of the rational people said that in this statement they are deniers of self-evident truths. Rather, we say: Indeed a group of the greatest Muslims chose this doctrine, such as Muʿammar ibn ʿAbbād al-Sulamī from the Muʿtazila, and such as Abū Sahl al-Nawbakhtī, and Muḥammad ibn al-Nuʿmān from the Rāfiḍa, and such as Abū al-Qāsim al-Rāghib and Abū Ḥāmid al-Ghazālī from Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jamāʿah. And also: Most rational people from the various religious communities agreed on the transcendence (tanzīh) of God Most High from being located, from being inherent in a located thing, from being inside the world, and from being outside it. So it is proven by what we have mentioned: that if this proposition were self-evident (badīha), disagreement about it would be impossible, and since disagreement about it is established—rather the majority deny that proposition and are certain of its falsity—it necessarily follows that it is not among the self-evident things.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof that the existence of a non-bodily, non-accidental existent is not a self-evident (badīhī) proposition. The author begins by noting that philosophers unanimously affirm incorporeal entities like intellects and souls. Even the human self (ana) is known as a non-physical reality. No rational person considers this denial of self-evident truths. Moreover, eminent Muslim theologians (like al-Ghazālī and some Muʿtazila) held this view. The author then argues that if the existence of an immaterial being were self-evident, universal agreement would be required. But in fact, most people deny that this is self-evident; rather, they consider it demonstrable. Therefore, the proposition is not badīhī.

Key Terms
بديهة (badīha)
متحيز (mutaḥayyiz)
حال في المتحيز (ḥāll fī al-mutaḥayyiz)
تنزيه (tanzīh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that widespread acceptance of a proposition makes it self-evident.
Correction: The author distinguishes between consensus and self-evidence. Self-evident truths require universal agreement; disagreement proves they are not self-evident.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why is the existence of immaterial entities not considered a self-evident (badīhī) proposition?
A) Because no rational person affirms it.
B) Because there is disagreement about it, which cannot happen for a self-evident truth.
C) Because the philosophers unanimously rejected it.
D) Because it contradicts sensory experience.

Key Takeaway
The existence of disagreement proves that a proposition is not self-evident; thus, immaterial existence requires demonstration, not intuition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about God simply because it 'seemed obvious' to you, only to later encounter someone who genuinely disagrees? How did that challenge your certainty, and what did you do?
?
If disagreement about a religious truth proves it is not self-evident, then which of your core beliefs about God have you actually examined with evidence? Are you prepared to defend them from the sources?
?
The author lists scholars from different denominations who agreed on God's transcendence. When you differ with a fellow Muslim on a matter of creed, do you assume they are simply 'denying the obvious'? What would it mean for you to treat their position as something that requires evidence rather than dismissal?

The Correct Division of Existent and Its Self-Evidence
Proof that the third category of existent is not self-evidently impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof demonstrates that the existence of a non-physical, non-accidental entity is not self-evident, and why a threefold division of existents is rationally required.

Core Idea
Reason demands three categories of existent; the third is not self-evidently impossible.

In this proof, the author examines the rational division of existents. He shows that plain reason requires a third category beyond body and accident. Then he refutes the objection that this category is self-evidently impossible by comparing it to a clear falsehood, showing that the mind does not judge it with the same certainty. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the structure: the author states the correct division, then addresses an objection, and finally gives a refutation.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the threefold division of existent: mutaḥayyiz (body), ḥāll fī al-mutaḥayyiz (accident), and neither. Understand why the author insists that leaving out the third makes the division incomplete.
The author says that plain reason (ṣarīḥ al-ʿaql) judges the incomplete division as insufficient.
3
Understand the objection and refutation
The objection claims the third category is self-evidently impossible. The author responds by comparing two statements: 'the third category is impossible' and 'one is half of two'. The intellect immediately knows the second is false but does not judge the first with the same certainty, proving it is not self-evident.
This comparison test is a key method in kalām to distinguish self-evident from non-self-evident claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : إن صريح العقل شاهد بأن التقسيم الصحيح أن يقال : الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً أو حالاً في المتحيز، أو لا يكون متحيزاً ولا حالا في المتحيز . ولو أنا قلنا : الموجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً ، أو حالا في المتحيز . واقتصرنا على هذا القدر. فإن بدائه العقول قاطعة بأن هذا التقسيم غير تام . بل لا بد وأن نضم إليه القسم الثالث ، وهو الذي لا يكون متحيزا ، ولا حالا في المتحيز حتى يتم ذلك التقسيم . ولو أن قائلا قال : هب أن هذا القسم معتبر بحسب التقسيم ، إلا أنه معلوم الامتناع بالبديهة . فنقول : ليس الأمر كذلك ، لأنا إذا عرضنا على بديهة العقل قولنا : إن هذا القسم ممتنع الوجود ، وعرضنا أيضا على البديهة : أن الواحد ضعف الاثنين . فإنا نجد البديهة جازمة بكذب هذه الثانية ، وغير جازمة بالأولى . ومن أنكر التفاوت بين الصورتين كان مكابرا في أجل البديهيات وأقواها فيثبت بما ذكرنا : إن إثبات موجود ليس بمتحيز ولا حال في المتحيز ليس من البديهيات البتة
The second proof: Indeed, plain reason witnesses that the correct division is to say: The existent is either located (mutaḥayyiz) or inherent in a located thing (ḥāll fī al-mutaḥayyiz), or it is neither located nor inherent in a located thing. And if we said: The existent is either located or inherent in a located thing, and we limited ourselves to this extent, then the self-evident principles of the intellect would be decisive that this division is incomplete. Rather, we must add to it the third category, which is that which is neither located nor inherent in a located thing, so that the division becomes complete. And if someone said: 'Suppose this category is valid according to the division, yet its impossibility is known by self-evidence (badīha),' we reply: It is not so. For if we present to the self-evident intellect our statement: 'This category is impossible in existence,' and also present to the self-evident intellect that 'one is half of two,' we find that the self-evident faculty is certain of the falsehood of the second statement, but not certain of the first. And whoever denies the difference between the two cases is being obstinate in the greatest and strongest of self-evident truths. So it is established by what we have mentioned that affirming an existent that is neither located nor inherent in a located thing is absolutely not among the self-evident truths.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. The author first states that reason itself demands a threefold division of existents: (1) a body (mutaḥayyiz), (2) an accident inherent in a body (ḥāll fī al-mutaḥayyiz), and (3) that which is neither. If we ignore the third category, the division is clearly incomplete. The author then addresses an objection: someone might concede the division but claim that the third category is self-evidently impossible. The author responds by comparing two propositions: 'The third category is impossible' and 'One is half of two.' Our self-evident intuition affirms the falsehood of the second proposition immediately, but it does not do so for the first. This difference proves that the impossibility of the third category is not self-evident. Therefore, the existence of such a category (immaterial existents) is not badīhī.

Key Terms
تقسيم (taqsīm)
متحيز (mutaḥayyiz)
حال في المتحيز (ḥāll fī al-mutaḥayyiz)
بديهة (badīha)
ممتنع (mumtaniʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third category (non-bodily, non-accidental existent) is self-evidently impossible because it is not sensory.
Correction: Self-evidence requires immediate intellectual judgment. The author shows that the intellect does not judge the impossibility of the third category with the same certainty as it judges clearly false statements like 'one is half of two'. Therefore, it is not self-evident.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, how does the author demonstrate that the impossibility of the third category (non-bodily, non-accidental existent) is not self-evident?
A) By noting that the division into three categories is incomplete without the third.
B) By comparing the judgment of impossibility with a clearly self-evident falsehood (one is half of two) and showing that the mind is not certain about the former.
C) By citing the consensus of philosophers.
D) By arguing that such an existent contradicts causality.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of a non-physical, non-accidental existent is not self-evident because the intellect does not judge it with immediate certainty, unlike clearly self-evident false statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you dismissed an idea as impossible simply because it didn't match your sensory experience? How does this passage challenge you to re-examine that dismissal?
?
Can you think of a belief you hold with certainty that you have never tested against the intellect's immediate judgment? What would it take for you to apply the author's test to that belief?
?
If the existence of a non-physical reality is not self-evidently false, how must you adjust your search for truth? Are you willing to entertain possibilities beyond what your senses can grasp?

Third Proof: The Universal Concept of Man
Proof from the abstract nature of the concept 'man' that the Creator transcends matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the abstract concept of man provides a rational basis for affirming God's transcendence over matter.

Core Idea
The universal concept 'man' is abstract, not sensory, proving that non-material reality is rationally conceivable.

This lesson presents the third proof for God's transcendence, drawn from the nature of universal concepts. The author argues that the concept 'man' is a single meaning shared by all individuals, yet it is not itself a physical object. By reflecting on this, we see that the mind grasps non-sensory realities, making it reasonable to affirm a Creator beyond matter. The argument is reinforced by a quotation from Ibn Sina's *al-Isharat*.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the central claim that 'man' is an abstract concept shared by all individuals despite their differences.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the objection calling the reasoning sophistry. Then read the Ibn Sina quote to see how it defends the abstraction of universals.
Ibn Sina's argument uses the example of 'man' being predicated of many individuals with a single meaning.
3
Connect to the Creator
Observe how the author concludes: if an abstract concept exists in the mind, then a transcendent Creator is not improbable. Reflect on the logical link.
The proof moves from the intelligibility of the abstract concept to the possibility of a non-sensory Creator.
4
Summarize the proof
Try to restate the third proof in your own words. Include the objection and how Ibn Sina's argument refutes it.
This will help solidify the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إنا نعلم بضرورة : أن أشخاص الناس مشتركة في المعنى المفهوم من قولنا: إنسان ومتباينة بتعيناتها وخصوصياتها وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة .. وهذا يدل على أن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان : مفهوم مجرد عن الشكل المعين ، والحيز المعين. فثبت أن الإنسان معقول مفهوم مجرد . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المخلوقات منزها عن لواحق الحس وعلائق الخيال ؟ فإن قيل : هذا الكلام مغالطة ، وذلك لأن قال ابن سينا في : الإشارات والتنبيهات، في النمط الرابع من الإلهيات : اعلم : أنه قد يغلب على أوهام الناس : أن الموجود هو المحسوس ، وأن ما لا يناله بجوهره ، ففرض وجوده محال . وأن ما لا يتخصص بمكان أو وضع بذاته كالجسم ، أو لسبب ما هو فيه كأحوال الجسم. فلا حظ له من الوجود . وأنت بتأني لك أن تتأمل نفس المحسوس ، فتعلم منه بطلان قول هؤلاء ، لأنك - ومن يستحق أن يخاطب - تعلمان أن هذه المحسوسات قد يقع عليها اسم واحد . لا على سبيل الاشتراك الصرف ، بل بحسب معنى واحد . مثل اسم الانسان . فإنكما لا تشكان في أن وقوعه على زيد وعمرو ، بمعنى واحد : موجود . وذلك المعنى الموجود ، لا يخلو إما أن يكون بحيث يناله الحس، أو لا يكون . فإن كان بعيدا من أن يناله الحس، فقد أخرج التفتش من الحوسات : ما ليس بمحسوس . وهذا أعجب. وإن كان محسوسا لله لا محالة وضع وأين ومقدار معين وكيف معين ، لا يتأتي أن يحس ، بل ولا أن يتخيل إلا كذلك . فإن كل محسوس وكل متخيل ، فإنه يتخصص لا محالة لشيء من هذه الأحوال . وإذا كان كذلك لم يكن ملائما لما ليس بتلك الحال ، فلم يكن مقولا على كثيرين مختلفين في تلك الحال فإذن الإنسان من حيث هو واحد الحقيقة بل من حيث حقيقته الأصلية التي لا تختلف فيها الكثرة غیر محسوس ، بل معقول صرف. وكذلك الحال في كل كلي. ولعل قائلا منهم يقول : إن الإنسان مثلا إنما هو إنسان من حيث له أعضاء ، من يد وعين وحاجب وغير ذلك . ومن حيث هو كذلك ، فهو محسوس ، فتنبه ونقول له : إن الحال في كل عضو كلي ، مما ذكرته أو تركته . كالحال في الإنسان نفه . أهـ فثبت : أن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان ، مفهوم مجرد عن . فقد أخرج التفتيش عن المحسوس ما هو معقول مجرد . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فكيف يستبعد في العقل أن يكون خالق المحسوسات منزها عن لواحق الحس وعلائق الخيال ؟
The third proof: We know by necessity that individual humans share in the meaning understood from our saying 'man' and differ in their particularities and specifics, and that by which they share is other than that by which they differ. And this indicates that man insofar as he is man is a concept abstracted from specific form and specific location. So it is established that man is an intelligible abstract concept. And if this is the case, how can it be deemed improbable in reason that the Creator of creatures is transcendent of sensory accidents and imaginative attachments? If it is said: This speech is a sophistry, because... (then the quote from Ibn Sina: 'He said in his book al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat, the fourth pattern of metaphysics: Know that it often dominates people's imaginations that the existent is the sensible, and that what the essence cannot reach, its existence is impossible. And that which is not specified by a place or position in itself like a body, or by a cause in which it inheres like states of a body, has no share in existence. But you can, by deliberation, reflect on the sensible itself and learn from it the falsehood of these people's statement. For you and the one who deserves to be addressed both know that these sensibles are given a single name, not in a purely equivocal way, but according to a single meaning, like the name 'man.' For you both do not doubt that its application to Zayd and Amr is with a single meaning that exists. And that existing meaning is either such that the sense can reach it or not. If it is far from being reached by sense, then the investigation has extracted from the sensibles what is not sensible—and this is more wondrous. And if it is sensible, then necessarily it has a position, a location, a specific quantity, and a specific quality; it cannot be sensed, nor even imagined, except so. For every sensible and every imaginary thing is necessarily specified by some of these states. If this is so, it would not be compatible with what is not in that state, so it would not be predicated of many that differ in that state. Therefore, man insofar as he is one reality—rather insofar as his original reality in which multiplicity does not differ—is not sensible but a pure intelligible. And the same holds for every universal. Perhaps one of them might say: 'Man, for example, is man only insofar as he has limbs, such as hand, eye, eyebrow, etc., and insofar as he is that, he is sensible.' Then pay attention and say to him: The situation for every universal limb, whether mentioned or omitted, is the same as the situation for man himself. End of quote.) So it is established that man insofar as he is man is an abstract concept. Thus the investigation has extracted from the sensible what is an abstract intelligible. And if this is the case, how can it be deemed improbable in reason that the Creator of sensible things is transcendent of sensory accidents and imaginative attachments?
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof, focusing on the abstract nature of universal concepts. The author argues that the concept of 'man' is a single meaning shared by all individuals, abstracted from specific forms and locations. This shows that the human mind grasps non-sensory realities. If such an abstract concept exists in our minds, it is not unreasonable that the Creator, who is the source of all existence, is even more transcendent of sensory and imaginative attributes. The author then anticipates an objection calling this reasoning sophistry, and responds by quoting Ibn Sina's argument from al-Isharat: the universal 'man' cannot be purely sensible because it is predicated of many individuals with different sensible qualities; thus, the common meaning is intelligible, not sensible. The quote reinforces the point that we apprehend abstract realities. The author concludes by reiterating that the existence of a transcendent Creator is not improbable.

Key Terms
إنسان (insān)
مفهوم مجرد (mafhūm mujarrad)
معقول (maʿqūl)
حس (ḥiss)
خيال (khayāl)
تنزيه (tanzīh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the concept 'man' is purely sensory and reducible to physical features.
Correction: The author shows that the universal meaning 'man' cannot be purely sensible because it is predicated of many individuals with different sensible qualities; thus, it is an abstract intelligible.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what does the universal concept 'man' demonstrate about the possibility of a transcendent Creator?
A) That the Creator must be a body like humans.
B) That abstract concepts are impossible, so the Creator is unknowable.
C) That if an abstract concept like 'man' exists in the mind, it is not inconceivable that the Creator is also abstract and transcendent.
D) That sensory experience is the only valid source of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The universal concept 'man' is a non-sensory intelligible, showing that the mind can conceive of non-material realities, thereby making God's transcendence rationally plausible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Ibn Sina's proof confronts the assumption that only what is tangible is real. When you act as if your spiritual state doesn't matter because it's 'invisible', are you not falling into the same error? What specific step will you take today to acknowledge the reality of the non-material?

The proof shows that the mind can grasp realities beyond sensory experience. If you accept this, then why do you still doubt the reality of the Hereafter or the unseen? What would it cost you to live today as if the unseen is more certain than what you see?

The Commonality Among Humans: Two Answers to the Objection
Objection-and-answer with two responses and their critiques

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author addresses the objection that commonality exists only in the mind, and why both answers are ultimately insufficient.

Core Idea
Commonality exists mentally but proving its external reality requires a stronger argument.

In this passage, the author tackles a critical objection: if the commonality among humans (like 'humanity') has no external existence, then how can we speak of a shared reality? He offers two answers, but candidly acknowledges their limitations. This lesson guides you through his reasoning and the subsequent critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it in depth. Then read the English translation to get a sense of the author's argument. Notice the two answers and the objections that follow each.
The passage is dense; read slowly and note the transitions.
2
Identify the objection and the two answers
The objector claims the commonality among humans only exists in the mind. The author first calls this sophistry, then gives two distinct answers. Identify where each answer begins and ends.
The first answer starts with 'First' (الوجه الأول) and the second with 'Second' (الوجه الثاني).
3
Examine the first answer and its critique
The first answer argues that the concept of human must exist externally because it is part of an existent human's quiddity. The author then immediately presents a critique showing this only proves a conceptual distinction, not a separate external reality.
Focus on the reasoning: concept → part of quiddity → exists → must be external → but then the critique.
4
Examine the second answer and its critique
The second answer concedes the concept exists only mentally but argues that this still shows the intellect can conceive an abstract entity. The critique is that the intellect may still judge that such an entity cannot be free from position and location if it existed externally.
The author concludes that the proof is weak despite being relied upon by Ibn Sina.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدر المشترك بين أشخاص الناس ليس له وجود خارج الذهن ، بل الموجود في الخارج هو الذوات المتعينة ، والأشخاص المتباينة ، فأما القدر المشترك فلا وجود له إلا في الذهن . والنزاع إنما وقع في أنه : هل يوجد في الأعيان موجود مجرد عن الوضع والحيز ؟ فأين أحمد البابين من الآخر ؟ فيثبت : أن هذا الكلام مغالطة محضة . والجواب عنه من وجهين : الأول : إنه ثبت بالدليل الذي ذكرناه : أن القدر المشترك بين الأشخاص الإنسانية هو مجرد المفهوم من معنى لفظ الإنسان . نقول : هذا المفهوم إما أن يكون موجوداً ، وإما أن يكوم معدوما محضا . والثاني باطل . لأن المفهوم من أن كون الإنسان إنسانا ، جزء من ماهية هذا الإنسان ، والعدم المحض يمتنع يكون جزءا من ماهية الموجود فيثبت : أنه موجود . فإما أن يكون موجوداً في الأعيان ، وإما أن لا يكون . والثاني باطل . لأن الإنسان المعين موجود في الأعيان . وما كان موجودا في الأعيان كان جزء ماهيته أيضا موجوداً في الأعيان ، لأن بديهة العقل شاهدة بأن المركب لا يوجد إلا عند وجود جميع أجزائه . فلما كان المفهوم من الإنسان جزءا من ماهية هذا الإنسان ، وثبت أن هذا الإنسان موجود في الأعيان ، لزم أن يكون الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان موجودا في الأعيان. لكن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان مغاير لمعنى الوضع والحيز . فيثبت : أن الإنسان من حيث هو مع قطع النظر عن لواحقه ، وعن عوارضه : مجرد عن الوضع والحيز. وهذا غاية ما يمكن ذكره في تقرير هذا الكلام ، مع أن البحث باق فيه . فإن لقائل أن يقول : هذا الكلام يفيد أن الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان ، مغاير لمعنى الوضع والحيز ، ولا يفيد أن المفهوم من الإنسان ينفك عن هذه المعاني . والمطلوب : إثبات موجود تنفك حقيقته في الوجود الخارجي عن هذه المفهومات . وما ذكر تموه لا يدل عليه . الوجه الثاني في الجواب : أن نقول : هب أن هذا المفهوم المجرد لا وجود له إلا في الذهن ، إلا أن هذا يدل على أن صريح العقل لا يستبعد تصور موجود مجرد عن الوضع والحيز ونحن لا نطلب في هذا المقام إلا هذا القدر . فأما الجزم بأن هذا الشيء موجود أو ليس بموجود فذاك موقوف على الدليل المنفصل . ولقائل أن يقول : إن العقل إذا فهم معنى الإنسانية فإنه يحكم بأن هذا المعنى إذا وجد في الأعيان فإنه لا ينفك عن الوضع والحيز . والمقصود من هذا البحث : أن العقل هل يجوز إثبات موجود في الأعيان بشرط كونه مجردا عن الوضع والحيز ؟ وهذا الكلام لا يفيد هذا المطلوب ، فيثبت : أن هذا الدليل ليس بقوى في إفادة هذا المطلوب . والشيخ الرئيس أبو علي ذكره في الإشارات وعوّل عليه في هذا الباب . إلا أن البحث فيه ما ذكرناه
The commonality among individual humans has no existence outside the mind; rather, what exists externally is the determinate essences and the distinct individuals. As for the commonality, it has no existence except in the mind. The dispute is only about: does there exist in external entities an entity abstracted from position and location? So what relation does one door have to the other? So it is established that this speech is pure sophistry. The answer to it is from two aspects: First: It is established by the proof we mentioned that the commonality among human individuals is merely the concept from the meaning of the word 'human'. We say: this concept is either existent or pure non-existent. The second is false, because the concept of 'human being human' is a part of the quiddity of this human, and pure non-existence cannot be a part of the quiddity of the existent. So it is established that it is existent. Then it is either existent in external entities or not. The second is false, because the specific human is existent in external entities. And whatever is existent in external entities, the part of its quiddity is also existent in external entities, because the intuition of the intellect witnesses that the composite only exists when all its parts exist. So when the concept of human is a part of the quiddity of this human, and it is established that this human exists in external entities, it follows that human qua human exists in external entities. But human qua human is different from the meaning of position and location. So it is established that human qua human, disregarding its accidents and its attributes, is abstracted from position and location. This is the utmost that can be mentioned in establishing this speech, though the investigation remains in it. For one could say: this speech indicates that human qua human is different from the meaning of position and location, but it does not indicate that the concept of human is separated from these meanings. And the desired is to establish an existent whose reality in external existence is separated from these concepts. And what you have mentioned does not indicate it. The second aspect in the answer: we say: suppose that this abstract concept has no existence except in the mind, yet this indicates that the pure intellect does not find it impossible to conceive an existent abstracted from position and location. And we do not seek in this context except this much. As for asserting that this thing is existent or not existent, that depends on a separate proof. And one could say: when the intellect understands the meaning of humanity, it judges that if this meaning exists in external entities, it is not separated from position and location. And the purpose of this investigation is: does the intellect permit the establishment of an existent in external entities on condition of being abstracted from position and location? This speech does not serve this desired. So it is established that this proof is not strong in serving this desired. And the Chief Master Abu Ali mentioned it in al-Isharat and relied on it in this chapter, but the investigation therein is as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence. The objector argues that the commonality (e.g., 'humanity') shared among individual humans has no external reality—only mental existence. The author initially dismisses this as sophistry (مغالطة محضة) and then gives two answers. 

The first answer: The concept 'human' is either existent or pure non-existence. It cannot be pure non-existence because it is part of the quiddity of a real existent human; pure non-existence cannot be a part of an existent. Therefore the concept exists. It must exist either in external entities or only in the mind. It cannot be only in the mind, because a specific human exists externally, and the part of its quiddity must also exist externally—since a composite only exists when all its parts exist. Hence 'human qua human' exists externally. But 'human qua human' is distinct from position and location, so there is an external entity (humanity) free from these. However, the author immediately acknowledges a remaining objection: this only shows that the concept is different from position/location, not that it is actually separated from them in external reality. The desired proof requires an entity whose very reality is free from these concepts, not merely a conceptual distinction. So the first answer is insufficient.

The second answer concedes that the abstract concept exists only in the mind, but that itself shows that the intellect can conceive an existent abstracted from position and location—which is all that is needed for this stage. Whether such an entity actually exists externally requires a separate proof. The objector may still insist that when the intellect considers humanity, it judges that if it existed externally it would never be free from position and location. Thus the argument does not prove the possibility of such an abstract entity in external reality. The author concludes that the proof is weak, despite being relied upon by Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh al-Ra'is) in al-Isharat.

Key Terms
قدر مشترك
مغالطة محضة
الوجه الأول
الوجه الثاني
الإنسان من حيث هو إنسان

بديهة العقل

The Fourth Proof: Self-Awareness Without Spatial Attributes
The author proves that abstract entities can be conceived without spatial attributes by reflecting on self-awareness during deep thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how self-awareness during deep thought provides a proof that abstract entities can be conceived without spatial attributes.

Core Idea
Self-awareness proves that existence can be known apart from location and shape.

This lesson presents the fourth proof from the author, which uses a common human experience—deep thought—to demonstrate that we can have knowledge of something without knowing its location or shape. By reflecting on our own self-awareness when absorbed in a difficult problem, we realize that we know ourselves as existing even when we are not thinking about any spatial attributes. This establishes that it is logically valid to conceive of an entity that is abstracted from such attributes, paving the way for arguments about the nature of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the author describes the state of deep thought and the self-awareness involved.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify the premises: (1) during deep thought one can say 'I judged' or 'I understood', (2) this requires self-awareness, (3) in that state one is heedless of shape/location. Then see how these lead to the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this proof tells us about conceiving of an abstract entity. Why is this step important for later theological arguments?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الواحد منا حال ما يكون مستغرق الفكر والرؤية في استخراج مسألة معضلة . فقد يقول في نفسه : إني حكمت بكذا أو اعترفت بكذا فهو حال ما يقول في نفسه إني عقلت كذا وحكمت بكذا . لا بد وأن يكون عارفاً بنفسه إذ لو لم يعرف نفسه لامتنع منه أن يحكم على نفسه بأنه حكم بكذا ، أو عرف كذا . ثم إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أنه في تلك الحالة قد يكون غافلا عن معنى الشكل [والوضع والحيز (۱) ] والمقدار , فضلا عن أن يعلم كون ذاته في حيز وموصوفه بشكل ومقدار . فيثبت : أن العلم بالشيء الموجود في الأعيان قد يحصل عند عدم العلم بحيزه أو بشكله أو مقداره . وذلك يفيد القطع بأن الشيء المجرد عن هذه الأشياء يصح أن يكون معقولا
The fourth proof: That one of us, when he is deeply absorbed in thought and vision in extracting a difficult problem, may say in himself: 'I judged such-and-such' or 'I acknowledged such-and-such'. So when he says in himself 'I understood such-and-such' or 'I judged such-and-such', he must be aware of himself, because if he did not know himself, it would be impossible for him to judge about himself that he judged such-and-such or knew such-and-such. Then we know by necessity that in that state he may be heedless of the meaning of shape, [position and location,] and magnitude, let alone knowing his essence to be in a location or described by shape and magnitude. So it is established that knowledge of a thing existing in external entities can occur in the absence of knowledge of its location, or its shape, or its magnitude. And that yields certainty that a thing abstracted from these things is valid to be conceived.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a proof (حجة) for the conceivability of an abstract entity free from spatial attributes. The author argues from ordinary self-awareness during deep thought. When a person is absorbed in solving a difficult problem, they may reflect on their own judgments (e.g., 'I judged this' or 'I understood that'). In doing so, they are necessarily aware of themselves as the subject of these judgments. However, during that state, they are typically heedless of any shape, position, location, or magnitude of themselves. They do not think about their own body's location or shape. Yet they still have knowledge of themselves as an existing thing. Therefore, it is possible to have knowledge of an existing entity without simultaneously knowing its location, shape, or magnitude. This implies that it is valid for the intellect to conceive of a thing that is abstracted from these spatial attributes. The proof establishes that abstract existence is at least conceivable, even if its actual external existence requires further argument.

Key Terms
الحجة الرابعة
العلم بالشيء
غافلا
شكل
وضع
حيز
مقدار
مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof claims that the self is actually abstract in external existence.
Correction: The proof only shows that it is conceivable for a thing to be abstracted from spatial attributes; actual abstract existence requires further argument.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof claim about self-awareness during deep thought?
A) The self is always aware of its shape and location
B) The self cannot know itself without knowing its location
C) The self can be known without knowledge of its shape or location
D) The self is not an existing thing

Key Takeaway
Self-awareness during deep thought shows that an existing thing can be known without knowledge of its location or shape, thus abstract entities are conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a moment when you were completely absorbed in thought. Did you feel a sense of 'I' that existed independently of your body? If so, how does that experience challenge your attachment to material things? Will you now seek to know that abstract 'I'—your soul—more deeply?
?
If you can know yourself without knowing your shape or location, what does that imply about the ultimate reality of your existence beyond this physical world? How should this affect the way you prioritize your spiritual life over your physical desires?
?
The proof argues that abstract entities are conceivable. If you truly accept that, are you willing to submit to a Creator who is absolutely abstract—without location, shape, or magnitude? What would change in your worship if you internalized that He is not confined to any place?

The Fourth Proof: Self-Awareness Without Spatial Attributes
Sanusi's fourth proof: self-awareness without spatial attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth proof uses introspection to establish that one can know oneself without knowledge of spatial attributes, thereby proving that an abstracted existent is conceivable.

Core Idea
Self-awareness in deep thought proves that existence does not require shape or place.

Sanusi presents a powerful argument from ordinary human experience. When we are deeply absorbed in solving a problem, we can still be aware of ourselves without any thought of our body's shape or location. This introspective observation becomes the basis for a rational proof that immaterial existence is conceivable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This proof is based on a common experience; try to recall a similar moment in your own life.
2
Identify the key inference
Notice that the author argues that self-knowledge is necessary for making a self-attribution. If a person says 'I judged,' they must know themselves as the subject.
3
Notice the absence of spatial awareness
The author points out that in the state of deep thought, the person is heedless of shape, position, place, and quantity. Yet self-knowledge remains.
This is the crucial empirical observation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that knowledge of an existent can occur without spatial attributes, so an entity abstracted from these is validly conceivable. This is the point of the proof.
Think about how this supports the possibility of an immaterial soul or God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : إن الواحد منا حال ما يكون مستغرق الفكر والرؤية في استخراج مسألة معضلة . فقد يقول في نفسه : إني حكمت بكذا أو اعترفت بكذا فهو حال ما يقول في نفسه إني عقلت كذا وحكمت بكذا . لا بد وأن يكون عارفاً بنفسه إذ لو لم يعرف نفسه لامتنع منه أن يحكم على نفسه بأنه حكم بكذا ، أو عرف كذا . ثم إنا نعلم بالضرورة : أنه في تلك الحالة قد يكون غافلا عن معنى الشكل [والوضع والحيز (١) ] والمقدار ، فضلا عن أن يعلم كون ذاته في حيز وموصوفه بشكل ومقدار . فيثبت : أن العلم بالشيء الموجود في الأعيان قد يحصل عند عدم العلم بحيزه أو بشكله أو مقداره . وذلك يفيد القطع بأن الشيء المجرد عن هذه الأشياء يصح أن يكون معقولا
The fourth proof: When one of us is deeply absorbed in thought and vision in extracting a difficult problem, he may say to himself: I have judged such-and-such or I have acknowledged such-and-such. So when he says in himself: I have understood such-and-such and judged such-and-such, he must be cognizant of himself, for if he did not know himself, it would be impossible for him to make a judgment about himself that he judged such-and-such or knew such-and-such. Then we know necessarily that in that state he may be heedless of the meaning of shape, position, place, and quantity, let alone knowing that his essence is in a place and described by a shape and quantity. So it is established that knowledge of a thing existing in external entities may occur without knowledge of its place, shape, or quantity. And that yields the certainty that the thing abstracted from these things is valid to be intellectually conceived.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-ḥujja al-rābiʿa) that an abstracted existent is conceivable. It is an argument from introspection. The author describes a scenario where a person is completely absorbed in solving a difficult intellectual problem. In this state, the person may say to himself that he has understood or judged something. For such a self-attribution to be possible, he must have self-knowledge; otherwise, he could not attribute the judgment to himself. However, the author points out that in this state of deep thought, the person may be entirely heedless (ghāfil) of any awareness of his own shape (shakl), position (wadʿ), place (ḥayyiz), or quantity (miqdār). He does not know that his essence is in a place or has a shape. From this, the author concludes that it is possible to have knowledge of an existent thing without knowing its spatial attributes. Therefore, an entity abstracted from these attributes is a valid object of intellection. This proof is designed to show that existence without spatial properties is not contradictory.

Key Terms
الحجة الرابعة
مستغرق الفكر
عارفاً بنفسه
غافلا عن معنى الشكل
المقدار
مجرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-awareness always includes awareness of the body.
Correction: In deep thought, one can be self-aware while entirely heedless of shape, place, or quantity.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof conclude from the state of deep thought?
A) That self-awareness requires knowledge of the body.
B) That one can know one's self without knowing one's spatial attributes.
C) That the intellect cannot conceive of abstract entities.
D) That the self is identical to the body.

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that we can know ourselves without any spatial attributes, proving that immaterial existence is rationally conceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are completely absorbed in a problem, have you ever noticed that you forget your body? What does that tell you about who you truly are beyond your physical form? How can you live today more aware of your immaterial self rather than being consumed by bodily concerns?
?
If your self-awareness can exist without any shape or place, then what part of you will remain when your body dies? Are you preparing that part for its eternal journey? What is one specific habit you can change starting now to prioritize your soul over your body?

Refuting the Self-Evidence Claim
Al-Razi refutes the self-evidence claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi argues that the claim that God must be a body or mode is not self-evident, and how he uses the logic of sense perception to expose its weakness.

Core Idea
Sensory imagination cannot dictate necessary attributes of God.

Al-Razi opens his refutation of anthropomorphists by challenging the assumption that rationality settles the matter. He argues that what seems obvious to the senses cannot be transposed onto the Divine. In this passage, he demonstrates that the very logic of extension and indivisibility leads to absurdity, and therefore the opponent's charge of self-evidence is baseless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two possibilities for a sensed object: extended (which implies division) or non-extended (which implies an insignificant point). Find these and note the consequences al-Razi draws.
3
Understand the refutation of self-evidence
Ask yourself: if both options lead to absurdities when applied to God, how can the opponent claim that denying body/mode is self-evidently false? Al-Razi concludes the opponent is obstinate.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical move
Notice how al-Razi labels the opponent as 'obstinate' (متعنت). This is a strong accusation that highlights the perceived irrationality of the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه ليس بصغير ولا بحقير. ومعلوم أن هذا الذي ذكروه مما لا يقبله الحس والخيال البتة ، بل لا يقبله العقل البتة . لأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس . إن حصل له امتداد في الجهات والأحياز كان أحد جانبيه مغايراً للثاني ، وذلك يوجب الانقسام، وإن لم يحصل له امتداد في شيء من الجهات لا في اليمين ولا في اليسار ولا في الفوق ولا في التحت ولا في القدام ولا في الخلف ، كان نقطة غير منقسمة ، وكان في غاية الصغر والحقارة . وإذا لم يبعد عندهم التزام كونه تعالى غير قابل للقسمة مع القول بكونه عظيها غير متناه فكيف حكموا بأن القول بكونه تعالى ليس بمتحيز [ ولا حـال في المتحيز (۱) ] مدفوع في بديهة العقل . فيثبت : أن الذاهب إلى هذا القول : متعنت . يحكم على ما كان بديهي البطلان بالصحة ، ويحكم على غير البديهي بكونه بديهيا . وأما الحنابلة الذين التزموا الأجزاء والأبعاض . فهم أيضا معترفون بأن ذات الله تعالى مخالفة لذوات هذه المحسوسات . فإنه تعالى لا يساوي هذه الذوات في قبول الاجتماع والافتراق ، وفي الصحة والمرض وفي الحياة والموت والصلابة واللين والاستئناس بالغير، والتوحش بسبب الوحدة . فهم معترفون بأن هذه الأشياء ممتنعة الثبوت في حق الله تعالى. فإنا إذا قلنا لهم : لو وصل شيء إلى ذاته. فهل يمكنه أن يغوص فيه أولا يمكنه ؟ فإن أمكنه كانت ذاته بمنزلة الماء والهواء. فتتمزق وتتفرق تلك الذات وإن تعذر عليه ذلك كانت ذاته بمنزلة الحجر الصلد . وأيضا : الحي الذي لا يأكل ولا يشرب ولا يطيب قلبه بسبب الاستئناس بالغير ، ولا تستوحش نفسه بسبب التفرد : غير معقول . فإن قيل للحنابلة : هذه الكلمات . قالوا : إنه تعالى يخالف خلقه . فلا يجوز أن يقاس حاله بحال غيره . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنهم قد اعترفوا في هذا المقام : بأن حكم الوهم والخيال غير مقبول في حق الله تعالى . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فكيف يمكنهم أن يحكموا بأن حكم الوهم والخيال في أن كل موجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً ، وإما أن يكون حالا في المتحيز : يجب أن يكون مقبولا ؟
For it is neither small nor insignificant. And it is known that what they mentioned is not accepted by sense and imagination at all, nor is it accepted by reason at all. Because the thing pointed to according to sense: if it has extension in directions and spaces, one of its sides is different from the second, and that necessitates division; and if it has no extension in any direction — neither right nor left, nor above nor below, nor front nor back — it would be an indivisible point, and it would be in extreme smallness and insignificance. And if it is not far-fetched for them to affirm that He, exalted, is not divisible while saying He is immense without limit, how did they rule that the statement that He, exalted, is not a body [nor a mode in a body (1)] is repudiated by the self-evidence of reason? It is established that the one who goes to this statement is obstinate: he judges what is self-evidently false as correct, and judges what is not self-evident as self-evident. As for the Hanbalis who affirmed parts and dimensions, they also acknowledge that the essence of God, exalted, is different from the essences of these sensed things. For He, exalted, does not equal these essences in accepting union and separation, in health and sickness, in life and death, hardness and softness, familiarity with others and wildness due to solitude. They acknowledge that these things are impossible of affirmation with respect to God, exalted. For if we say to them: 'If something reached His essence, could it penetrate it or not?' If it could, then His essence would be like water and air, and that essence would be torn and scattered; and if it could not, then His essence would be like solid stone. Also: a living being that does not eat nor drink, whose heart is not pleased by familiarity with others, and whose soul is not desolate due to solitude, is inconceivable. If it is said to the Hanbalis: 'These words [are problematic],' they say: 'He, exalted, is unlike His creation, so it is not permissible to compare His state to the state of others.' And if this is established, we say: they have acknowledged in this context that the ruling of illusion and imagination is not accepted with respect to God, exalted. And if the matter is so, how can they rule that the ruling of illusion and imagination — that every existent is either a body (muttahayyiz) or a mode (hall) in a body — must be accepted?
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphists who claim it is self-evident that God is either a body or a mode. The author first argues that the object of sense perception, if extended, is divisible, and if not extended, it is an insignificant point. Therefore, the claim that it is self-evident that God is not a body or mode is not rationally compelling. He then turns to the Hanbalis who accept that God has parts and dimensions but concede that God's essence differs from created bodies in various ways. They admit that sensory imagination (wahm) does not apply to God. The author exposes their inconsistency: they reject imagination when it contradicts their views but accept it when arguing that every existent must be a body or mode. This demonstrates their arbitrary application of rational criteria.

Key Terms
متحيز (muttahayyiz)
وهم (wahm)
بديهة (badiha)
تعنت (ta'annut)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Razi is rejecting reason entirely in theology.
Correction: Al-Razi is not rejecting reason; he is critiquing the selective and inconsistent use of sensory imagination as a rational criterion for divine attributes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what are the two possibilities for a sensed object?
A) It is either a body or a mode.
B) It is either extended (hence divisible) or non-extended (hence an indivisible point).
C) It is either material or immaterial.
D) It is either created or uncreated.

Key Takeaway
The claim that denying God's corporeality is self-evident is not rationally justified; it relies on sensory imagination which leads to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your limited imagination can capture the reality of God? In what specific ways might you be projecting human constraints onto the Divine, and what will you do this week to surrender those assumptions to revelation?
?
When you encounter a theological claim that feels 'obviously true' to you, do you check if it is actually from scripture or just your own intuition? How can you protect yourself from being 'obstinate' like the opponents Al-Razi critiques?
?
Al-Razi exposes inconsistency in his opponents' reasoning: they affirm indivisibility yet claim denying body is self-evidently false. Where in your own beliefs about God might you hold contradictory positions? Are you willing to let go of comfortable but inconsistent ideas, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Hanbali Inconsistency on Sensory Imagination
Al-Razi exposes the Hanbalis' inconsistent use of imagination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi exposes the inconsistency of the Hanbalis who affirm divine transcendence yet rely on sensory imagination to argue that every existent is a body or mode.

Core Idea
Those who admit imagination does not apply to God cannot then use it as a universal test.

Al-Razi now turns to the Hanbalis, who accept that God has parts but also insist that God's essence is unlike created things. He demonstrates that they themselves reject sensory imagination when it conflicts with their theology, yet they invoke it to claim that every existent must be a body or mode. This inconsistency undermines their entire position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Examine the Hanbali position
The Hanbalis affirm that God has parts but also acknowledge that God's essence is unlike created bodies. Identify their concessions.
3
Analyze the hypothetical examples
Al-Razi presents examples about penetration and living beings. How do these examples challenge the Hanbali view?
4
Observe the Hanbali response
The Hanbalis respond by saying God is unlike creation, so comparisons are invalid. Note their rejection of sensory imagination in this context.
5
Identify the inconsistency
Al-Razi points out that if imagination is rejected for God, how can they use it to assert that every existent is a body or mode?
This is the key critique.

فإنه ليس بصغير ولا بحقير. ومعلوم أن هذا الذي ذكروه مما لا يقبله الحس والخيال البتة ، بل لا يقبله العقل البتة . لأن المشار إليه بحسب الحس . إن حصل له امتداد في الجهات والأحياز كان أحد جانبيه مغايراً للثاني ، وذلك يوجب الانقسام، وإن لم يحصل له امتداد في شيء من الجهات لا في اليمين ولا في اليسار ولا في الفوق ولا في التحت ولا في القدام ولا في الخلف ، كان نقطة غير منقسمة ، وكان في غاية الصغر والحقارة . وإذا لم يبعد عندهم التزام كونه تعالى غير قابل للقسمة مع القول بكونه عظيها غير متناه فكيف حكموا بأن القول بكونه تعالى ليس بمتحيز [ ولا حـال في المتحيز (۱) ] مدفوع في بديهة العقل . فيثبت : أن الذاهب إلى هذا القول : متعنت . يحكم على ما كان بديهي البطلان بالصحة ، ويحكم على غير البديهي بكونه بديهيا . وأما الحنابلة الذين التزموا الأجزاء والأبعاض . فهم أيضا معترفون بأن ذات الله تعالى مخالفة لذوات هذه المحسوسات . فإنه تعالى لا يساوي هذه الذوات في قبول الاجتماع والافتراق ، وفي الصحة والمرض وفي الحياة والموت والصلابة واللين والاستئناس بالغير، والتوحش بسبب الوحدة . فهم معترفون بأن هذه الأشياء ممتنعة الثبوت في حق الله تعالى. فإنا إذا قلنا لهم : لو وصل شيء إلى ذاته. فهل يمكنه أن يغوص فيه أولا يمكنه ؟ فإن أمكنه كانت ذاته بمنزلة الماء والهواء. فتتمزق وتتفرق تلك الذات وإن تعذر عليه ذلك كانت ذاته بمنزلة الحجر الصلد . وأيضا : الحي الذي لا يأكل ولا يشرب ولا يطيب قلبه بسبب الاستئناس بالغير ، ولا تستوحش نفسه بسبب التفرد : غير معقول . فإن قيل للحنابلة : هذه الكلمات . قالوا : إنه تعالى يخالف خلقه . فلا يجوز أن يقاس حاله بحال غيره . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إنهم قد اعترفوا في هذا المقام : بأن حكم الوهم والخيال غير مقبول في حق الله تعالى . وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فكيف يمكنهم أن يحكموا بأن حكم الوهم والخيال في أن كل موجود إما أن يكون متحيزاً ، وإما أن يكون حالا في المتحيز : يجب أن يكون مقبولا ؟
For it is neither small nor insignificant. And it is known that what they mentioned is not accepted by sense and imagination at all, nor is it accepted by reason at all. Because the thing pointed to according to sense: if it has extension in directions and spaces, one of its sides is different from the second, and that necessitates division; and if it has no extension in any direction — neither right nor left, nor above nor below, nor front nor back — it would be an indivisible point, and it would be in extreme smallness and insignificance. And if it is not far-fetched for them to affirm that He, exalted, is not divisible while saying He is immense without limit, how did they rule that the statement that He, exalted, is not a body [nor a mode in a body (1)] is repudiated by the self-evidence of reason? It is established that the one who goes to this statement is obstinate: he judges what is self-evidently false as correct, and judges what is not self-evident as self-evident. As for the Hanbalis who affirmed parts and dimensions, they also acknowledge that the essence of God, exalted, is different from the essences of these sensed things. For He, exalted, does not equal these essences in accepting union and separation, in health and sickness, in life and death, hardness and softness, familiarity with others and wildness due to solitude. They acknowledge that these things are impossible of affirmation with respect to God, exalted. For if we say to them: 'If something reached His essence, could it penetrate it or not?' If it could, then His essence would be like water and air, and that essence would be torn and scattered; and if it could not, then His essence would be like solid stone. Also: a living being that does not eat nor drink, whose heart is not pleased by familiarity with others, and whose soul is not desolate due to solitude, is inconceivable. If it is said to the Hanbalis: 'These words [are problematic],' they say: 'He, exalted, is unlike His creation, so it is not permissible to compare His state to the state of others.' And if this is established, we say: they have acknowledged in this context that the ruling of illusion and imagination is not accepted with respect to God, exalted. And if the matter is so, how can they rule that the ruling of illusion and imagination — that every existent is either a body (muttahayyiz) or a mode (hall) in a body — must be accepted?
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphists who claim it is self-evident that God is either a body or a mode. The author first argues that the object of sense perception, if extended, is divisible, and if not extended, it is an insignificant point. Therefore, the claim that it is self-evident that God is not a body or mode is not rationally compelling. He then turns to the Hanbalis who accept that God has parts and dimensions but concede that God's essence differs from created bodies in various ways. They admit that sensory imagination (wahm) does not apply to God. The author exposes their inconsistency: they reject imagination when it contradicts their views but accept it when arguing that every existent must be a body or mode. This demonstrates their arbitrary application of rational criteria.

Key Terms
متحيز (muttahayyiz)
حال (hall)
وهم (wahm)
خيال (khayal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Hanbalis are simple anthropomorphists who affirm God has a body like humans.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the Hanbalis actually affirm God's essence is unlike creation, but they are inconsistent in applying this principle.

Checkpoint
What inconsistency does al-Razi point out in the Hanbali position?
A) They claim God is not a body but then describe Him with human-like features.
B) They accept the ruling of imagination when it supports their claim that every existent must be a body or mode, but reject it when it contradicts their affirmation of God's unique essence.
C) They deny the existence of accidents (a'rad) but affirm bodies.
D) They use rational proofs but reject revelation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanbalis' own admission that God is unlike creation and that imagination does not apply to Him undermines their argument that every existent must be a body or mode.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you insisted on a principle for others that you made an exception for yourself? How can you ensure your beliefs about God are logically consistent, even when it is uncomfortable?
?
The Hanbalis claimed God is unlike creation yet used an imaginative rule to describe Him. Is there any area of your faith where you accept a claim without examining its consistency with your deeper convictions? Will you examine it today?
?
Al-Razi's critique demands intellectual honesty. What belief do you hold that you have never fully scrutinized for coherence? What would change if you allowed yourself to see its inconsistencies?
