The Logical Dilemma of Imagination in Theology
Al-Razi summarizes the ruling on accepting or rejecting imagination in divine matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's logical argument that imagination cannot be selectively applied in theology.

Core Idea
Imagination in divine matters cannot be partial — it must be all or nothing.

Al-Razi presents a concise logical dilemma to show why theologians cannot pick and choose when to rely on imagination. The argument is simple but decisive: if imagination were a valid tool for knowing God, it would have to be valid in every case; since it leads to false conclusions when applied everywhere, it cannot be trusted at all. This passage sets up the proper methodology for reasoning about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi uses a very condensed style here, packing a complete argument into one sentence.
2
Identify the three options
The passage presents three possible judgments on imagination regarding God: absolutely acceptable, absolutely unacceptable, and selectively acceptable. Find each one in the text.
The third option is rejected as false.
3
Understand the logical consequence
Why does Al-Razi say that absolute acceptance is false by consensus? And why is selective acceptance logically inconsistent? Reflect on how this argument eliminates any middle ground.
This connects to the core idea that imagination cannot be partial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الكلام : أن حكم الوهم والخيال في حق الله تعالى . إن كان مقبولا وجب أن يقبل على الإطلاق. وذلك باطل بالاتفاق . وإن لم يكن مقبولا أن لا يلتفت إليه البتة . فأما قبوله في بعض المواضع ورده في سائرها فهو حكم باطل .
The conclusion of the discourse: that the judgment of imagination (wahm) and fantasy (khayal) regarding God the Exalted: if it is acceptable, it must be accepted absolutely, and that is false by consensus. And if it is not acceptable, then one should not pay attention to it at all. As for accepting it in some places and rejecting it in others, it is a false judgment.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the use of imagination (wahm) and fantasy (khayal) in theology. The author states a logical dilemma: either the evidence of imagination is entirely valid in matters of God, or it is entirely invalid. If it is valid, then it must be accepted universally, which is impossible by consensus because it would lead to anthropomorphism. If it is not valid, then it should be completely disregarded. To accept it in some cases and reject it in others is inconsistent and therefore a false judgment. This sets the stage for the arguments that follow, which demonstrate that imagination fails in divine matters.

Key Terms
وهم
خيال
حكم باطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi is rejecting all use of imagination in theology.
Correction: Al-Razi is not rejecting all use of imagination, but ruling out its selective application. He argues that if imagination is to be used at all, it must be used absolutely; since absolute use leads to false conclusions, imagination must be rejected entirely.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if imagination were acceptable in matters of God?
A) It must be accepted absolutely.
B) It must be rejected entirely.
C) It can be accepted in some places and rejected in others.
D) It is always false.

Key Takeaway
For theology, imagination must either be accepted universally or rejected entirely; selective acceptance is logically false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever formed a mental image of God that made you feel comfortable, even though you knew it was inaccurate? What steps will you take the next time such an image arises to remind yourself of Allah's incomparability?
?
When you read descriptions of God in the Qur'an, do you sometimes imagine them in human terms? How can you train yourself to stop that tendency immediately, recognizing it as an invalid partial acceptance of imagination?
?
In your daily life, do you find yourself applying logic selectively—accepting it when it supports your desires and rejecting it when it challenges you? How does al-Razi's insistence on consistency in theology expose a similar inconsistency in your own heart?

Three Proofs Against Wahm and Khayal Regarding Divine Attributes
Three proofs that wahm and khayal cannot grasp God's attributes, with a fortiori conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three specific arguments demonstrating that human imaginative faculties (wahm and khayal) cannot fully grasp Allah's attributes and actions, and why this applies even more strongly to His essence.

Core Idea
Human imagination cannot grasp the infinite; divine attributes surpass all mental pictures.

This passage presents three distinct proofs that our imaginative faculties (wahm and khayal) are inherently limited when it comes to understanding Allah's attributes. By reflecting on human cognitive limitations, we realize that Allah's knowledge, power, and perception are beyond what we can picture. This lesson will walk through each proof and its powerful conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the three enumerated proofs.
The passage uses clear numbering (الأول, الثاني, الثالث).
2
Identify the three proofs
For each proof, note how it contrasts human experience with divine attributes. Proof 1: Human knowledge cannot hold infinite details simultaneously; God knows all. Proof 2: Human actions require tools and cause fatigue; God's actions do not. Proof 3: Humans cannot see/hear all things at once; God does.
Each proof shows that wahm/khayal would deny these attributes.
3
Understand the conclusion
After the proofs, the author concludes that wahm and khayal are incapable of knowing God's actions and attributes. Then he adds an a fortiori argument: since we must exclude imagination for attributes, we should do so even more for the essence, which is even more obscure.
The argument is: if imagination fails in the easier, it fails more in the harder.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this matters: it guards against anthropomorphism and reminds us that reason and revelation must guide theological knowledge, not mere imagination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنا لا نعقل ذاتا يكون عالما بمعلومات لا نهاية لها على التفصيل دفعة واحدة. فإنا إذا جربنا أنفسنا وجدنا أنها متى اشتغلت باستحضار معلوم معين ، امتنع عليها في تلك الحالة استحضار معلوم آخر ، ثم إنا مع ذلك نعتقد بأنه تعالى عالم بجميع المعلومات على التفصيل من غير أن يحصل في ذلك العلم اشتباه والتباس ، فكان كونه تعالى عالما بجميع المعلومات أمرا على خلاف مقتضى الوهم والخيال . الثاني : إن كل من فعل فعلا فلا بد له من آلة أو أداة . وأن الأفعال الشاقة تكون سببا للكلال واللغوب والتعب . ثم إنا نعتقد أنه تعالى يدير من العرش إلى تحت الثري مع أنه منزه المشقة والتعب . والثالث : إنا نعتقد أنه تعالى يسمع أصوات الخلق من العرش إلى ما تحت الثري ، ويرى الصغير والكبير فوق أطباق السماوات العلى وتحت الأرض السفلى . ومعلوم أن الوهم والخيال لا يتصوران هذه الأحوال . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الوهم والخيال قاصران عن معرفة أفعال الله تعالى وصفاته . ومع ذلك فإنا نثبت أن أفعال الله تعالى وصفاته على مخالفة الوهم والخيال . ومن المعلوم بالبديهة : أن معرفة كنه الذات أعلى وأجل وأغمض من معرفة الأفعال والصفات . قلما عزلنا الوهم والخيال في المقام الأقرب الأظهر ، فلأن نعزلهما في المقام الأغمض الأصعب كان أولى
First: We do not conceive of an essence that knows infinite details simultaneously. For when we examine ourselves, we find that whenever we engage in recalling a specific piece of knowledge, it becomes impossible for us in that state to recall another piece of knowledge. Yet we believe that He, exalted, knows all things in detail without any confusion or ambiguity. Thus His being omniscient is contrary to what wahm (imagination) and khayal (fantasy) assume. Second: Every agent of an action must have a tool or instrument, and strenuous actions cause fatigue and weariness. Yet we believe that He, exalted, administers from the Throne to beneath the earth while being free from fatigue and weariness. Third: We believe that He, exalted, hears the voices of creatures from the Throne to beneath the earth, and sees the small and the large above the layers of the high heavens and beneath the lowest earth. And it is known that wahm and khayal cannot conceive these states. So it is established from what we have mentioned that wahm and khayal are incapable of knowing God's actions and attributes. Moreover, we establish that God's actions and attributes are contrary to what wahm and khayal assume. And it is known by intuition that knowledge of the essence (kunh al-dhat) is higher, more sublime, and more obscure than knowledge of actions and attributes. Since we have excluded wahm and khayal in the nearer and more apparent realm, it is all the more appropriate to exclude them in the more obscure and difficult realm.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct proofs demonstrating the inadequacy of wahm (imagination) and khayal (fantasy) in comprehending God's attributes and actions, followed by a conclusion extending this limitation to knowledge of the divine essence. The first proof argues from the nature of knowledge: human minds cannot simultaneously hold infinite details, yet God knows all things in detail without confusion. This contradicts what wahm would expect. The second proof points out that in human experience, strenuous actions require tools and cause fatigue, yet God governs all without any hardship. The third proof notes that God hears and sees all things simultaneously, which wahm and khayal cannot picture. From these three examples, the author concludes that wahm and khayal are fundamentally incapable of grasping God's actions and attributes. Moreover, since knowledge of the essence is even more obscure than knowledge of actions and attributes, it is even more necessary to exclude wahm and khayal in that realm. The argument is structured as an a fortiori argument: if we set aside imagination in the easier matters, we should do so even more in the harder matters.

Key Terms
الوهم (wahm)
الخيال (khayal)
الذات (al-dhat)
الصفات (attributes)
الأفعال (actions)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human imagination (wahm/khayal) can fully grasp divine attributes and actions.
Correction: The passage shows that our imaginative faculties are inherently limited; they cannot conceive of omniscience, power without fatigue, or all-encompassing perception.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion the author draws from the three examples in this passage?
A) God's actions and attributes are impossible to know.
B) Wahm and khayal are capable of conceiving divine matters.
C) Wahm and khayal are insufficient for knowing God's actions and attributes, and even more so for the essence.
D) Only the third example proves the point.

Key Takeaway
Wahm and khayal are insufficient for knowing God's actions and attributes, and even more so for the essence — we must rely on revelation and reason guided by scripture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you try to picture God's knowledge or power, do you find yourself relying on imagination? How does this passage challenge that habit, and what will you do to train yourself to think of God beyond images?
?
The passage says that knowledge of God's essence is even more obscure than of His attributes. Does this humble you, or do you still assume you can fully 'understand' God? What concrete step can you take to embrace this mystery without frustration?
?
If wahm and khayal are excluded from even the 'easier' matters of God's actions and attributes, how should this affect the way you speak about God in daily conversations? Name one phrase or assumption you will consciously avoid from now on.

The Soul's Ignorance and the Unreliability of Wahm
The soul's inability to know itself despite being apparent shows wahm and khayal are unreliable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the soul's inability to know itself and the phenomenon of accessing the unseen in sleep demonstrate the unreliability of wahm and khayal.

Core Idea
The soul's self-ignorance proves it cannot know the Creator, and sleep shows wahm and khayal are unreliable.

This lesson explores the seventh proof, which uses the soul's own limitations to challenge the reliability of wahm and khayal. The author presents two interconnected arguments: first, since the soul cannot fully know itself, it certainly cannot know the utterly different Creator; second, the fact that knowledge of the unseen comes in sleep, not wakefulness, contradicts what wahm and khayal would predict. Understanding this proof helps clarify the role of rational faculties in theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that the proof has two parts: one about the soul itself, one about sleep.
2
Understand the first argument: soul's self-ignorance
The author argues that since the soul, which is the most apparent thing, cannot fully know itself, it is even more incapable of knowing the Creator, who is utterly different from all creation.
This is an a fortiori (all the more) argument.
3
Understand the second argument: sleep and the unseen
The author observes that when awake, a person cannot access the unseen world, but in sleep, when the intellect is absent, he can connect with the unseen. This is contrary to the expectation of wahm and khayal.
Wakefulness is a state of perfection, yet fails; sleep is deficient yet succeeds.
4
Connect to the conclusion about wahm and khayal
The contradiction shows that the judgments of wahm and khayal can be false and rejected, so they are not reliable for theological knowledge.
This proof weakens reliance on imagination and estimation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إن العقول والأفهام اضطربت في معرفة جوهر النفس أنا بينا في أول هذا الكتاب : أنها أظهر المعلومات وأقربها إلى الإنسانية ، مع العقل والعلم والفهم . ولما عجزت النفس عن معرفة نفسها فلأن تعجز عن معرفة خالق الكل - مع أنه لا نزاع في كونه مخالفا لجميع المخلوقات بالحقيقة والماهية - كان أولى . بل نقول : الإنسان حال ما يكون يقظان فإنه لا يمكنه الوقوف على شيء من أحوال عالم الغيب . فإذا نام وزال عقله واستولت الغفلة على جسده قدر على الاتصال بعالم الغيب والاستفادة من تلك الأسرار . وذلك على خلاف حكم الوهم والخيال لأن الإنسان حال اليقظة أكمل منه حال النوم فلما عجز عن معرفة الغيب حال كماله فلان يعجز عنها حال كماله في الغفلة والنقصان كان ذلك أولى. فعلمنا أن حكم الوهم والخيال قد يكون باطلا مردوداً .
The seventh proof: Intellects and understandings have been perplexed regarding knowledge of the substance of the soul (jawhar al-nafs). We have shown at the beginning of this book that it is the most apparent of known things and the closest to humanity, along with intellect, knowledge, and understanding. Since the soul is incapable of knowing itself, it is all the more incapable of knowing the Creator of all – without dispute that He is different from all creatures in reality and quiddity. Rather, we say: When a person is awake, he cannot attain anything of the states of the unseen world (ghayb). But when he sleeps, his intellect departs and heedlessness overcomes his body, he is able to connect with the unseen world and benefit from those secrets. This is contrary to the ruling of wahm and khayal, because the person in wakefulness is more perfect than in sleep. Thus, having been incapable of knowing the unseen in his state of perfection, his being incapable in his state of heedlessness and deficiency is even more appropriate. So we learn that the judgment of wahm and khayal may be false and rejected.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof, which argues from the soul's own limitations to establish the unreliability of wahm and khayal. The author begins by noting that even the soul, which is the most apparent and closest thing to a person, is not fully known by the intellect. Since the soul cannot know itself, it certainly cannot know the Creator, who is utterly different from all creatures. This is an a fortiori argument. Then the author adds another observation: when awake, a person cannot access the unseen world; but when asleep and the intellect is removed, he can. This is contrary to what wahm and khayal would expect, because wakefulness is a state of greater perfection. The fact that knowledge of the unseen comes in sleep shows that the normal judgment of wahm and khayal (that wakefulness is better for knowing) is false. Therefore, we learn that the rulings of wahm and khayal can be invalid.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs)
جوهر النفس (jawhar al-nafs)
عالم الغيب (world of the unseen)
الوهم والخيال (wahm and khayal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that wakefulness is always superior for knowledge; the sleep example shows the opposite in matters of the unseen.
Correction: The example of sleep demonstrates that access to the unseen occurs in a deficient state, so wahm and khayal's assumption that wakefulness is better for all knowledge is false.

Checkpoint
What does the author use as evidence that wahm and khayal can be false?
A) The soul is easy to know.
B) People know the Creator easily.
C) In sleep, a person can access the unseen world, contrary to what wahm expects.
D) Wahm always agrees with reason.

Key Takeaway
The soul's self-ignorance and the sleep phenomenon reveal that wahm and khayal can be false, undermining their authority in theological matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider how little you truly know your own soul – the closest thing to you – does that humble your confidence in your own reasoning about God? What specific belief about God will you now hold with more humility?
?
Think of a recent time you trusted your imagination (khayal) or gut feeling (wahm) about something unseen. How might that have led you astray? What one practice can you adopt to test such judgments against revelation?
?
Your soul is the most apparent thing to you, yet you remain ignorant of its essence. How does that change your approach to seeking knowledge of God? Write a concrete commitment to rely more on divine guidance than your own mental images.

Eighth Proof: Sight and Imagination Cannot Perceive Themselves
Argument that visual and imaginative powers' self-ignorance proves not all is sensible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fact that the visual and imaginative powers cannot perceive themselves demonstrates that not all existents are sensible or imaginable.

Core Idea
Not everything that exists can be sensed or imagined.

This lesson presents a simple yet profound argument from al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author points out that while we see objects and imagine scenarios, the very powers of sight and imagination themselves are not perceived. Their existence proves that reality includes things beyond sensation and imagination. This argument refutes those who limit reality to what can be sensed or imagined.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument premise
Read the passage: We see things, but the visual power itself is not seen. Similarly, the imagination imagines things but cannot imagine itself.
Notice the self-referential limitation.
2
Identify the conclusion
The existence of these powers indicates that not everything must be sensible or imaginable. This is the core conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implication
If these faculties themselves are real yet imperceptible, then reality is not limited to what is sensible. This undermines the claim that wahm (estimative) and khayal are ultimate judges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : إنا نبصر الأشياء . إلا أن القوة الباصرة نفسها وكذلك القوة الخيالية تتخيل الأشياء إلا أن هذه القوة لا يمكنها أن تتخيل نفسها ، فوجود القوة الباصرة والقوة الخيالية يدل على أنه لا يجب أن يكون كل شيء محسوساً متخيلا .
The eighth proof: We see things. However, the visual power itself, and likewise the imaginative power, imagines things except that this power cannot imagine itself. So the existence of the visual power and the imaginative power indicates that it is not necessary that everything be sensible or imaginable.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This is a concise argument that uses the nature of perception and imagination to demonstrate that not all existents are subject to sensation or imagination. The author notes that we perceive objects with our sight, but the power of sight itself is not seen. Similarly, the imagination can picture many things, but it cannot picture itself. Since these powers exist and are real, yet they are not perceived or imagined, it follows that existence is not limited to what is sensible or imaginable. This undermines the claim that wahm and khayal are the ultimate judges of reality.

Correction: The powers themselves are real but not sensed or imagined, so reality is broader.

Checkpoint

A) Everything is sensible.
B) Not everything is sensible or imaginable.
C) The imagination can imagine itself.

Key Takeaway
The visual and imaginative powers, though real, are not perceived by themselves, proving that reality extends beyond the sensible and imaginable.

?

The Trilemma of Creation: Inside, Outside, or Neither?
The first argument of those claiming بديهة presents a trilemma: creation in God's essence, outside it, or neither.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first expression used by proponents of the intuitive premise and the flaws they identify in the options.

Core Idea
The trilemma forces a choice between God being mixed with bodies or spatially separate.

This lesson examines the first of three expressions used by those who claim it is self-evident that an existent must be either inside or outside the world. They present a trilemma: creation inside God’s essence, outside it, or neither. By rejecting the inside and neither options, they aim to prove that God must be spatially separate from creation. However, as we will see, the author refutes this claim by challenging the premise itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the trilemma structure and the reasoning for each option.
The passage is the opponents' argument, not the author's own view.
2
Identify the three options and their rejections
The passage lists three options: inside, outside, neither. Understand why the first and third are rejected according to the opponents.
The second option is left as the only viable one, leading to spatial separation.
3
Understand the author's context
Realize that this argument is being presented as part of a larger refutation. The author challenges the premise itself, not just these options.
This trilemma is considered flawed by the author because God transcends spatial categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون بأن هذه المقدمة بديهية. فقد يذكرون في تقريرها عبارات مختلفة
فالعبارة الأولى: قالوا: إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته أولا في ذاته ولا خارج ذاته؟ والأول باطل وإلا لزم أن تكون ذاته مخالطة لهذه الأجسام المستقذرة. والثاني يوجب القول بأنه تعالى مباين للعالم بالحيز والجهة. وأما الثالث وهو أنه تعالى خلق هذا العالم لا في ذاته ولا خارج ذاته فهذا قول لا يقبله العقل البتة فكان باطلا.
As for those who claim that this premise is intuitive, they have mentioned in its formulation various expressions. The first expression: they said: Did God, exalted, create the world in His essence or outside His essence, or neither in His essence nor outside His essence? The first is false, otherwise it would necessitate that His essence is mixed with these filthy bodies. The second necessitates the statement that He, exalted, is separate from the world by space and direction. As for the third, which is that He, exalted, created this world neither in His essence nor outside His essence, this is a statement that the intellect absolutely does not accept, so it is false.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three expressions used by those who claim that the premise (that an existent cannot be neither inside nor outside the world) is intuitive (بديهية). The author introduces the section by stating that these proponents offer various formulations. The first expression poses a trilemma: Did God create the world within His essence, outside His essence, or neither within nor outside? The first option is false because it would imply that God's essence is mixed with filthy physical bodies. The second option, creation outside His essence, is also problematic because it leads to the anthropomorphist view that God is spatially separate from the world. The third option—neither inside nor outside—is claimed to be unacceptable to the intellect, thus forcing the conclusion that only the second option is viable, i.e., that God must be spatially separate. However, the author is presenting this as the opponents' argument; the broader context shows that he rejects the claim of بديهة itself. The argument is meant to show the alleged absurdity of the third option, but the author's overall refutation (in the previous lesson) demonstrates that the intellect can indeed conceive of a being beyond inside/outside.

ذات
مخالطة
جسم
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third option (neither inside nor outside) is rationally impossible.
Correction: The author argues that God transcends spatial categories; the intellect can accept the third option when properly understood.

Checkpoint
According to the first expression, why is the first option (creation in God's essence) rejected?
A) It would make God spatial.
B) It would mean God's essence mixes with filthy bodies.
C) It implies the world is eternal.
D) It is the same as the second option.

Key Takeaway
The first expression of the intuitive premise forces a false choice between God being mixed with bodies or being spatial, ignoring the transcendence of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument assumes that God and the world share a spatial relationship. In what areas of your life do you still treat God as if He is limited by space or time? How might your worship change if you truly believed He is beyond all such categories?

The Dichotomy of Interpenetration or Direction
The second argument claims only two relations between existents are conceivable: interpenetration or separation in a direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some theologians argued that only two relations between two existents are conceivable, and why this leads to a problem for God's relation to the world.

Core Idea
For some, two existents must either interpenetrate or be spatially separate; no third option is conceivable.

In this passage, we encounter a second version of an argument that attempts to limit the possible relations between any two existents. The argument claims that the intellect can only conceive of two possibilities: one existent penetrating the other, or they are separate in one of the six spatial directions. Any third alternative is deemed rationally impossible. This sets up a dilemma for those who affirm God's existence while denying spatial location to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: it presents two conceivable relations and one inconceivable.
The passage is short and dense; focus on the terms used.
2
Identify the two conceivable relations
Find the two relations: (1) one existent penetrating the other like accident in substance, (2) they are separate from each other in one of the six directions like two substances or bodies.
These are presented as the only rational possibilities.
3
Understand why the third is rejected
The third scenario—neither penetrating nor spatially separate—is described as 'what the intellect does not accept'. Ask yourself: why might someone think this is inconceivable?
This sets up a dilemma for God's relation to the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعبارة الثانية: قالوا: الموجودان لا يعقلان إلا أن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر مثل العرض والجوهر، أو مباينا عنه في جهة من الجهات الست مثل الجوهرين والجسمين. فأما القسم الثالث وهو أن لا يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر، ولا مباينا عنه بشيء من الجهات فهذا مما لا يقبله العقل
The second expression: they said: Two existents are not conceivable except that one is penetrating the other, like the accident and the substance, or separate from it in one of the six directions, like two substances and two bodies. As for the third division, which is that one of them is not penetrating the other, nor separate from it by any of the directions, this is what the intellect does not accept.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the second of three expressions used to argue that the premise (that an existent cannot be neither inside nor outside the world) is intuitive. The proponents claim that when we conceive of two existents, only two possibilities are rationally conceivable: either one interpenetrates the other (like an accident inhering in a substance), or they are separate from each other in one of the six spatial directions (like two distinct bodies or substances). Any third possibility—where the two existents are neither interpenetrating nor separate in a direction—is dismissed as rationally unacceptable. The author is here reporting the opponents' argument; the implication is that since God and the world are two existents, they must be either interpenetrating (which is impossible for God) or separate in a direction, leading to anthropomorphism. However, the author's earlier refutation shows that God's relation to the world transcends both categories, as God is not a body subject to spatial relations.

جهات ست
مباين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that 'separate in a direction' is the only alternative to interpenetration, but the author argues for a non-spatial distinction.
Correction: The passage itself only presents two options; however, the author's overall argument shows that a third, non-spatial relation is possible for God and the world.

Checkpoint

A) One is inside the other or both are outside.
B) One penetrates the other, or they are separate in a direction.
C) They are both eternal or both originated.
D) They exist together or one precedes the other.

Key Takeaway
The second expression insists that only two spatial relations are conceivable, but this view is contested by those who affirm a non-spatial relation between God and the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If you are someone who insists on logical clarity, how do you handle the claim that the 'third division' is rationally unacceptable? Does that make you doubt the truth of revelation, or can you trust that God's reality transcends human logic? Have you ever rejected a truth because it did not fit your mental framework?
?
This argument was used by some to force God into a spatial relation. Have you ever unknowingly limited God by your own assumptions about what is logical? What would it cost you to admit that your intellect cannot grasp the nature of God's relation to creation, and to submit to what revelation teaches without demanding complete rational comprehension?

Third Expression: Reason's Judgment on the Absence of Direction
Third expression claiming sound reason declares directionlessness impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument used by proponents of spatial locality for God, and why it is flawed.

Core Idea
If something isn't in any direction, sound reason says it's impossible — but God is not a something like that.

This passage presents the third expression of an argument that attempts to prove God must be located in a direction. The proponents claim that sound reason itself declares that anything not found in any of the six directions is impossible. As we will see, this reasoning fails when applied to the Creator, who transcends spatial categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that it presents a claim about 'sound reason' judging something impossible if it is not in any direction.
The passage is brief; the conclusion is implied.
2
Identify the claim
The claim is that sound reason judges that a thing not present in any of the six directions is impossible. This is an appeal to intuition.
3
Consider the flaw
Think about why this argument does not apply to God. The previous lessons have established that God is the Creator of direction and space, so He cannot be contained by them.
The author's refutation is implied though not in this snippet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعبارة الثالثة: قالوا: إن صريح العقل حاكم بأن الشيء إذا لم يكن حاصلا في هذه الجهة البتة، ولم يكن حاصلا في شيء من الجهات الست
The third expression: they said: Sound reason judges that if a thing is not at all present in that direction, and is not present in any of the six directions...
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the third expression used by proponents of the بديهة claim. They assert that 'sound reason' (صريح العقل) rules that when a thing is not located in any specific direction at all, and indeed not in any of the six directions (up, down, front, back, right, left), then such a thing is impossible. The argument is that existence necessarily implies being somewhere spatially. However, the author's earlier discussion has already refuted this by showing that God, the Creator of direction itself, cannot be confined to any direction. The argument here is a rephrasing of the same idea: the opponents are using the intuitive notion that everything must be in some place to deny the possibility of a being that is neither inside nor outside the world. The author's overall point is that this 'intuition' is false when applied to the transcendent Creator.

Key Takeaway
This argument mistakenly equates existence with spatial location, which does not apply to the Creator of space.

Second Argument: Relations Between Two Existents
Presents the second argument: two existents must either interpenetrate or be separate in one of six directions; a third possibility is absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second expression's argument that two existents must either interpenetrate or be directionally separate, and why a third possibility is deemed impossible.

Core Idea
According to this argument, the only conceivable relations between two existents are permeation or directional separation.

This passage presents the second of several arguments used by opponents to claim that God must occupy space. They assert that any two existents can only relate by one permeating the other or by being separate in a direction. Anything else is inconceivable. We will examine this classification and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear: the two accepted possibilities and the rejected third.
Notice the examples given: accident/substance and two substances/bodies.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage explicitly states two conceivable relations: one existent pervades the other (like an accident in a substance), or they are separate in one of the six directions (like two substances or bodies).
3
Understand the rejected third category
The third possibility—neither pervading nor directionally separate—is declared intellectually unacceptable. Reflect on why the opponents consider this impossible.
This sets up the later refutation by the author.
4
Reflect on the implication for God
If this classification is correct for all existents, then God would have to either be inside the world (pervading) or outside it in a direction. The lesson challenges us to consider whether this applies to a non-physical being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعبارة الثانية : قالوا : الموجودان لا يعقلان إلا أن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر . مثل العرض والجوهر ، أو مباينا عنه في جهة من الجهات الست ، مثل الجوهرين والجسمين . فأما القسم الثالث وهو أن لا يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ، ولا مباينا عنه بشيء من الجهات فهذا مما لا يقبله العقل

As for the third division, that one of them neither pervades the other nor is separate from it in any of the directions, this is something the intellect does not accept.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the opponents' second expression, which is a classification of the possible relationships between two existents. They claim that the only two possibilities are: (1) one existent permeates the other, like an accident inhering in a substance; or (2) they are separate from each other in one of the six spatial directions (up, down, front, back, left, right), like two distinct substances or bodies. They then assert that a third possibility—where neither permeation nor directional separation occurs—is inconceivable to the intellect. This supports their claim that God must be either in the world (pervading) or outside it (separate in a direction), and that a being neither inside nor outside is impossible.

Third Expression: The Argument from Six Directions
Opponents present third argument: absence from six directions equals non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' third argument that absence from all six directions implies non-existence, and how they apply it to deny God's existence.

Core Idea
Absence from all directions means non-existence for physical objects – but does it apply to God?

The opponents present their third and final expression, claiming that sound reason dictates that anything not located in any of the six spatial directions must be non-existent. They illustrate this with the everyday example of a person neither inside nor outside a house. Then they conclude that if God is not in any direction, He cannot exist. This lesson examines their reasoning and the self-evident premise they rely on.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the opponents' argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the text calls this premise 'self-evident' (بديهية).
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that links absence from all six directions to non-existence. Understand how sound reason (صريح العقل) is invoked.
The claim is: if a thing is not in any direction, it is pure non-existence.
3
Consider the example and its application
Reflect on the example of a person not in the house and not outside. How does the opponents use this to argue about God? Ask yourself if this analogy is valid for a non-physical being.
This step prepares you for the author's refutation in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعبارة الثالثة : قالوا : إن صريح العقل حاكم بأن الشيء إذا لم يكن حاصلا في هذه الجهة البتة ، ولم يكن حاصلا في شيء من الجهات الست المحيطة بنا . كان هذا نفيا لوجوده ، وحكما بكونه عدما محضا وسلبا صرفا . ولذلك فإنا إذا قلنا : إن فلانا لم يوجد في الدار ولم يوجد خارج الدار : كان هذا نفيا لوجوده ، وإذا كان لا طريق إلى تقرير النفي المحض والسلب الصرف إلا هذا ، ثبت أنا لو نفينا كونه تعالى على أحد هذه الوجوه فإنه يلزم منه نفي وجوده . فهذه جملة كلمات الخصوم في تقرير أن هذه المقدمة بديهية
And the third expression: They said: Sound reason judges that if a thing is not present in this direction at all, and is not present in any of the six directions surrounding us, this is a negation of its existence, and a judgment that it is pure non-existence and absolute negation. For this reason, if we say: So-and-so was not found in the house and was not found outside the house, this is a negation of his existence. And since there is no way to establish pure negation and absolute denial except this, it is established that if we negate His, exalted, being in any of these modes, then a negation of His existence necessarily follows. So this is the summary of the opponents' words in establishing that this premise is self-evident.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage presents the third and final expression of the opponents. They argue that reason itself rules that if something is not located in any of the six directions (the spatial framework we experience), then that thing does not exist at all. They illustrate this with the example of a person: if we say 'So-and-so is not in the house and not outside the house,' we are effectively denying his existence. The opponents then apply this to God: if we deny that God is in any of these directions (i.e., neither inside nor outside the world), we are denying His existence. They conclude that this premise—that a being must be either inside or outside—is self-evident. The author then adds a transitional line summarizing the opponents' three expressions, preparing for his refutation in the next lesson.

Key Terms
صريح العقل
الجهات الست
النفي المحض
السلب الصرف
البديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial absence always implies non-existence, even for non-physical beings like God.
Correction: The premise only holds for physical objects that are subject to spatial location. God transcends space, so absence from directions does not negate His existence.

Checkpoint

B) A person who is neither in the house nor outside the house.
C) Two bodies not separated by a direction.
D) A book on theology.

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that if God is not in any spatial direction, He cannot exist, but this premise only applies to physical beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would it cost you to surrender that rational pride?

Refuting Self-Evidence and Exposing Circularity
Refutes two interpretations: self-evidence and proof, exposing circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponents' argument for God's direction fails—either because their claim of self-evidence is baseless or because their proof is circular.

Core Idea
The opponents' case collapses: self-evidence is false; their proof is circular.

In this passage, the author presents a two-pronged refutation of the opponents' argument that God must be in a direction. He first addresses their claim that the impossibility of a being neither inside nor outside the world is self-evident, showing that this is clearly false since the matter is disputed. Then he examines their attempt to provide a proof and demonstrates that it falls into circular reasoning: their division into inside, outside, or neither only makes sense if we already assume God is spatially located, which is exactly what they are trying to prove. This lesson will walk through both criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two interpretations
The author addresses two possible intentions of the opponents: claiming self-evidence or offering a proof. Read the passage to see how he handles each.
The opponents' words could be interpreted in two ways, and both are refuted.
2
Analyze the refutation of self-evidence
Note that the author says he has already given ten arguments against the self-evidence claim. He also argues that if something is disputed among rational thinkers, it cannot be self-evident.
Self-evidence requires universal agreement; here there is disagreement.
3
Expose the circularity in the proof
The author shows that the opponents' threefold division (inside, outside, neither) presupposes that God is localized. Using this division to prove locality is circular.
Circularity arises when the proof depends on the conclusion and vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه إن كان المقصود من هذه الكلمات : ادعاء أن إثبات موجود لا في داخل العالم ولا في خارج العالم قول معلوم الفساد بالضرورة والبديهة . فنحن قد بينا بالوجوه العشرة الكاملة أن ادعاء البديهة ههنا فاسد باطل . وإن كان المقصود منها ذكر الدليل على وجوب كون الله تعالى في الجهة . فنقول : هذا باطل لأن بتقدير أن يكون الحق هو أن الله تعالى غير مختص بالحيز والجهة أصلا . فهل يصح على هذا التقدير أن يقال : إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته ؟ لا أظن أن العاقل يحكم بصحة هذا التقسيم على تقدير سبق الاعتقاد بأنه مختص بالحيز والجهة أصلا . لأن على هذا التقدير يكون الحق هو أنه تعالى خلق العالم لا في ذاته ولا مباينا عن ذاته بالحيز والجهة . فيثبت : أن قوله : إنه تعالى خلق العالم في ذاته أو خارج ذاته، إنما يظهر كونه حقا بتقدير أن يثبت أنه يجب كونه تعالى مختصا بالحيز والجهة. فلو أثبتنا صحة هذه المقدمة بالبناء على تلك المقدمة لزم توقيف الدليل على المدلول وتوقيف المدلول على الدليل ، وأنه يوجب الدور . والدور باطل. فيثبت أنه إن كان مقصودهم من تلك الكلمات مجرد ادعاء البديهة فهو باطل قطعا . لأن الشيء المختلف فيه بين العقلاء قديما وحديثا . كيف يمكن ادعاء البديهة فيه ؟ لا سيما وجمهور العقلاء المحققين مصرون على فساده، وإما إن كان مقصودهم منه ذكر الدليل فهو يوجب الدور ، لأجل أن هذه المقدمة لا يمكن الاعتراف بصحتها إلا بعد إثبات هذا المطلوب ، فلو حاولنا إثبات هذا المطلوب بتلك المقدمة لزم الدور . وهذا كلام
And know that if the intent of these words is the claim that affirming an existent neither inside the world nor outside it is a statement known to be corrupt by necessity and self-evidence—then we have shown by ten complete arguments that the claim of self-evidence here is corrupt and false. And if the intent is to mention proof for the necessity of God, exalted, being in a direction—then we say: This is false because, assuming that the truth is that God, exalted, is not specialized by space and direction at all, then is it valid on this assumption to say: He, exalted, created the world in His essence or outside His essence? I do not think that a rational person would judge the validity of this division on the assumption that it is previously believed that He is specialized by space and direction at all. Because on this assumption, the truth is that He, exalted, created the world neither in His essence nor separate from His essence by space and direction. So it is established that the statement, 'He, exalted, created the world in His essence or outside His essence,' only appears to be true on the assumption that it is established that He, exalted, must be specialized by space and direction. So if we try to establish the correctness of this premise (the threefold division) by building on that premise (that God must be in a direction), then the proof depends on the conclusion and the conclusion depends on the proof, and that necessitates circularity. And circularity is invalid. So it is established that if their intent from those words is merely the claim of self-evidence, it is absolutely false—because how can self-evidence be claimed for something that is disputed among rational thinkers past and present, especially when the majority of verifying rational thinkers insist on its corruption? And if their intent is to present proof, then it necessitates circularity, because this premise cannot be acknowledged as correct except after the establishment of this very conclusion; so if we attempt to prove this conclusion using that premise, circularity would follow. This is a statement.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage is the author's refutation of the opponents' three expressions. He first addresses two possible interpretations of their words: either they are claiming self-evidence (badihah) for the impossibility of a being neither inside nor outside, or they are offering a proof that God must be in a direction. If they claim self-evidence, the author states that he has already shown in ten complete arguments that this claim is false—since the matter is historically and contemporaneously disputed among rational thinkers, and the majority of verifiers reject it. If they are offering a proof, the author exposes a circularity (dawr): the opponents' threefold division (inside, outside, or neither) only makes sense if we already assume that God must be localized in space or direction. But that assumption is precisely what they are trying to prove. Thus they are using the conclusion as a premise and the premise as a conclusion, which is a logical fallacy. The author concludes that in either case, the opponents' position is invalid.

Key Terms
البديهة
الدليل
الدور
المدلول
الجهة
الحيز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the opponents' threefold division is a neutral, unbiased framework for discussing God's relationship to the world.
Correction: The division implicitly assumes that God is spatially located (inside or outside), which is exactly what the opponents are trying to prove. Thus it is not neutral but question-begging.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the opponents' argument fall into circularity (dawr)?
A) Because they use the same terms repeatedly.
B) Because their division only makes sense if God is already assumed to be in space, which is what they try to prove.
C) Because they rely on Aristotle's authority.
D) Because they admit the third option is false.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' case fails both ways: their claim to self-evidence is false because the matter is disputed, and their proof is circular because it presupposes what it aims to prove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that claiming self-evidence for a disputed matter is invalid. When have you been guilty of assuming something is 'obvious' in your faith, only to later realize it was not universally agreed upon? Will you commit to investigating matters of aqeedah with more humility?
?
The opponents' proof was circular—they assumed what they wanted to prove. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that are actually based on unexamined assumptions? Ask Allah to show you where your reasoning may be flawed, and resolve to seek the truth even if it requires dismantling comfortable ideas.
?
The author uses rational arguments to defend Allah's transcendence beyond space and direction. How committed are you to letting both reason and revelation guide your understanding of Allah, even when it challenges deep-seated mental habits? Pray for sincerity in seeking the truth.

Refutation of Ibn Sina's Claim on Certainty from Wahm
Rebuttal of Ibn Sina's view on certainty via dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that refutes Ibn Sina's claim that the estimative faculty (wahm) can produce certainty in abstract premises.

Core Idea
Certainty from wahm leads to circularity or collapse.

Ibn Sina conceded that certainty is obtained in a certain premise but attributed it to the estimative faculty (wahm) rather than the intellect. This passage presents a powerful dilemma that exposes the inconsistency of that view. By examining the two possible positions regarding the strength of certainty, the author shows that Ibn Sina's position either leads to circularity or undermines itself.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ibn Sina's Claim
Read the passage to see that Ibn Sina admits certainty in the premise but says it comes from wahm, not intellect, and that wahm is unreliable for non-sensibles.
The key terms are الوهم (wahm) and الجزم (certainty).
2
Identify the Dilemma
Notice the dilemma: either the certainty from wahm equals that of self-evident truths (like 'one is half of two') or it does not. Both options lead to problems.
The dilemma structure is a powerful argumentative tool.
3
Examine the First Horn
If equal, then since one is true and the other false, the difference must be known either necessarily or theoretically. If necessary, it prevents strong certainty in both; if theoretical, it leads to circularity (الدور).
Circularity is a logical fallacy that invalidates the argument.
4
Examine the Second Horn
If the certainty is not equal, then the premise is not initially certain, so it loses all epistemic support and collapses entirely.
This horn shows that Ibn Sina's position self-destructs.
5
Synthesize the Refutation
Understand that both horns lead to unacceptable conclusions, so Ibn Sina's claim is refuted. The certainty cannot come from wahm.
This passage concludes the discussion on this topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واف بالكشف عن حقيقة الحال في هذه المباحث . أما الشيخ الرئيس أبو علي ابن سينا فإنه سلم أن الجزم بهذه المقدمة حاصل . إلا أنه زعم أن الجازم بها هو الوهم لا العقل . وزعم أن حكم الوهم في غير المحسوسات غير موثوق به. ولقائل أن يقول : إما أن تقولوا : إن الجزم الحاصل [ في هذه المقدمة يساوي الجزم الحاصل ] في سائر البديهيات. كقولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين ، وأن النفي والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان . وإما أن تنكروا هذه المساواة . فإن ذهبتم إلى القسم الأول ، وهو : إذا كانت قوة الجزم في الصورتين بالتساوي . فالعلم يكون إحداهما حقة والأخرى باطلة ، إما أن يكون علما ضروريا أو نظريا. فإن كان العلم الضروري بالفرق بينها حاصلا ، كان ذلك مانعا من حصول الجزم القوي لكل واحد منهما ، وأن كان الفرق لا يعرف إلا بالنظر والدليل ، فحينئذ تصير صحة البديهيات موقوفة [ على الدليل ، إلا أن صحة الدليل موقوفة ] على البديهيات فيلزم الدور وهو باطل . وإما إن ذهبتم إلى القسم الثاني وهو أن الجزم بهذه القضية ليس في القوة والشدة [ مثل جزم العقل بالبديهيات ] فحينئذ تخرج هذه القضية عن أن يكون مجزوما بها ابتداء . وذلك يوجب سقوطها بالكلية [ فهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب ]
And it is sufficient to reveal the true state of affairs in these investigations. As for the Chief Master Abu Ali Ibn Sina, he conceded that certainty in this premise is obtained. However, he claimed that what is certain about it is the estimation (al-wahm), not the intellect (al-ʿaql). And he claimed that the judgment of estimation regarding non-sensible things is not reliable. But one may say: Either you say that the certainty obtained in this premise equals the certainty obtained in other self-evident truths (al-badīhiyyāt), like our saying 'one is half of two' and that negation and affirmation cannot both be true nor both false, or you deny this equality. If you adopt the first position, that the strength of certainty in the two cases is equal, then since one of them is true and the other false, that knowledge is either necessary (ḍarūrī) or theoretical (naẓarī). If the necessary knowledge of the difference between them is obtained, that would prevent strong certainty in each of them; and if the difference is known only through reflection and proof, then the validity of self-evident truths would depend on proof, and yet the validity of proof depends on self-evident truths, resulting in circularity (al-dawr), which is invalid. If you adopt the second position, that certainty in this proposition is not in strength and intensity like the intellect's certainty in self-evident truths, then this proposition would cease to be initially certain, and that would cause it to fall entirely. This concludes the discussion in this chapter.
Source pages p. 21

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of Ibn Sina's claim that the certainty in a particular premise is produced by the estimative faculty (wahm) rather than the intellect. The author presents a dilemma to refute this: either the certainty in that premise equals the certainty of self-evident truths (like 'one is half of two' and the law of non-contradiction), or it does not. If it does equal them, then since some self-evident truths are true and the premise is false (as argued), the knowledge of the difference between them must be either necessary or theoretical. If it is necessary, it would prevent having strong certainty in both; if it is theoretical, then the validity of self-evident truths would depend on proof and vice versa, leading to circularity, which is absurd. If the certainty is not equal, then the premise is not initially certain and thus loses all epistemic support. The passage concludes by marking the end of the discussion on this topic.

Key Terms
الوهم
الجزم
البديهيات
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the estimative faculty (wahm) can provide reliable certainty in abstract matters because it deals with meanings.
Correction: This refutation shows that if wahm gives certainty equal to self-evident truths, it leads to circularity; if weaker, the premise loses all support. Thus wahm cannot be a source of certainty for abstract propositions.

Checkpoint
According to the rebuttal, what is the consequence if the certainty in the premise equals that of self-evident truths?
A) The premise becomes false.
B) Circularity would result because the validity of self-evident truths would depend on that premise.
C) The premise would be accepted as self-evident.
D) The certainty would be stronger.

Key Takeaway
The certainty claimed by Ibn Sina cannot come from wahm without leading to circularity or self-destruction, thus the refutation stands.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may rely on your instinct (wahm) in matters of faith. After reading this refutation, where in your own life have you accepted certainty without rational scrutiny? What will you do about it?
?
The argument uses a dilemma to show that Ibn Sina's position collapses. Reflect on a time you held a belief that seemed certain but was actually based on unreliable grounds. How can you ensure your certainties are founded on intellect and proof?
?
Circularity is a fatal flaw in reasoning. In your daily judgments, do you ever find yourself arguing in circles? Commit to one area where you will break that cycle.

The First Proof: Uniqueness Does Not Negate Existence
Sanusi's first proof: uniqueness does not negate existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the primordial intellect to argue that uniqueness does not imply non-existence.

Core Idea
Uniqueness is not a proof of non-existence.

Sanusi begins his first proof by appealing to the most basic faculty of reason—the primordial intellect, untouched by learning. He asks a deceptively simple question: Is it rationally impossible to conceive of a being so unique that everything else differs from it? If not, then the lack of a counterpart cannot be used to deny existence. This passage lays the foundation for the entire argument for God's oneness and transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the author's own wording sink in.
The passage is short but dense. Read it twice.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on بديمة العقل (primordial intellect), بديهة (intuitive), and نظير (counterpart). Understand their roles.
These terms are central to the proof.
3
Understand the logical move
Note how Sanusi moves from the intuitive possibility of a unique existent to the conclusion that lacking a counterpart does not prove non-existence.
This is a simple but powerful argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الأولى : إن بديمة العقل لا تستبعد وجود موجود موصوف بصفات مخصوصة ، بحيث يكون كل ما سواه مخالفا له في تلك الخصوصية . وإذا لم يكن هذا ممتنعاً في البديهة ، علمنا : أنه لا يلزم من عدم نظير الشيء ، عدم ذلك الشيء
The First Proof: The primordial intellect does not consider impossible the existence of an existent described with specific attributes, such that everything other than it is different from it in that specificity. And if this is not impossible in the intuitive (badīha), we know that the non-existence of a counterpart of a thing does not necessitate the non-existence of that thing.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs. It is an argument from the intuitive judgment of the intellect. Sanusi states that the basic, untaught intellect does not find it impossible to conceive of an existent that has unique attributes, with everything else being different from it in those attributes. Since this concept is not inherently impossible according to intuitive reason, we conclude that the absence of a counterpart or similar being does not imply that the thing itself does not exist. In other words, the lack of a peer does not negate the existence of the entity.

Key Terms
بديمة العقل
بديهة
نظير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is unique and without peer, its existence is doubtful.
Correction: Sanusi argues that uniqueness is not a proof of non-existence; the intuitive intellect accepts the possibility of a unique existent.

Checkpoint

A) The existence of a thing with a counterpart
B) The existence of a thing with unique attributes
C) The non-existence of a thing without a counterpart
D) The impossibility of a thing without a peer

Key Takeaway
The primordial intellect shows that uniqueness does not imply non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Sanusi's proof speak directly to that doubt in your heart? What would it take for you to truly internalize that uniqueness is not a proof of non-existence?
?
This proof appeals to your 'primordial intellect'—the reason Allah gave you before any worldly influence. Are there areas in your life where you have allowed cultural or social comparisons to overshadow that innate conviction in the truth of Allah's existence? What would it mean for you to rely more on that God-given intuition rather than on what 'seems reasonable' by worldly standards?
?
Sanusi argues that the absence of a counterpart does not negate existence. Yet in your daily life, do you sometimes measure the reality of something by its familiarity or prevalence? Is your faith in the unseen strong enough to affirm the existence of a unique Creator even when you cannot see His 'peer'? What is one concrete step you can take today to strengthen that conviction?

The Second Proof: Dependency on a Counterpart Leads to Circularity
Sanusi proves that existence cannot depend on a counterpart via circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence cannot depend on the existence of a counterpart, as shown by the logical argument of circularity.

Core Idea
Dependency on a peer collapses into self-dependency, which is absurd.

Sanusi now presents his second proof, which eliminates the possibility that a thing's existence depends on the existence of a similar counterpart. This argument uses the principle of symmetry between likes to expose a vicious circularity. By the end, we see that lacking a counterpart does not imply non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's own logical rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact logical style.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Sanusi sets up a division: either existence depends on a counterpart or it does not. Find where he states this division.
The phrase 'أما أن يتوقف أو لا يتوقف' marks the division.
3
Follow the refutation of dependency
Trace how Sanusi argues from symmetry between likes to the conclusion of self-dependency, which he calls absurd (محال). Ask yourself why self-dependency is impossible.
Self-dependency means a thing's existence depends on itself, which is circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : وهي أن وجود الشيء أما أن يتوقف على وجود مثله ، أو لا يتوقف. والأول باطل. لأنهما لما كانا مثلين وجب استواؤهما في جميع اللوازم، فلو توقف وجود هذا، على وجود مثله، لزم أن يتوقف وجود الثاني على وجود الأول، فيلزم أن يتوقف وجود كل واحد منهما على وجود نفسه وذلك محال في البديهة .
The Second Proof: The existence of a thing either depends on the existence of its like, or does not depend. The first is false. Because when they are two likes, they must be equal in all concomitants. So if the existence of this one depends on the existence of its like, it would necessitate that the existence of the second depends on the existence of the first, leading to the existence of each one depending on its own existence—and that is absurd in the intuitive (badīha).
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the second proof, a logical argument by division. Sanusi divides the possibility: either a thing's existence depends on the existence of a counterpart like it, or it does not. He then refutes the first option. For if two things are likes, they must be equal in all their necessary properties. If the existence of one depends on the existence of the other, then by symmetry the existence of the second would depend on the existence of the first. This leads to each one's existence depending on itself—a clear circularity that the intuitive intellect judges as impossible. Therefore, existence cannot depend on having a counterpart. Thus, lacking a counterpart does not imply non-existence.

Key Terms
توقف وجود
مثلين
لزم
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a thing's existence could depend on a counterpart (a similar being) without problem.
Correction: Sanusi shows that dependency on a like forces reciprocal dependency, leading to self-dependency which is intuitively absurd. Therefore, such dependency is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof conclude about the possibility of existence depending on a counterpart?
A) It is necessary
B) It is possible but not necessary
C) It is false because it leads to circularity
D) It is true because likes are equal

Key Takeaway
The existence of a thing cannot depend on the existence of a counterpart, because that would force each thing's existence to depend on itself, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What or who do you rely on for your sense of worth or security other than Allah? Sanusi's proof shows that depending on anything that is like you (another creature) leads to an absurd cycle. How does this expose the futility of placing your ultimate trust in anyone or anything other than the Creator? Will you repent of that today and turn to Him alone?
?
This argument uses the intuitive intellect to recognize self-dependency as impossible. Do you ever feel self-sufficient, as if you can depend on your own abilities without Allah? How does this proof remind you that even your own existence is not self-sustained, but entirely dependent on its Origin? Commit to daily reciting 'Hasbunallahu wa ni'mal wakeel' as a declaration of your dependence.
?
Sanusi says the absurdity of each depending on itself is obvious to the badīha. Yet we often live as if we are independent. Have you dulled that innate awareness by heedlessness? What practical step will you take this week to sharpen your consciousness that everything except Allah is dependent and that He alone is Self-Sufficient?

The Third Proof: Individuality Cannot Be Transferred
Sanusi presents a proof by distinguishing essence from individuation and arguing the impossibility of transfer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's third proof that the specific individuation (taʿayyun) of each thing is unique and cannot occur in another, and why this conclusion supports the main thesis that lacking a counterpart does not mean non-existence.

Core Idea
Each thing's identity is so unique it cannot be shared with anything else.

Sanusi now presents his third argument, which focuses on the distinction between a thing's common essence and its unique individuality. He argues that the very individuality of each entity is non-transferable; otherwise, it would be identical to another, which is absurd. From this, he draws an important implication for the main thesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: تعين (individuation) and ماهية (essence). Understand the flow of the argument.
The passage is concise; read it twice to grasp the logical steps.
2
Distinguish essence from individuation
Sanusi first distinguishes between the common essence shared by individuals and the specific individuation that makes each thing itself. This sets up the core premise.
Essence is what is common; individuation is what is unique.
3
Analyze the impossibility argument
He then argues that the individuation of a particular thing cannot occur in another, because if it did, that other would be identical to the first—an intuitive falsehood.
The reasoning is based on the principle of identity: a thing cannot be another thing.
4
Understand the conclusion
From this, Sanusi concludes that the non-existence of a counterpart or equal does not prove the non-existence of the thing itself, because its individuality is unique and non-transferable.
This directly supports the broader discussion about the necessity of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : إن تعين كل شيء من حيث أنه هو هو ، مغاير لأصل الماهية المشترك فيها ، بين تلك الأشخاص . ثم نقول : إن تعين هذا الشيء المعين، ممتنع الحصول في غيره [ وإلا لكان ذلك الشيء نفس غيره ، وذلك باطل في بديهة العقل فيثبت : أن تعين كل شيء من حيث إنه هو ممتنع الحصول في غيره (۱) ] فعلمنا : أن عدم النظير والمساوي لا يدل على عدم الشيء .
The Third Proof: The individuation (taʿayyun) of each thing, inasmuch as it is itself, is distinct from the essence (māhiyya) common among those individuals. Then we say: The individuation of this particular thing is impossible to obtain in another; otherwise that thing would be the same as another, and that is false in the intuitive intellect. So it is established that the individuation of each thing, inasmuch as it is itself, is impossible to obtain in another. Thus we know that the non-existence of a counterpart and equal does not indicate the non-existence of the thing.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is the third proof, which differentiates between the common essence (māhiyya) shared by individuals and the specific individuation (taʿayyun) that makes each thing itself. Sanusi argues that the individuation of each particular thing is impossible to occur in another; if it did, the thing would be identical to another, which is intuitively false. Therefore, the lack of a counterpart or equal does not imply that the thing does not exist, because its individuality is unique and cannot be replicated. This reinforces the main thesis.

Key Terms
تعين (individuation)
ماهية (essence)
نظير (counterpart)
مساوي (equal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a thing has no equal or counterpart, it might not exist.
Correction: Sanusi argues that the uniqueness of individuation means lack of a peer does not imply non-existence.

Checkpoint
What does the third proof identify as impossible to obtain in another entity?
A) The common essence (māhiyya)
B) The specific individuation (taʿayyun)
C) The existence of a counterpart
D) The equality of attributes

Key Takeaway
The uniqueness of each thing's individuation proves that lacking a peer does not make it non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your individual identity (your soul, your deeds) cannot be transferred to anyone else, then you are uniquely responsible before Allah for everything you do. Would you live differently today knowing that no one can bear your burden for you on the Day of Judgment?
?
Allah is unique with no equal or counterpart. If that is true, how does your worship reflect His uniqueness? Do you ever treat anything else as worthy of the same submission you give to Him?
?
Sanusi proves that lacking a peer does not mean non-existence. Your faith in Allah's existence does not depend on finding something like Him. Do you sometimes demand empirical proof or comparable examples before submitting? What would it cost you to accept His uniqueness without analogy?

Author remarks on strength and limitation of second proof.

Core Idea
A strong argument can still imply consequences that opponents deny.

This short remark by the author evaluates the second proof presented earlier. He affirms its strength but immediately notes a problematic implication: it suggests that the celestial sphere can be pierced and reassembled, a notion that philosophers reject. This teaches us that even a robust argument can have implications unacceptable to certain schools of thought.
