Why Greatness Requires Divisibility
Author refutes the claim that God can be great yet indivisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's logical proof that any spatially great being must be divisible, and why this refutes the claim that God can be both indivisible and physically great.

Core Idea
Greatness in space forces divisibility; a being that is one and indivisible cannot be physically great.

This passage tackles a specific objection: if God is one and indivisible, how can He be described as 'great'? The author first shows that analogies from the physical world are insufficient, then provides a decisive logical proof based on the division between affirmation and negation. You'll see how kalam uses rigorous reasoning to defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Read the first part of the passage where the objector argues that 'great' implies composition and divisibility, and the author responds by questioning whether this necessity carries over to the unseen realm.
Notice the author grants the premise for the witnessed world but challenges the analogy.
2
Follow the counter-objection and answer
The author then flips the argument by asking why God cannot be extremely small. Read how the author refutes the claim that this would make Him contemptible.
3
Understand the decisive proof
The author presents a proof based on logical division: if we point to an indivisible point, either something else exists above it or not. Work through both branches to see why a great, indivisible being is impossible.
This proof is not an analogy but a deductive argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that the proof is based on 'division between affirmation and negation.' Consider why this method is considered decisive in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى واحد منزه عن التأليف والتركيب . ومع ذلك فإنه يكون عظيها ؟ قوله : ( العظيم يجب أن يكون مركبا منقسما ، قلنا : سلمنا أن العظيم يجب أن يكون منقسما في الشاهد ، فلم قلتم : إنه يجب أن يكون كذلك في الغائب ؟ فإن قياس الغائب على الشاهد من غير جامع باطل . ثم نقول : لم يجوز أن يكون غير منقسم، ويكون في غاية الصغر ؟ قوله : « إنه على هذا التقدير يكون حقيرا . وذلك على الله محال ، . قلنا : الذي لا يمكن أن يشار إليه البتة ولا يمكن أن نحس به ، يكون كالعدم ، فيكون أشد حقارة . وإذا جاز هذا فلم لا يجوز ما ذكرناه ؟ والجواب عن الأول : أن نقول : إنه إذا كان عظيما في الإشارة الحسية كان منقسما فليس هذا من باب قياس الغائب على الشاهد . بل هذا بناء على البرهان القطعي . وذلك لأنا. إذا أشرنا إلى نقطة لا تنقسم ، فإما أن يحصل فوقها شيء آخر أو لا يحصل ، فإن حصل فوقها شيء آخر ، كان ذلك الفوقاني ، مغايرا للتحتاني. إذ لو جاز أن يقال : ذلك الفوقاني هو عين التحتاني ، فحينئذ يكون قد حصل الشيء الواحد دفعة واحدة في حيزين ، وذلك باطل في البديهة . ولأنه لو جاز ما ذكرتم ، لجاز أن يقال : إن هذا الجبل ليس إلا جوهرا فردا ، وجزءا لا يتجزأ . إلا أنه حصل في أحياز كثيرة ، فلهذا السبب نشاهده شيئا عظيما، مع أن ذلك لا يقوله عاقل . وأما أنه لم يحصل فوق تلك النقطة شيء آخر ، لا على يمينها ولا على يسارها ولا من تحتها ، فحينئذ يكون ذلك المشار إليه نقطة غير منقسمة ، وجزءا لا يتجزأ فيكون في غاية الحقارة ، وذلك باتفاق العقلاء باطل . فيثبت : أن هذا الشيء ليس من باب قياس الغائب على الشاهد ، بل هو مبني على التقسيم الدائر بين النفي والإثبات
Why is it not permissible to say: He, exalted, is one, free from composition and compounding, and yet He is great? His statement: 'The great must be composed and divisible.' We said: We grant that the great must be divisible in the witnessed [world]; but why do you say that it must be so in the absent? For analogizing the absent to the witnessed without a common ground is invalid. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that He be indivisible and be extremely small? His statement: 'On this assumption, He would be contemptible, and that is impossible for God.' We said: That which cannot be pointed to at all and cannot be sensed is like non-existence, so it is more contemptible. If this is permissible, why is what we mentioned not permissible? The answer to the first: We say: If He is great in sensory indication, He is divisible. This is not a case of analogizing the absent to the witnessed; rather it is based on a decisive proof. That is because if we point to an indivisible point, either something else occurs above it or not. If something else occurs above it, that upper thing is distinct from the lower. For if it were said that the upper is identical to the lower, then the same thing would occur in two spaces at once, and that is self-evidently false. And because if what you mentioned were permissible, it would be permissible to say that this mountain is only an individual atom and an indivisible part, but it occurs in many spaces, and for this reason we see it as something great, but no rational person says that. But if nothing else occurs above that point, neither to its right nor to its left nor below it, then that indicated point is an indivisible point and an indivisible part, so it is extremely contemptible, and that is false by the agreement of the rational. Thus it is established that this matter is not a case of analogizing the absent to the witnessed, but is based on the division that revolves between affirmation and negation.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a decisive proof. The author first presents an objection: why can it not be said that God is one, free from composition, yet still 'great'? The objector then asserts that 'great' necessarily implies being composed and divisible. The author responds by first granting that in the witnessed world greatness implies divisibility, but questions whether this necessity holds in the unseen realm, rejecting analogy without a common ground. The author then turns the tables by asking why God could not be indivisible and extremely small, anticipating the objection that this would make Him contemptible. The author counters that something imperceptible is even more contemptible, so the objection fails. Then the author gives the real answer: if God is great in the sense of sensory indication, He must be divisible. This is not an analogy but a deductive proof based on logical division. The proof proceeds by considering a point that is indicated and analyzing whether something else exists above it or not. If something does, there are distinct spaces, and the same thing cannot occupy two spaces. If nothing else exists, the point is an indivisible part, which is contemptible—contradicting the claim of greatness. Thus, the argument is a logical division (division between affirmation and negation) that decisively shows the impossibility of a spatially great yet indivisible being.

Key Terms
عظيم (great)
منقسم (divisible)
قياس الغائب على الشاهد (analogizing the absent to the witnessed)
برهان قطعي (decisive proof)
تقسيم دائر بين النفي والإثبات (division revolving between affirmation and negation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's proof is based on an analogy from the physical world (qiyas al-gha'ib 'ala al-shahid).
Correction: The author explicitly states that the proof is not an analogy but a decisive proof (burhan qati') based on logical division (taqsim da'ir bayn al-nafy wa al-ithbat).

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the author's proof that a great being must be divisible?
A) Analogy from the witnessed world
B) A logical division between affirmation and negation
C) The claim that everything great is composed
D) The impossibility of pure non-existence

Key Takeaway
The author proves that a being that is great in a spatial sense must be divisible, so God's greatness is not spatial but transcends every measurement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author rejects the idea that God's greatness is like a large object. In your daily life, do you ever unconsciously attribute physical characteristics to Allah (e.g., thinking of Him as 'above' in a literal sense)? What steps will you take to train your mind to think of Him as utterly transcendent?

Refuting God's Directionality: The Tenth Proof
Refutes the claim that God is in a specific direction using logical dilemmas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God is located in a specific direction leads to logical absurdities, including the impossibility of an indivisible atom and the finitude of infinite separation.

Core Idea
If God were in a direction, He would be limited like all created things.

This lesson examines the tenth proof from Al-Matalibul Aliyah, which refutes the notion that God occupies a specific direction. The argument employs a series of dilemmas and absurd consequences to demonstrate that attributing spatial location to God leads to contradictions. By following the dialectic, we see why classical Islamic theology affirms God's transcendence beyond space and direction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial dilemma
The proof begins by considering a point in space. It asks whether the thing in that space exists in all six directions or not. Each branch leads to an absurdity.
This sets up the entire argument structure.
2
Examine the first branch: indivisible atom
If it does not exist in all directions, it would be an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which is rejected by consensus.
This shows the first absurdity.
3
Analyze the second branch: same essence in multiple spaces
If it does exist in all directions, either the essence is identical in each space or not. Identity would mean one essence in many places, which is self-evidently impossible.
This leads to the second absurdity.
4
Consider the objection about parts that don't separate
The opponent suggests God could be composed of indivisible parts. But parts occupying space must be separable, leading to the same absurd consequences.
The author refutes this by showing it allows the Throne to be God.
5
Refute the infinite separation objection
The argument shows that if God is separate from the Throne, both must have limits, making the distance finite, contradicting infinite separation. This is unlike pre-eternity which is not bounded.
This completes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : إنا إذا أشرنا إلى شيء مختص بحيز وجهة ، فقد حصل هناك شيء . وذلك الشيء هل له وجود في سائر الجوانب الستة أم لا ؟ أما الثاني فهو باطل . لأنه يلزم عليه أن يكون إله العالم جوهراً فرداً، وجزء لا يتجزأ؟ وذلك باطل بالاتفاق . يقي القسم الأول فنقول : الحاصل في كل واحد من الأحياز التي لا تنقسم . إما أن يكون عين الحاصل في الحيز الآخر أو غيره ؟ فإن كان الأول لزم أن يكون الذات الواحدة حاصلة في الأحياز الكثيرة دفعة واحدة ، وذلك معلوم الامتناع ببديهة العقل. وأيضاً : فلو عقل ما ذكرتم فلم لا يعقل أيضاً أن يقال: العرش جوهر فرد ، وجزء لا يتجزأ وهو بعينه حاصلاً في الأحياز الكثيرة ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير ، فلم يجوز أن يكون إله العالم هو نفس العرش ؟ وحينئذ يلزم إبطال القول بإثبات الصانع وهو باطل . قوله : « لم لا يجوز أن يكون مركبا من الأجزاء إلا أن تلك الأجزاء لا تقبل التفرق والتمزق ، ؟ قلنا : لأنا بينا أن الأجزاء الشاغلة للحيز ، يجب كونها متساوية في تمام الماهية ، وحينئذ يلزم جواز التفرق والتمزق ، ولأنه لو عقل ما ذكرتم ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إله العالم هو العرش نفسه ؟ وهو وإن كان مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلا أن يقال : إله العالم هو العرش نفسه ؟ وهو وأن كان مركبا من الأجزاء والأبعاض إلا أنه لا يقبل التفرق والتمزق ، وحينئذ يلزم نفي الصانع وهو باطل . أما قوله « لم لا يجوز أن يكون الباري تعالى مباينا للعرش بينونة غير متناهية ) ؟ فنقول : هذا باطل . وذلك لأن ذات الباري تعالي ، لا بد وأن يكون متناهيا محدودا من جانب التحت وإلا لزم أن يكون العالم حاصلا في ذات الباري تعالى وهو محال بالاتفاق ، وإذا ثبت لذات الباري حد ونهاية من جانب التحت ، وثبت أيضا للعالم حد ونهاية ، فحينئذ يلزم أن يكون البعد بين الباري وبين العالم محدوداً بهذين الحدين ، وذلك يوجب كون البعد متناهيا ، فالقول بأنه غير متناه يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . وهذا بخلاف قولنا : إنه تعالى موجودا في الأزل ، فإن الأزل ليس عبارة عن وقت معين ، وحد محدود ، حتى يقال : البعد بين ذلك الوقت المعين وبين هذا الوقت الذي نحن فيه ، بعد غير متناه ، بل الأزل عبارة عن نفي الأولية من غير أن يكون ذلك عبارة عن وقت معين ، وحد محدود . وأما ههنا فبتقدير أن تكون ذات الله تعالى مباينة للعالم بالحيز والجهة ، وجب أن يكون ذات الله محدودا من جهة التحت ، فيكون ذلك الحد ظرفا معينا ، وحدا معينا ، فيكون البعد بينه وبين طرف العالم محدودا بهذين الطرفين ، فيمتنع وصفه بكونه غير متناه. وهذا تمام الكلام في تقرير هذا الدليل .
The answer: If we point to something specific to a space and direction, then something exists. Does that something have existence in all six directions or not? The second is false because it would necessitate that the God of the world is an indivisible atom and an indivisible part—that is false by consensus. As for the first division, we say: what is present in each of the indivisible spaces—either it is identical to what is present in the other space or different? If the first, then the same essence would be present in many spaces at once—something known to be impossible by the self-evident intellect. Also: if what you mentioned were conceivable, why wouldn't it also be conceivable to say: the Throne is an indivisible atom and an indivisible part, and it itself is present in many spaces? On this assumption, why is it not permissible that the God of the world is the Throne itself? Then that would invalidate the assertion of the Maker, which is false. His statement: 'Why is it not permissible that He be composed of parts but those parts do not accept separation and fragmentation?' We reply: Because we have shown that parts occupying space must be equal in entire quiddity, and then separation and fragmentation are possible. And because if what you mentioned were conceivable, why wouldn't it be permissible to say: the God of the world is the Throne itself? Even if it is composed of parts and limbs, it does not accept separation and fragmentation, and then that would necessitate denial of the Maker—which is false. As for his statement: 'Why is it not permissible that the Most High Creator be separate from the Throne with an infinite separation?' We say: This is false. That is because the essence of the Most High Creator must be finite and bounded from the underside, otherwise the world would exist within the essence of the Most High Creator—which is impossible by consensus. And if it is established that the essence of the Creator has a limit and an end from the underside, and also the world has a limit and an end, then the distance between the Creator and the world must be limited by these two limits, and that necessitates the distance be finite. So saying it is infinite necessitates combining two contradictories, which is impossible. This is unlike our saying: He exists in pre-eternity, for pre-eternity is not a specific time or a bounded limit, such that one could say: the distance between that specific time and this time in which we are is an infinite distance. Rather, pre-eternity is the negation of beginning, not a specific time or a bounded limit. But here, on the assumption that the essence of God is separate from the world by space and direction, the essence of God must be bounded from the underside, so that boundary becomes a specific container and a specific limit, and the distance between it and the edge of the world is limited by these two edges, so it is impossible to describe it as infinite. This completes the elaboration of this proof.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a proof against the claim that God is located in a specific direction. It responds to several objections. The argument begins by considering a point in space and direction. The opponent's claim implies that something exists in that particular space. The author then poses a dilemma: either that thing exists in all six directions or not. The second option is absurd because it would make God an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), which is rejected by consensus. The first option leads to another division: the object present in one indivisible space is either identical to that in another space or different. Identity would mean the same essence is simultaneously in many spaces, which is self-evidently impossible. The author further argues that if the opponent's view were accepted, one could also claim that the Throne ('arsh) is an indivisible atom present in many spaces, making the Throne itself God—an absurdity that negates the existence of the Creator. The next objection posits that God could be composed of parts that are indivisible. The author refutes this by stating that parts occupying space are essentially equal, hence divisible, which leads to the same absurd consequence. Another objection suggests that God could be separate from the Throne with an infinite separation. The author refutes this by showing that if God is separate in a direction, then His essence from the underside must be finite; otherwise the world would be within Him, which is impossible. Since both God and the world have limits, the distance between them is finite, contradicting the claim of infinite separation. The author distinguishes this from God's existence in pre-eternity (azal), which is not a temporal duration but a negation of beginning. Thus the argument concludes that the notion of God being in a direction is impossible.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (indivisible atom)
جزء لا يتجزأ (indivisible part)
بينونة غير متناهية (infinite separation)
الأزل (pre-eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'infinite separation' avoids finitude, but the author shows it still implies a finite distance because both extremes are bounded.
Correction: If God has a limit from the underside and the Throne also has a limit, the distance between them is limited by these two limits, hence finite, contradicting the claim of infinite separation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with saying God is separate from the Throne with an infinite separation?
A) It implies God is not powerful.
B) It necessitates that the distance between God and the Throne is finite because both have limits from the underside.
C) It implies the Throne is divine.
D) It contradicts the eternity of God.

Key Takeaway
The claim that God is in a direction leads to logical absurdities, confirming His transcendence beyond space.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God in your mind, do you ever unconsciously picture Him in a specific place or direction? How might this subtle assumption affect the way you relate to Him? Commit to consciously affirming His transcendence beyond space in your daily thoughts.
?
The argument shows that attributing spatial location to God leads to logical absurdities. How does this affect your confidence in the rational foundations of your faith? In what area of your aqeedah do you feel you rely more on emotion than reason? Take one step this week to study a rational proof for a belief you hold.
?
If God is not limited by space, then He is equally near to you wherever you are. How does this truth challenge your feeling of distance from Him during times of hardship? What specific action can you take today to act on the belief that He is closer than your jugular vein?

The Eleventh Proof: The Absurdity of Spatial Location
Shows absurdity: if God occupies space, He must be either penetrable like air or impenetrable like stone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the eleventh proof against God being in a place and why both possibilities lead to absurdity.

Core Idea
If God occupies space, He becomes either divisible like air or solid like stone — impossible for the Creator.

This lesson presents one of the clearest reductio arguments in kalam. The author imagines a logical consequence of affirming that God is in a specific place. By following the two possibilities, we see that the notion collapses into absurdity. We'll walk through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Understand the hypothetical scenario
The argument supposes God is in a specific space, and a person reaches the edge of that space. The question: can the person enter or not?
This sets up the dichotomy.
3
Examine the first possibility (penetration)
If the person can enter, God becomes like thin air or water—divisible and fragile. This is impossible for the Creator.
Divisibility implies contingency.
4
Examine the second possibility (impenetrability)
If the person cannot enter, God becomes like hard stone, solid and impenetrable. This would make God like a mountain above the Throne—absurd.
Both options are incompatible with divinity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that such a view is 'far from reason'. Consider why this proof is a strong refutation of anthropomorphism.
This proof guards against literalist interpretations of 'above the Throne'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : إنه تعالى لو كان حاصلا في حيز معين لكنا إذا فرضنا وصول إنسان إلى طرف ذلك الشيء ، فإما أن يمكنه الدخول فيه ، والنفوذ فيه وإما أن لا يمكن، فإن كان الأول كان كالهواء اللطيف ، والماء اللطيف ، فحينئذ يكون قابلا للتفرق والتمزق ، وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون صلبا كالحجر الصلد الذي يمتنع النفوذ فيه ، فيرجع حاصل هذا القول : إلى أن إله العالم عند القائلين بالمكان والجهة : شيء رقيق كالماء والهواء أو شيء صلب كالحجر فيكون هذا القابل أما الهواء اللطيف وأما الحجر الصلب فيكون حاصل قوله : إنه حصل فوق العرش حجر صلب : جاري مجرى جبل عظيم ، وهو بعينه ذلك الجبل . ومعلوم أن هذا القول بعيد عن العقل جدا .
The eleventh proof: If the Most High were present in a specific space, then if we suppose a person reaches the edge of that thing, either he can enter it and penetrate into it or he cannot. If the first, then it is like thin air and thin water, and then it is susceptible to separation and fragmentation. If the second, then it is solid like hard stone in which penetration is impossible. The upshot of this statement is: the God of the world according to those who affirm place and direction is either a thin thing like water and air or a hard thing like stone; so the recipient is either the thin air or the hard stone. The result of his statement is that above the Throne there is a hard stone, running like a great mountain, and it is exactly that mountain. It is known that this statement is very far from reason.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof against the notion that God occupies a specific space or direction. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum. The author considers the hypothetical scenario: if God were in a specific location (hayyiz mu'ayyan), then if a person reached the edge of that thing, one of two possibilities holds. Either the person can enter and penetrate into that thing, or cannot. If penetration is possible, then God would be like thin air or thin water—substances that are divisible and can be fragmented. That would make God impermanent and divisible, which is impossible for the Creator. If penetration is impossible, then God would be solid like hard stone (hajar suld), impenetrable. Then, according to the proponents of spatial location, God would be something like a hard stone sitting above the Throne, comparable to a great mountain. The author concludes that such a notion is far from reason and clearly absurd. The proof thus demonstrates the irrationality of attributing spatial location to God.

Key Terms
الحيز المعين (specific space)
الهواء اللطيف (thin air)
الماء اللطيف (thin water)
الحجر الصلد (hard stone)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that verses like 'The Merciful is established above the Throne' imply a literal physical location for God.
Correction: This proof shows that such literalism leads to absurd consequences (divisibility or solidity), so the verses must be interpreted metaphorically.

Checkpoint

A) God would be like thin water
B) God would be penetrable
C) God would be like a hard stone that cannot be entered
D) God would be everywhere

Key Takeaway
The eleventh proof demonstrates that attributing spatial location to God leads to contradictory and irrational implications, thereby upholding divine transcendence.

?

?
The author calls the idea of God as a stone above the Throne 'very far from reason'. What images or concepts about God have you unknowingly held that might be equally irrational? What steps will you take to purify your belief in tawhid?

The Twelfth Proof: Inductive Argument from Bodily Strength
Uses induction: the more corporeal a thing, the weaker its active power; God is all-powerful, so not corporeal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an inductive correlation between corporeality and active power leads to the conclusion that God cannot be corporeal.

Core Idea
The more corporeal a thing is, the weaker its active power — God is all-powerful, so not corporeal.

This lesson introduces the twelfth proof from the text, an inductive speculative argument. The author observes a consistent pattern in creation: the stronger the element of corporeality in a thing, the weaker its power to act. This observed correlation sets the stage for a powerful argument for God's transcendence beyond physicality.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the concise style of the inductive argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the core correlation is clear.
2
Identify the inductive correlation
Look for the two-part observation: more corporeality → weaker active power; less corporeality → stronger active power. This is the key premise.
Induction generalizes from observed cases.
3
Examine the example of the earth
The author gives the earth as the extreme example: it is the most corporeal, so it only receives effects, never actively produces them.
This illustrates the correlation in an extreme case.
4
Connect to the conclusion about God
Reflect on the implication: if active power is inversely related to corporeality, then the absolutely powerful God must be completely non-corporeal.
This is the direction of the proof, though the conclusion is not fully stated in this excerpt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : وهي حجة استقرائية اعتبارية - فنقول : إنا رأينا أن الشيء كلما كان حصول معنى الجسمية فيه أقوى وأكمل ، كانت القوة الفاعلة المؤثرة فيه أضعف وأنقص . وكلما كان حصول معنى الجسمية فيه أنقص وأضعف ، كان حصول القوة الفاعلة فيه أقوى وأكمل ، وتقريره : إنا وجدنا الأرض أكثف الأجسام وأقواها حجمية ، فلا جرم لم يحصل فيها إلا قبول الأثر
The twelfth proof: It is an inductive speculative proof. We say: We have observed that whenever the acquisition of the meaning of corporeality (jismiyya) in a thing is stronger and more perfect, the active efficient power in it is weaker and deficient. And whenever the acquisition of the meaning of corporeality in it is deficient and weaker, the acquisition of active power in it is stronger and more perfect. Its clarification: We have found that the earth is the densest of bodies and the strongest in corporeality, so it does not acquire anything except the reception of effect.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage begins the twelfth proof, which is an inductive (istiqra'iyya) and speculative (i'tibariyya) argument. The author presents an observed correlation: the more something partakes of the meaning of corporeality (jismiyya), the weaker its active power (quwwa fa'ila mu'aththira). Conversely, the less corporeal a thing is, the stronger its active power. The author uses the earth as an example: it is the densest and most corporeal of bodies, and thus it only receives effects (qubul al-athar) from outside, without being an agent itself. The argument implies that God, being the most powerful agent, cannot have any corporeal nature. The text in this window is incomplete, but the direction of the argument is clear.

Key Terms
الجسمية (corporeality)
القوة الفاعلة (active power)
استقرائية (inductive)
قبول الأثر (reception of effect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'corporeal' simply means having size or shape, without any connection to power.
Correction: In this argument, corporeality is directly linked to passivity; the more corporeal a thing, the more passive and receptive it is, and the less active power it possesses.

Checkpoint

A) The more corporeal, the stronger the active power.
B) The less corporeal, the weaker the active power.
C) The more corporeal, the weaker the active power.
D) There is no relationship.

Key Takeaway
The inductive correlation that greater corporeality implies weaker active power provides a foundational premise for proving that God, who is absolutely powerful, cannot be corporeal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that the more corporeal something is, the weaker its active power. God is pure active power. Do you ever imagine God as having a location or physical form? What would it mean for your worship if you truly internalized that God is beyond all material limitations?
?
The earth, the densest body, only receives effects and does nothing itself. Are you letting yourself be passive like the earth, shaped by your environment and desires, or are you actively seeking to reflect God's power in your character? What is one concrete way you can act with intention today?
?

The First Preliminary: Spatial Relations in the Witnessed World
Author states the first preliminary: spatial judgment among existents and its causal proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every two existents in the witnessed world must be either adjacent or separated by a direction, and why this judgment requires a cause.

Core Idea
Spatial relations among existents are grounded in the nature of existence itself.

This passage establishes a foundational principle of kalam: that existent things in our world necessarily have spatial relationships. Before we can discuss higher theological matters, we need to understand this basic judgment and its rational cause. The author argues that it is existence itself that necessitates such relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the author's concise argument.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the statement that defines the first preliminary: every two existents are either adjacent or separated by a direction.
The terms 'adjacent' (mujanib) and 'separated' (mubayin) are spatial relations.
3
Understand the reasoning
Notice that the author argues this judgment must have a cause, which is existence itself, because non-existents do not admit spatial relations.
The proof is based on the distinction between existents and non-existents.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this preliminary is needed for kalam discussions — it establishes a rational basis for spatial concepts.
This shows that kalam uses rational arguments, not just empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا الكلام لا يتم إلا بتقرير مقدمات ، ونحن نذكرها : أما المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : كل موجودين في الشاهد ، فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما بجانبا للآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بجهة . فنقول : هذا الحكم لا بد له من علة . والدليل عليه : هو أن المعدوم لا يصح فيه هذا الحكم ، وهذه الموجودات التي في الشاهد يصح فيها هذا الحكم ، ولولا امتياز ما صح فيه هذا الحكم عما لا يصح فيه هذا الحكم ، وإلا لامتنع حصول هذا الامتياز .
Know that this discussion cannot be completed except by establishing preliminaries, and we mention them: As for the first preliminary: it is our statement: Every two existents in the witnessed world must either be adjacent to one another or separate from one another by a direction. So we say: this judgment must have a cause. The evidence for it is that the non-existent does not admit this judgment, and these existents in the witnessed world admit this judgment, and were it not for the distinction between what admits this judgment and what does not admit this judgment, then this distinction would be impossible.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage introduces the need for preliminaries and presents the first preliminary. The author states that in the witnessed world, any two existent things must have a spatial relationship: either they are adjacent (mujanib) or separate (mubayin) by a direction (jiha). This is a necessary judgment. The author then argues that this judgment requires a cause ('illa), and the cause is existence itself, because non-existent things do not admit such spatial relations. The distinction between existents (which have spatial relations) and non-existents (which do not) shows that existence is the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial relations are just observed facts with no deeper cause.
Correction: The author argues that the spatial judgment has a cause (existence), making it a rationally necessary judgment.

Checkpoint

C) God is not in a direction.
D) Non-existence has no spatial judgment.

Key Takeaway
The spatial relation between existents in the witnessed world is not arbitrary; it is a necessary consequence of existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Spatial Judgment Is Not Caused by Substance or Accident
Author argues spatial judgment is not caused by being substance or accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the spatial judgment of being adjacent or separate cannot be caused by being a substance or an accident, based on the author's reductio argument.

Core Idea
Substance and accident cannot determine spatial relations—only existence does.

This lesson presents the second preliminary from the author's argument about spatial judgment. The author uses a simple but powerful reductio to show that neither substance nor accident can be the cause of a thing being adjacent to or separate from another. Understanding this clears the way for the true cause, which is existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
2
Identify the two candidates being ruled out
Notice that the author examines two possibilities: substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad). These are the classical categories of existents.
3
Follow the reductio for substance
The author assumes substance is the cause. Trace how that leads to a contradiction because substance cannot be adjacent to another.
Adjacency (mujanab) means touching or being alongside; this is impossible for substances as each occupies its own place.
4
Follow the analogous argument for accident
Now see how the same reasoning applies to accident: if accident were the cause, it would have to be separate by direction, but accidents inhere in substances and lack independent spatial location.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Both candidates are eliminated. The spatial judgment's cause must be something else—existence itself, as the author will later argue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي في بيان أنه يمتنع تعليل هذا الحكم ، بخصوص كونه جوهرا ، أو بخصوص كونه عرضا . والدليل عليه : أن المقتضى لهذا الحكم لو كان هو كونه جوهرا ، لصدق على الجوهر أنه ينقسم إلى ما يكون مجانبا لغيره ، وإلى ما يكون مباينا عنه . ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل ، لأن الجوهر يمتنع أن يكون مجانبا لغيره ، وبهذا الطريق يظهر أن المقتضى لهذا الحكم ، ليس کونه عرضا أيضا ، لامتناع كون العرض مباينا لغيره بالجهة .
As for the second preliminary: it is an exposition of the impossibility of attributing this judgment to the specific fact of being a substance (jawhar) or being an accident ('arad). The evidence for it is that if the cause for this judgment were its being a substance, then it would be true of substance that it is divided into what is adjacent to another and what is separate from it. But it is known that this is false, because substance cannot be adjacent to another. And in this way it becomes clear that the cause for this judgment is also not its being an accident, because an accident cannot be separate from another by direction.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the second preliminary, which rules out substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad) as possible causes for the spatial judgment (adjacent or separate). The author uses a reductio: if being a substance were the cause, then substances would have to admit both possibilities, but in fact a substance cannot be adjacent to another (since each substance occupies its own place and cannot interpenetrate). Similarly, an accident cannot be separate (mubayin) by direction because accidents inhere in substances and do not have independent spatial position. Therefore, the cause must be something else, namely existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial relations (adjacent/separate) are determined by whether a thing is a substance or an accident.
Correction: The author argues that neither substance nor accident can be the cause, because each would lead to impossible consequences.

Checkpoint

C) Because substance is eternal.
D) Because substance cannot be separate.

Key Takeaway
The spatial judgment of adjacency or separation is not caused by being a substance or an accident, as shown by reductio arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Why Spatial Relations Are Not Based on Creation
Author provides three proofs that spatial judgment is not caused by origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the spatial judgment (adjacent or separate) is not caused by origination, and the three proofs that establish this.

Core Idea
Origination does not cause spatial relations; existence alone suffices.

In this third preliminary, the author argues that the judgment of spatial relationship—whether objects are adjacent or separate—does not depend on whether they are created. He provides three distinct proofs to show that the cause is existence itself, not the property of being originated. This is crucial for later arguments about the relationship between Creator and creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The author denies that origination (huduth) is the cause of the spatial judgment. Read the opening sentence carefully.
The judgment is 'adjacent or separate'.
2
First proof: non-existence is not a cause
Origination is existence preceded by non-existence. Non-existence cannot be a cause, so only existence remains as the cause.
3
Second proof: materialists affirm spatial relations
If origination were the cause, then ignorance of origination would imply ignorance of the spatial judgment. But materialists (dahriyya) who believe in eternity still affirm spatial relations, so origination is not the cause.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Third proof: necessary vs. inferred knowledge
The spatial judgment is known by necessity, while origination is known by proof. A necessary truth cannot be based on something inferential.
5
Conclusion: existence is the cause
The author concludes that the cause is not origination, but existence itself.
This aligns with the first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : في بيان أن هذا الحكم غير معلل بالحدوث . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن الحادث عبارة عن وجود سبقه عدم ، والعدم غير داخل في العلية وإذا سقط العدم عن درجة الاعتبار ، لم يبق إلا الوجود . والثاني : إنه لو كان هذا الحكم معللاً بالحدوث، لكان الجاهل بكون السماء محدثة ، وجب أن يكون جاهلا بأن السماء بالنسبة إلى سائر الموجودات، التي في هذا العالم ، إما أن تكون مجانبا لها ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة ، لأن المقتضى للحكم إذا كان أمرا معينا ، فالجاهل بذلك المقتضى بذلك المقتضى ، يجب أن يكون جاهلا بذلك الحكم ، ألا ترى أن الموجود لما كان هو المستدعي للتقسيم إلى القديم ، وإلى المحدث، لا جرم كان اعتقاد أنه غير موجود يكون مانعا للتقسيم بالقدم والحدوث ، ولما كان التقسيم إلى الأسود والأبيض مفرعا على كونه ملونا ، لا جرم كان اعتقاد أن الشيء غير ملون ، مانعا من اعتقاد التقسيم إلى الأسود والأبيض. ولما رأينا أن الدهري الذي يعتقد قدم السموات والأرضين لا يمنعه ذلك من اعتقاد أن السموات إما أن تكون مجانبة للأرضين ، أو مباينا عنها بالجهة ، علمنا : أن هذا الحكم غير معلل بالحدوث . الوجه الثالث : وهو أن كون هذه السموات والأرضين محدثة ، أمر علم بالدليل . [ ووجوب كونها إما مجانية ، وإما مباينة أمر معلوم بالضرورة ، والوصف الذي يعلم بالدليل (۱) ] يمتنع جعله أصلا للوصف الذين نعلم ثبوته بالضرورة . فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن المقتضى لقولنا : إن هذه الأشياء ، إما أن تكون مجانبة ، أو مباينة ، ليس هو الحدوث .
The third preliminary: it is an exposition that this judgment is not caused by origination (huduth). Evidence for it are aspects: First: The originated thing is an existence preceded by non-existence, and non-existence is not included in causality. When non-existence drops out of consideration, only existence remains. Second: If this judgment were caused by origination, then someone ignorant that the heaven is originated would necessarily be ignorant that the heaven in relation to other existents in this world is either adjacent to them or separate from them by direction, because if the cause of a judgment is a specific matter, then the one ignorant of that cause must be ignorant of that judgment. Do you not see that existence is what necessitates the division into eternal and originated, so belief that something is non-existent prevents the division into eternal and originated. And since the division into black and white is based on being colored, belief that a thing is not colored prevents belief in division into black and white. Since we see that a materialist (dahri) who believes in the eternity of the heavens and earth is not prevented by that from believing that the heavens are either adjacent to the earth or separate from it by direction, we know that this judgment is not caused by origination. Third: That these heavens and earth are originated is known by proof, while the necessity of their being either adjacent or separate is known by necessity. It is impossible to make that which is known by proof a basis for that which we know by necessity. So it is established by these aspects that the cause of our statement that these things are either adjacent or separate is not origination.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage provides three arguments to prove that the spatial judgment (adjacent or separate) is not caused by origination (huduth). The first argument: origination is existence preceded by non-existence; non-existence cannot be a cause, so the cause must be existence alone. The second argument: if origination were the cause, then ignorance of origination would entail ignorance of the spatial judgment. However, materialists (dahriyya) who believe the heavens are eternal still affirm spatial relations, which shows the judgment does not depend on origination. The third argument: origination is known by proof (dalil), while the spatial dichotomy is known by necessity (darura). A necessary truth cannot be based on something that is only probable or inferential. Therefore, the cause is not origination but existence.

Key Terms
حدوث
حادث
عدم
ضرورة
دهري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that being created (origination) necessitates spatial limitation or a specific relation to other things.
Correction: The author argues that origination does not cause spatial judgment; rather, existence itself is the cause. Even those who deny origination (materialists) still affirm spatial relations.

Checkpoint

C) Spatial judgment is known by necessity, origination by proof.
D) Origination implies a direction for God.

Key Takeaway
The spatial dichotomy of adjacent or separate is not caused by origination, but by existence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Are there areas where you have been looking for proofs to validate what you already know in your heart—and what is holding you back from simply acting on that certainty?

Answering the First Two Doubts: The Circularity Argument
The author answers the first two doubts by showing the impossibility premise is not necessary and leads to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility premise must be inferential and how the opponents' argument leads to circularity.

Core Idea
The opponents' proof collapses because it depends on the very premise it tries to disprove.

In this passage, the author delivers a decisive response to the first two doubts raised against affirming a being outside the world. He recalls that the impossibility of such a being is either known necessarily or by inference. Since it is not necessary, it must be inferential. But the opponents' proofs rely on rejecting this premise, creating a circularity. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice that the author references an earlier chapter.
2
Identify the classification of knowledge
The passage states that knowledge of the impossibility is either necessary (badihi) or inferential (istidlali). Understand the distinction: necessary knowledge is self-evident, inferential requires proof.
3
Trace the circularity argument
The author argues: if the premise is inferential, then the proofs used to invalidate it themselves depend on that invalidation. Using those proofs to disprove the premise creates a circle.
This is the key logical move.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author calls this the 'reliable answer'. Ask yourself why this refutation is considered strong and how it undermines the doubts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الجواب عن الشبهة الأولى والثانية: فقد تقدم في الفصل الأول على سبيل الاستقصاء. وذلك لأن قولنا: إن إثبات موجود لا يحصل في داخل العالم ولا في خارجه: محال، إما أن يكون العلم بامتناع هذا القسم علما ضروريا، أو استدلاليا. وبطل كونه ضروريا للوجوه الكثيرة المذكورة فوجب أن يكون استدلاليا. لكن صحة الدلائل التي ذكرتموها موقوفة على إبطال هذا، فلو ثبت إبطال هذه المقدمة بتلك الدلائل، لزم الدور، وهذا هو الجواب المعتمد الذي يعول عليه.
As for the answer to the first and second doubts: it has already been presented in the first chapter in a comprehensive manner. That is because our statement: 'It is impossible to affirm a being that is not found inside the world nor outside it' – the knowledge of the impossibility of this category is either necessary knowledge or inferential knowledge. Its being necessary has been invalidated by the many mentioned reasons, so it must be inferential. But the validity of the proofs you have mentioned depends on invalidating this [premise]. So if the invalidation of this premise were established by those proofs, circularity would follow. And this is the reliable answer to rely upon.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the first two doubts. The author recalls a premise: affirming a being neither inside nor outside the world is impossible. Knowledge of this impossibility is either necessary (badihi) or inferential (istidlali). It is not necessary, as proven earlier, so it must be inferential. However, the proofs offered by the opponents to establish their position rely on rejecting this premise. If they use those same proofs to invalidate the premise, a circularity (dawr) results. The author presents this as the definitive, reliable answer.

Key Terms
الشبهة
ضروري
استدلالي
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is committing a circular argument.
Correction: The author is exposing that the opponents' argument is circular because their proofs rely on the very premise they are trying to disprove.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the answer to the first two doubts is reliable?
A) Because it is based on necessary knowledge.
B) Because it shows the proofs are circular.
C) Because it relies on scripture.
D) Because it agrees with Ibn Sina.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' argument fails because it is circular: their proofs depend on rejecting the impossibility premise, yet they use those proofs to reject it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it cost you to submit fully to the truth even if it challenges your comfort?
?
The author points to the 'reliable answer' that breaks the circularity. In your spiritual life, when you encounter doubts, do you seek a reliable answer that cuts through the confusion, or do you remain in a cycle of questioning without resolution? Decide today to seek the truth with sincerity, and hold yourself accountable to follow it wherever it leads.

Dismissal of the Third Objection via Prior Treatment
The author dismisses the third objection by referring to a previous exhaustive treatment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third objection is considered answered by an earlier exhaustive discussion, and how this methodological approach works in theological argumentation.

Core Idea
A prior exhaustive treatment can suffice to dismiss later objections.

In this very brief passage, the author addresses the third objection against his view by simply pointing out that the premise underlying it has already been fully examined earlier. Rather than repeating the argument, he relies on the earlier thorough treatment. This lesson helps us see how Islamic theologians often use cross-referencing to avoid redundancy while maintaining argumentative rigor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the brevity of the response.
2
Identify the objection's premise
The objection is based on the claim that perception requires a form of the known in the knower. Recognize that the author does not challenge this premise here.
3
Understand the author's strategy
Instead of providing a new refutation, the author refers to an earlier exhaustive treatment. This shows his confidence in prior arguments.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this is a valid method in scholarly discourse: it avoids repetition and trusts that established arguments remain effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : إنه بناء على أن إدراك الشيء، لا يتم إلا عند حصول صورة المعلوم في ذات العالم، والاستقصاء التام فيه. قد سبق.
And the answer to the third objection: it is based on the fact that the perception of a thing is not completed except when a form of the known object occurs in the essence of the knower, and the exhaustive treatment of it has already preceded.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a brief response to the third objection. The author states that the objection is based on a particular epistemological premise: that perception of something requires a form (surah) of the known to be present in the knower. He does not elaborate further here, simply noting that an exhaustive treatment (al-istiqsa' al-tamm) of this matter has already been given earlier in the book. Thus this objection is considered already refuted by that earlier discussion.

The Refutation of Certainty Through Dilemma and Circularity
A dilemma and circularity refute a claim of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a dilemma can be used to refute a claim that a proposition is as certain as self-evident truths, and why this leads to either contradiction or circularity.

Core Idea
When a claim of certainty leads to either contradiction or circularity, it must be false.

Sometimes opponents claim their judgments are as certain as self-evident truths. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi here refutes such a claim by posing a powerful dilemma: either the knowledge needed to distinguish sources of certainty is necessary or theoretical. Both paths lead to absurdity. This passage demonstrates a sophisticated use of logical reasoning in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The opponent claims that certainty in a particular proposition (about two existing things) is equal to certainty in self-evident truths. The author challenges this by asking how we know which faculty (intellect or imagination) produces which certainty.
The key is that the opponent must have a way to distinguish between truthful and false sources of certainty.
2
Follow the first horn: necessary knowledge
If the knowledge distinguishing the certainties is necessary, then it would be necessarily known that the judgment is false, contradicting the claim that it is as certain as self-evident truths.
This horn shows that the claim leads to a direct contradiction.
3
Follow the second horn: theoretical knowledge
If that knowledge is theoretical, then self-evident truths would depend on theoretical reasoning, leading to circularity (since theoretical knowledge depends on self-evident truths). This would undermine all self-evident truths.
Circularity is invalid, so the original claim cannot be maintained.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا حاجة بنا في هذا المقام إلى بيان : أن الحاكم بهذه المقدمة هو الوهم أو غيره . وأما إن اعترفتم بأن الجزم بهذه القضية يساوي الجزم بالبديهيات . فنقول : إن العلم بأن أحد الجزمين حاصل من العقل الصادق ، وأن الجزم الثاني حاصل من الوهم الكاذب ، أهو علم ضروري أو نظري ؟ فإن كان علما ضروريا ، كان العلم الضروري حاصلا بأن الحكم بأن كل موجودين فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما ساريا في الآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة حكم كاذب . ومتي كان الأمر كذلك، كان كذب هذه القضية معلوما بالضرورة . وقد كنا فرضنا : أن الجزم بصحتها يساوي الجزم بالبديهيات في القوة . هذا خلف . وإن كان ذلك العلم مستفادا من الحجة والدليل، فحينئذ تتوقف صحة البديهيات ، على هذا النظر والدليل ، لكن العلم بصحة النظريات ، موقوف على العلم بالبديهيات. فيلزم الدور ، والدور باطل ، فكان العلم بصحة هذه البديهيات موقوفا ، على مقدمة باطلة ، والموقوف على الباطل باطل ، فيلزم القدح في كل البديهيات ، وذلك باطل قطعا . فعلمنا : أن الجواب الذي عول عليه الشيخ الرئيس ههنا فيه هذا البحث الغامض والله أعلم
We have no need in this context to explain that the judge of this premise is the imagination or something else. But if you acknowledge that certainty in this proposition equals certainty in self-evident truths, we say: the knowledge that one certainty comes from the truthful intellect and the second certainty comes from the false imagination—is it necessary knowledge or theoretical knowledge? If it is necessary knowledge, then it is necessarily known that the judgment that 'for every two existents, one must either permeate the other or be separate from it by direction' is false. When this is the case, the falsity of this proposition is known by necessity. And we had assumed that certainty in its truth equals certainty in self-evident truths in strength—this is a contradiction. And if that knowledge is derived from proof and evidence, then the validity of self-evident truths depends on this reasoning and evidence, but knowledge of the validity of theoretical matters depends on knowledge of self-evident truths. This leads to circularity, and circularity is invalid. Thus the knowledge of the validity of these self-evident truths became dependent on an invalid premise, and what depends on the invalid is invalid, so this necessitates undermining all self-evident truths, and that is absolutely invalid. So we know that the answer that the Chief Master relied on here contains this subtle investigation, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that a certain judgment (that every two existents must either permeate or be separate by direction) is as certain as self-evident truths. The author uses a dilemma: either the knowledge of which faculty produces each certainty is necessary or theoretical. If necessary, then the falsity of that judgment is necessarily known, contradicting its claimed certainty. If theoretical, then self-evident truths would depend on theoretical reasoning, leading to circularity and undermining all self-evident truths. Thus the argument fails.

Key Terms
ضروري
نظري
دور
بديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that appealing to necessity always resolves the issue, but here the author shows it leads to contradiction.
Correction: If the knowledge is necessary, it actually leads to a contradiction with the original claim of certainty, not a resolution.

Checkpoint
What two possibilities does the author consider for the knowledge that one certainty comes from the truthful intellect and the other from false imagination?
A) necessary or theoretical
B) intuitive or deductive
C) divine or human
D) innate or acquired

Key Takeaway
A claim that a judgment is as certain as self-evident truths is refuted by showing it leads either to a necessary contradiction or to a vicious circularity that undermines all knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief so firmly that you considered it beyond doubt, only to discover that it rested on an assumption that leads to contradiction or circular reasoning? Where in your life might that be happening right now?
?
This lesson shows that even a claim as strong as self-evident truth can be false if it leads to absurdity. What belief do you hold that you are unwilling to test with a similar dilemma? Are you prepared to subject it to such scrutiny?
?
The argument here threatens to undermine all self-evident truths if the opponent's position were correct. In your own understanding of faith, are there foundations you treat as self-evident that could be vulnerable if a single premise is flawed? How would you respond if someone showed you a circularity in your reasoning?

Divisibility as a Non-Existent Attribute
Demonstrates that the attribute of being divisible is a non-existent attribute and therefore cannot be caused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the acceptance of division is considered a non-existent attribute in kalam and why it cannot be causally explained.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot be caused; divisibility is a privation, not a real property.

In this passage, the author addresses a crucial objection: if divisibility were a real attribute, explaining it would lead to an infinite regress. By demonstrating that divisibility is a non-existent attribute, he shows that no causal explanation is needed. This argument is a cornerstone of kalam reasoning about attributes and causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once, then the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow: the objector concedes, then the author argues step by step.
2
Identify the main thesis
The author aims to prove that the acceptance of division (qabūl al-qisma) is a non-existent attribute (ḥukm ʿadamī) and therefore cannot be caused. Find where this claim is stated.
The thesis is stated at the beginning: 'but the acceptance of division is a non-existent attribute and non-existence is not caused.'
3
Understand the infinite regress argument
The author argues that if the original capacity to accept division were a real positive attribute, then to accept that capacity would require another capacity, leading to infinite regress. This shows the original capacity must be non-existent.
This uses the impossibility of tasalsul (infinite regress) to prove a privative attribute.
4
Analyze the specificity argument
The acceptance of division is a specific kind of acceptance. The specificity cannot be an existent attribute because that would make existence subsist in non-existence, which is impossible. Therefore, the specificity is also non-existent.
5
Conclude why causation is impossible
Since non-existence is pure negation, it cannot be the object of causal influence. Therefore, divisibility requires no cause for its existence.
This refutes the need to explain why something is divisible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا : أن كل موجودين في الشاهد ، فلا بد وأن يكون أحدهما مجانبا للآخر ، أو مباينا عنه بالجهة ، لكن كون الشيء بحيث يصدق عليه إما كذا وإما كذا : إشارة إلى كونه قابلا للانقسام إليهما لكن قبول القسمة حكم عدمي والعدم لا يعلل . وإنما قلنا : إن قبول القسمة حكم عدمي لأن الأصل للقبول حكم فوجب أن يكون قبول القسمة حكما عدميا . وإنما قلنا : إن أصل القبول حكم عدمي ، لأنه لو كان أمرا ثبوتيا ، لكان صفة من صفات الشيء المحكوم عليه ، بكونه قابلا . والذوات قابلة للصفات القائمة بها ، فيكون قبول ذلك القبول زائدا عليه . ويلزم التسلسل ، وهو محال . وإنما قلنا : إنه لما كان أصل القبول حكما عدميا ، كان قبول القسمة أيضا كذلك . لأن قبول القسمة قبول مخصوص وتلك الخصوصية . إن كانت صفة موجودة ، لزم قيام الوجود بالعدم، وهو محال ، وإن كانت عدمية ، لزم القطع بأن قبول القسمة عدمي . وإذا ثبت أنه حكم عدمي ، امتنع تعليله . لأن العدم نفي محض ، فكان التأثير فيه محالا . فثبت : أن قبول القسمة لا يمكن تعليله
Second question: We grant that any two existents in the observable world must be either adjacent or separate in direction. However, a thing being such that it can be described as either this or that is an indication that it is divisible into the two. But the acceptance of division (qabūl al-qisma) is a non-existent attribute (ḥukm ʿadamī), and non-existence is not caused. And we say: the acceptance of division is a non-existent attribute because the original acceptance (aṣl al-qabūl) is a non-existent attribute, so it follows that the acceptance of division is a non-existent attribute. And we say: the original acceptance is a non-existent attribute because if it were a positive (thubūtī) thing, it would be an attribute of the thing described as being receptive. And essences are receptive to the attributes subsisting in them, so the acceptance of that acceptance would be additional to it, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. And we say: since the original acceptance is a non-existent attribute, the acceptance of division is likewise, because the acceptance of division is a specific acceptance, and that specificity—if it were an existent attribute, then existence would subsist in non-existence, which is impossible; and if it were non-existent, then it is necessarily concluded that the acceptance of division is non-existent. And when it is established that it is a non-existent attribute, its causation is impossible, because non-existence is pure negation, so affecting it is impossible. So it is established that the acceptance of division cannot be caused.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer regarding the classification of divisibility. The objector concedes the earlier premise but argues that being divisible is a non-existent attribute and thus does not need a cause. The author explains: if the capacity to receive division were a real positive attribute, then to receive that capacity would require another capacity, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, the original capacity is a non-existent attribute. Since it is non-existent, it cannot be caused, because non-existence is pure negation with no substrate for causation. This refutes the need to explain why something is divisible.

Key Terms
قبول القسمة
حكم عدمي
تعليل
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes must be real, existing properties that require a cause.
Correction: In kalam, some attributes are 'non-existent' (ḥukm ʿadamī) like divisibility or blindness; they are privations, not positive properties, and thus do not need a cause.

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that the acceptance of division cannot be caused?
A) Because it is a positive attribute that requires no cause
B) Because it is a non-existent attribute and non-existence cannot be affected
C) Because it is a substance and substances are uncaused
D) Because it is a creation of God and thus beyond explanation

Key Takeaway
Divisibility is a non-existent attribute, so it requires no causal explanation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'non-existent attributes' do you cling to as if they were real flaws requiring an external cause? Could your excuses be mere privations that you keep 'causing' unnecessarily? How will you stop fabricating causes and simply acknowledge the absence without blame?
?
The argument shows that an infinite regress is impossible—there must be a stopping point. In your spiritual life, are you caught in an endless cycle of seeking reasons for your shortcomings instead of accepting them as privative? What would it mean to allow your soul to rest in the knowledge that some things simply are not?

Distinction Between Adjacent and Separate in Direction as Distinct Rulings
The author distinguishes two different rulings and their respective causes for substances and accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'being adjacent' and 'being separate in direction' are two different rulings with different causes, not two sides of the same property.

Core Idea
Adjacency and separation in direction apply to different categories: accident vs. substance.

In this passage, the author addresses a third objection to the claim that divisibility into parts is caused by something else. The objector suggests that perhaps the division into adjacent and separate could be caused by being a substance or an accident. The author clarifies that these two categories are not flip sides of the same coin; rather, they are two distinct rulings, each with its own cause. We'll unpack this critical distinction that prevents confusion between the properties of substances and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Begin by reading the objection: why can't divisibility be caused by being a substance or accident? This sets up the confusion.
The objector thinks the classification into adjacent/separate is a single property caused by either.
2
Follow the author's response
Read the author's reply carefully. He argues that being a substance prevents adjacency, and being an accident prevents separation in direction. Then he asks the objector to clarify what they mean by the division.
Notice the logic: a cause for division cannot be a preventer of one of the divisions.
3
Understand the two distinct rulings
The author distinguishes two actual rulings: for accidents, they must be adjacent to something (their substance); for substances, they must be separate in direction from other substances. Each has its own cause.
This is the key teaching: the two properties belong to different categories.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you know what causes an accident to be adjacent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : هب أنه من الأحكام المعللة ، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك معللا بخصوص كونه جوهرا أو بخصوص كونه عرضا ؟ قوله : « لأن كونه جوهرا ، يمنع من المجانبة ، وكونه عرضا يمنع من المباينة بالجهة ، وما كان علة لقبول الانقسام إلى قسمين يمتنع كونه مانعا من أحد القسمين ، قلنا : ما الذي تريدونه بقولكم : الموجود في الشاهد ينقسم إلى المجانب ، وإلى المباين بالجهة ؟ إن أردتم به أن الموجود في الشاهد قسمان : أحدهما : الذي يكون مجانبا لغيره ، وهو العرض . والثاني : الذي يجب أن يكون مباينا لغيره بالجهة وهو الجوهر ، فهذا مسلم . لكنه في الحقيقة إشارة إلى حكمين مختلفين معللين بعلتين مختلفتين ، فإن عندنا وجوب كونه مجانيا بغيره، معلل بكونه عرضا ، ووجوب كون القسم الثاني مباينا عن غيره
Third question: Suppose it is among the caused rulings; why is it not allowed that it be caused by its being specifically a substance or specifically an accident? He said: 'Because being a substance prevents adjacency, and being an accident prevents separation in direction, and whatever is a cause for accepting division into two parts cannot be a preventer of one of the two parts.' We say: What do you mean by your statement: 'The existent in the observable world is divided into the adjacent and the separate in direction'? If you mean by it that the existent in the observable world has two divisions: one of them is what is adjacent to another, which is the accident; and the second is what must be separate from another in direction, which is the substance—this is conceded. But in reality it is an indication of two different rulings caused by two different causes, for we hold that the necessity of being adjacent to another is caused by its being an accident, and the necessity of the second division being separate from another
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is the third objection and answer. The objector suggests that divisibility could be caused by being a substance or an accident. The author responds by clarifying that the categories 'adjacent' and 'separate in direction' are not two sides of the same coin but two distinct rulings. The accident is adjacent to its substance, while the substance is separate in direction from other substances. Each ruling has its own cause: adjacency is caused by being an accident, and separation by being a substance. Thus, the objector's confusion is resolved by distinguishing the two different types of existents and their properties.

Correction: They are distinct rulings applying to different categories: accidents are adjacent to their substances, substances are separate in direction from other substances.

Checkpoint

B) Its being an accident
C) Its being divisible

The properties of adjacency and separation in direction belong to different categories of existents (accidents and substances) and each has its own specific cause.

Fourth Objection: Exhaustive Enumeration of Causes
Author refutes objection that only huduth and wujud are possible causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that only huduth and wujud are possible causes is not logically proven, and how the author refutes it using the principle that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

Core Idea
Surveying all possibilities does not prove exclusivity.

The objector challenges the assertion that the cause must be either huduth or wujud. He demands a proof for this exhaustive enumeration. The author responds by invoking a fundamental logical principle: just because you haven't found something does not mean it does not exist. This lesson unpacks that response and its implications for certainty in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Understand what the objector concedes (the judgment is not due to being a substance or accident) and what he rejects (the exclusivity of huduth and wujud).
2
Grasp the logical principle
Focus on the author's statement that 'absence of finding does not necessitate the absence of existence.' This is the core of the refutation.
This principle is well-known in logic.
3
Apply to kalam methodology
Consider why this principle undermines the certainty of the enumeration. The author also notes that broad divisions do not even yield probability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : سلمنا أنه لا يمكن تعليل هذا الحكم بخصوص كونه جوهرا ، ولا بخصوص كونه عرضا . فلم قلتم : إنه لا بد من تعليله إما بالحدوث وإما بالوجود ؟ وما الدليل على هذا الحصر ؟ أقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : سبرنا وبحثنا فلم تجد قسما آخر إلا أنا بينا في الكتب المنطقية : أن عدم الوجدان لا يوجب وجدان العدم، وقد قررنا : أن التقسيمات المنتشرة لا تفيد الظن فضلا عن اليقين
The fourth question: We concede that this judgment cannot be caused by the specificity of being a substance, nor by the specificity of being an accident. So why do you say that it must be caused either by temporal origination (huduth) or by existence (wujud)? And what is the proof of this enumeration? The utmost that can be said is: 'We surveyed and investigated and did not find another category except that we have shown in logical works that the absence of finding does not necessitate the absence of existence, and we have established that the widespread divisions do not yield probability, let alone certainty.'
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is the fourth objection and its answer. The objector questions the exhaustive claim that the cause must be either huduth or wujud. The author responds by citing the logical principle that absence of evidence is not evidence of absence, and that broad divisions do not even give probability, let alone certainty. Thus the enumeration is not proven.

First Aspect of the Fifth Objection: Sensory Indication as Cause of Distinctness
Objection proposes sensory indication as alternative cause; first aspect given.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fifth objection proposes sensory indication as an alternative determinant for the division of things into adjacent and distinct.

Core Idea
Sensory indication might be the cause of distinctness, not temporal origination.

This passage continues the objections to the proof of huduth. The opponent concedes the principle that absence of finding indicates non-existence, but offers alternative causes. Here, the first aspect explores whether the possibility of sensory indication could be what makes two things distinct in direction, rather than their temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Note the structure: the opponent first concedes the principle, then introduces the first aspect.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; we only have the first aspect.
2
Identify the logical move
The opponent accepts that 'absence of finding proves non-existence' but proposes an alternative cause for the division into adjacent or distinct. What is that alternative?
The key term is 'imkan al-ishara al-hissiyya' (possibility of sensory indication).
3
Analyze the reasoning
The opponent argues: if two things can be pointed to sensorily, the indication to one is different from the indication to the other. This difference is precisely what it means to be distinct in direction. Thus, sensory indication could be the determinant.
Notice that the argument uses 'every two things' — it is a general claim.
