Existence Does Not Necessitate Division
Dialectical refutation of necessity of division by existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that existence necessitates division into contiguous and separate is invalid, and how the response refutes it by pointing to the role of essence-specificity.

Core Idea
Existence alone does not guarantee a judgment; essence can prevent it.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical exchange with an opponent who argues that existence, being common to all things, requires them to be divisible into contiguous and separate categories. The opponent is forced to admit that essence-specific factors can block the judgment, thereby undermining the original claim. Follow the argument step by step to see the refutation unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the dialectical structure become apparent.
Notice the back-and-forth between the opponent and the responder.
2
Identify the opponent's initial claim and consequence
The opponent claims existence is the determinant for division into contiguous and separate. They then infer that substance and accident must be divisible, but division is impossible. Find where this inference is stated.
The opponent uses the phrase 'then existence is equal in all' to generalise.
3
Observe the opponent's modification
The opponent adds a qualification: division is possible in principle based on existence, but prevented by the specificity of essence. Note how they introduce a 'preventer' (mani') and 'specificity of essence' (khususiyyat al-mahiyya).
This modification is crucial—it accepts that essence can block the division.
4
Understand the responder's rebuttal
The responder seizes on the qualification, pointing out that it admits existence does not necessitate the judgment. The existence being a cause does not mean the judgment applies to all existents because essence can prevent.
This refutes the original claim that existence alone forces the division.
5
Reflect on the key insight
The key takeaway is that universal properties (like existence) do not automatically produce the same effect in all things—the specific nature of each thing matters.
This principle applies beyond this specific argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقتضى لقبول الانقسام إلى المجانب وإلى المباين هو الوجود . ثم إن الوجود حاصل بالسوية . فوجب أن ينقسم الجوهر ، إلى ما يكون مجانبا ، وإلى ما يكون مباينا بالجهة . ومعلوم أن انقسام الجوهر إلى هذين القسمين: محال . ولزم أيضاً في العرض أن ينقسم إلى هذين القسمين . ومعلوم أنه محال . فإن قالوا : إن كل جوهر وعرض ، فإنه يصح أن يكون منقسما إلى هذين القسمين، نظرا إلى كونه موجودا. وإنما يمتنع ذلك الإنقسام ، نظرا إلى مانع منع منه ، وهو خصوصية ماهيته. فنقول : فهذا اعتراف بأنه لا يلزم من كون الوجود علة لصحة أمر من الأمور ، أن يصح ذلك الحكم على كل ما كان موصوفا بالوجود ، لاحتمال أن تكون خصوصية تلك الماهية ، ماتعة من ذلك الحكم
The determinant for accepting division into contiguous and separate is existence. Then existence is equal in all. So substance must be divided into what is contiguous and what is separate in direction. And it is known that the division of substance into these two divisions is impossible. And it also follows that accident must be divided into these two divisions. And it is known that it is impossible. If they say: Every substance and accident, it is possible that it be divided into these two divisions, considering its existence. And that division is only prevented due to a preventer from it, which is the specificity of its essence. We say: This is an admission that from existence being a cause for the validity of a certain matter, it does not necessitate that that judgment applies to everything described with existence, because it is possible that the specificity of that essence is a preventer of that judgment.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. First, the opponent claims that existence is the common factor that necessitates division into 'mujanib' (contiguous) and 'mubayin' (separate) for all existing things. They argue that since existence is shared, substance and accident must each be divisible into these two categories, but we know this is impossible. The opponent then modifies their claim: every substance and accident can be considered divisible based on existence, but actual division is prevented by the specificity of each essence. The response seizes on this qualification: it admits that existence alone does not guarantee the judgment, because essence-specific factors may block it. Thus, the argument that existence necessitates the division is invalid.

Key Terms
مجانب
مباين
خصوصية الماهية
علة صحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence is a universal cause that must produce the same effect in all existents.
Correction: Existence can be a cause of validity, but essence-specific factors can prevent the effect from materializing.

Checkpoint

A) Existence is not a cause at all
B) Existence alone does not guarantee the judgment
C) The division is impossible for substance only
D) Specificity of essence is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
Existence alone does not force a specific judgment because the particular nature of a thing can prevent it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Will you now re-examine an area where you felt trapped by a 'necessary' outcome?

The opponent in this passage had to admit that the essence can act as a preventer. What specific step will you take today to remove that preventer?
?
When you hear a definitive religious ruling or statement, do you automatically assume it applies to you in the same way as everyone else, without considering your own unique circumstances? This lesson teaches that existence is not a blanket cause. How will you apply this principle to seek deeper understanding before making judgments about yourself or others?

The Three Ranks of Perception
Al-Razi enumerates the three ranks of perception with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three levels of perception as defined by Al-Razi and how they differ in directness and completeness.

Core Idea
Perception has three ranks: inference, conceptual knowledge, and direct sensation.

Al-Razi begins his discussion of whether God can be seen by first clarifying what 'vision' means. He distinguishes three levels of perceiving something, each stronger than the last. This threefold distinction is crucial for understanding the subsequent theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three ranks
Carefully read Al-Razi's definition of the three ranks of perception. Notice the examples he gives for each.
2
Identify each rank and its example
List the three ranks: first rank (inference from a building to a builder), second rank (conceptual knowledge of color and sound), third rank (direct sensation of color and sound).
The first rank gives only relational knowledge, the second gives essence knowledge, the third adds direct presence.
3
Reflect on the progression
Consider why Al-Razi says each subsequent rank is stronger. What does direct sensation add beyond conceptual knowledge?
Certainty is not the issue; it's about completeness of disclosure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العاشر
في
أنه هل يصح أن نرى واجب الوجود لذاته؟
اعلم : أنه قد عظم الشغب فيه بين المتكلمين . ويجب قبل الخوض فيه تقديم مقدمات ، لا يتم الكلام إلا بتقديمها
المقدمة الأولى : في تفسير الرؤية . فنقول : إدراك الشيء يقع على ثلاث مراتب . فالمرتبة الأولى : إنا إذا رأينا البناء ، علمنا أنه لا بد له من بانٍ وههنا المعلوم ليس إلا كونه بانيا فأما حقيقته المخصوصة ، وذاته المعينة من حيث هي هي، فإنها لا تصير معلومة البتة . والمرتبة الثانية : إنا إذا علمنا الماهية المخصوصة المسماة باللون عن طريق الإبصار ، وعلمنا الماهية المخصوصة المسماة بالصوت عن طريق السماع ، ثم أغمضنا العين ، وسددنا الأذن ، فإنا نكون عالمين بحقيقة اللون من حيث إنه هو ، وبحقيقة الصوت من حيث إنه هو ، ولا شك أن هذه المرتبة أقوى في كونه معلوما متصورا مدركا من المرتبة الأولى . فإنا في المرتبة الأولى ما علمنا الشيء إلا بحسب صفة عرضية من صفاته ، وأما في هذه المرتبة فقد علمنا الماهية المخصوصة من حيث إنها هي ، فهذه المرتبة أقوى في المعلومية وفي المتصورية من المرتبة الأولى . وأما المرتبة الثالثة في الإدراك : فهو أن ننظر بالعين إلى اللون ، وأن نسمع بالأذن ذلك الصوت فإنا حال ما غمضنا العين ، كنا عالمين بماهية اللون المخصوص ، علما لا شك لنا فيه ، ثم لما فتحنا العين ونظرنا إليه حصلت حالة زائدة على الحالة الأولى في الخلاء والكشف. وصريح العقل حاكم بأن هذه المرتبة الثالثة أكمل وأقوى من المرتبة الثانية ، كما أن المرتبة الثانية كانت أقوى وأكمل من المرتبة الأولى .
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا إذا علمنا أن العالم ممكن ، علمنا أن كل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب ، وعلمنا أن الدور والتسلسل باطلان ، فحينئذ نعلم أن هذه الممكنات يجب انتهاؤها إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته . فههنا المعلوم : إثبات موجود ، يستند غيره إليه ولا يستند هو إلى غيره ، والمعلوم ههنا أمور ثلاثة : أحدها : الوجود المطلق . والثاني : وجوب استناد غيره إليه ، وهذا نفس الإضافة . والثالث : امتناع استناده إلى غيره وهذا سلب . فيثبت أن المعلوم ههنا وجود مقيد ، يفيد إضافة ويفيد سلبا ، وكل ذلك مغاير للحقيقة المخصوصة التي لواجب الوجود .
فيثبت : أن الحاصل عندنا من معرفة الله تعالى ليس إلا المرتبة الأولى وأما المرتبة الثانية فهل هي حاصلة لأحد من الخلق ؟ وبتقدير أن لا تكون حاصلة لأحد ، فهل هي ممكنة الحصول للملائكة فقط ، أو لهم وللأنبياء ، أو ممكنة الحصول للكل؟ وهذه المباحث لا بد فيها من البحث. أما المرتبة الثالثة فهل هي ممكنة الحصول لأحد من الخلق أم لا ؟ هذا هو المراد من قولنا : إن رؤية الله تعالى هل هي ممكنة الحصول أم لا ؟ فهذا تمام البحث عن قولنا : إنه هل تصح رؤية الله تعالى أم لا ؟
The Tenth Chapter: On whether it is correct that we see the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) in His essence? Know that great dispute has occurred among the theologians (mutakallimun). It is necessary before entering into it to present preliminaries (muqaddimat), without which the discussion is not complete.
The First Preliminary: On the interpretation of vision (ru'ya). We say: The perception of a thing occurs in three ranks. The first rank: When we see a building, we know that it must have a builder. Here the known is only that it has a builder. As for its specific essence (haqiqatuhu al-makhsusa) and its particular entity (dhatuhu al-mu'ayyana) in itself, it does not become known at all. The second rank: When we know the specific quiddity (mahiyya) called color by way of sight, and we know the specific quiddity called sound by way of hearing, then we close the eye and plug the ear, we are then knowers of the reality of color in that it is it, and of the reality of sound in that it is it. There is no doubt that this rank is stronger in its being known, imaged, and perceived than the first rank. For in the first rank we only knew the thing according to an accidental attribute (sifa 'aradiyya) of its attributes, but in this rank we have known the specific quiddity in that it is it. So this rank is stronger in knowability and imagedness than the first rank. As for the third rank in perception: It is that we look with the eye at color, and we hear with the ear that sound. For when we had closed the eye we were knowers of the specific quiddity of color with knowledge in which there is no doubt, then when we opened the eye and looked at it, an additional state occurred beyond the first state in manifestation and disclosure. Sound reason judges that this third rank is more complete and stronger than the second rank, just as the second rank was stronger and more complete than the first.
When you have known this, we say: When we know that the world is possible (mumkin), we know that every possible must have a cause, and we know that circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) are invalid. Then we know that these possibles must terminate at an existent that is necessary in its essence (Wajib al-Wujud li-dhatihi). So here the known is: the affirmation of an existent to which other than it is dependent and which is not dependent on other than it. And the known here are three things: One: absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq). Two: the necessity of other's dependence on it, and this is the relation (al-idafa) itself. Three: the impossibility of its dependence on other than it, and this is a negation (salb). So it is established that the known here is a qualified existence (wujud muqayyad) that conveys a relation and conveys a negation, and all of that is other than the specific reality (al-haqiqa al-makhsusa) that belongs to the Necessary Existent.
Thus it is established that what we obtain from knowledge of God (Allah ta'ala) is only the first rank. As for the second rank, is it obtained for any of the creatures? And if it is not obtained for anyone, is it possible of attainment for the angels only, or for them and for the prophets, or possible for all? These investigations must be investigated. As for the third rank, is it possible of attainment for any of the creatures or not? This is the meaning of our statement: Is the vision (ru'ya) of God possible of attainment or not? This completes the discussion concerning our statement: Is the vision of God correct or not?
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a definition and application passage. Al-Razi first defines three ranks of perception. He then applies them to knowledge of God and clarifies that our knowledge is only of the first rank, while the possibility of the second and third ranks corresponds to the question of whether God can be seen.

He begins by noting the great dispute among theologians on whether it is possible to see God. He then presents the first preliminary: the interpretation of vision (ru'ya). Perception occurs in three ranks:
1. First rank: When we see a building, we infer it has a builder. We only know a relational attribute (that it has a builder), not the builder's essence. This is the weakest rank.
2. Second rank: We know the essence of a thing by conceptual knowledge without direct sensation, e.g., knowing the nature of color or sound after experiencing them, even when not perceiving them. This is stronger than the first because we know the quiddity itself.
3. Third rank: Direct sense perception, e.g., seeing color with the eye or hearing sound with the ear. This adds a state of manifestation and disclosure beyond mere conceptual knowledge. It is the most complete rank.

Al-Razi then applies this to knowledge of God. Using the cosmological argument (the world is possible, possibles need a cause, no circularity or infinite regress, so there must be a Necessary Existent), he shows that what we know about God is only that He exists, that other things depend on Him, and that He does not depend on others. This is a qualified existence involving relation and negation. This is only the first rank of perception—we know God through His effects, not His essence.

He then raises the question: Can anyone obtain the second rank (knowledge of God's essence without direct vision) or the third rank (direct vision)? This is the real debate about the possibility of seeing God.

Key Terms
الرؤية (ru'ya)
المراتب (maratib)
الإدراك (idrak)
الماهية (mahiyya)
الحقيقة (haqiqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second rank is weaker because it lacks certainty.
Correction: Al-Razi says the second rank is stronger than the first because it knows the essence, not just an attribute. Certainty is not the criterion; directness and completeness are.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, which rank of perception involves knowing the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing without currently sensing it?
A) The first rank
B) The second rank
C) The third rank
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi defines three progressive ranks of perception: inferential (relational), conceptual (essential), and direct sensory (most complete).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you satisfied with knowing Allah only through inferential reasoning from creation (first rank) without striving to know His names and attributes in a deeper, more direct way? What concrete step can you take today to move toward the second rank?
?
Al-Razi describes the third rank as adding disclosure and clarity. In your worship, do you ever experience a sense of being seen by Allah (ihsan) that goes beyond mere conceptual beliefs? If not, are you willing to ask Allah to grant you that direct awareness?
?
When you contemplate the creation, do you stop at inferring a Creator, or do you also ponder His essence and attributes as described in revelation? What one attribute can you meditate on this week to transform your inferential knowledge into essential knowledge?

Our Knowledge of God is Only the First Rank
Al-Razi applies the three ranks to show that our knowledge of God is only the first rank.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi concludes that our rational knowledge of God corresponds only to the first rank of perception.

Core Idea
The cosmological argument gives us relational knowledge of God, not His essence.

Al-Razi now applies the three ranks to theology. He presents the cosmological argument for God's existence and shows that what we know is only that God exists, that others depend on Him, and that He is independent. This is qualified existence, not His specific reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the cosmological argument
Al-Razi argues: the world is possible; every possible needs a cause; no circularity or infinite regress; so there must be a Necessary Existent. Follow this logic.
2
Identify the three known things
Al-Razi says we know three things: absolute existence, the relation of dependents to Him, and the negation of dependence. These are not His essence.
3
Connect to the first rank
Relate this to the first rank of perception: we know God only through His effects, like knowing a builder from a building.
This is the weakest form of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العاشر
في
أنه هل يصح أن نرى واجب الوجود لذاته؟
اعلم : أنه قد عظم الشغب فيه بين المتكلمين . ويجب قبل الخوض فيه تقديم مقدمات ، لا يتم الكلام إلا بتقديمها
المقدمة الأولى : في تفسير الرؤية . فنقول : إدراك الشيء يقع على ثلاث مراتب . فالمرتبة الأولى : إنا إذا رأينا البناء ، علمنا أنه لا بد له من بانٍ وههنا المعلوم ليس إلا كونه بانيا فأما حقيقته المخصوصة ، وذاته المعينة من حيث هي هي، فإنها لا تصير معلومة البتة . والمرتبة الثانية : إنا إذا علمنا الماهية المخصوصة المسماة باللون عن طريق الإبصار ، وعلمنا الماهية المخصوصة المسماة بالصوت عن طريق السماع ، ثم أغمضنا العين ، وسددنا الأذن ، فإنا نكون عالمين بحقيقة اللون من حيث إنه هو ، وبحقيقة الصوت من حيث إنه هو ، ولا شك أن هذه المرتبة أقوى في كونه معلوما متصورا مدركا من المرتبة الأولى . فإنا في المرتبة الأولى ما علمنا الشيء إلا بحسب صفة عرضية من صفاته ، وأما في هذه المرتبة فقد علمنا الماهية المخصوصة من حيث إنها هي ، فهذه المرتبة أقوى في المعلومية وفي المتصورية من المرتبة الأولى . وأما المرتبة الثالثة في الإدراك : فهو أن ننظر بالعين إلى اللون ، وأن نسمع بالأذن ذلك الصوت فإنا حال ما غمضنا العين ، كنا عالمين بماهية اللون المخصوص ، علما لا شك لنا فيه ، ثم لما فتحنا العين ونظرنا إليه حصلت حالة زائدة على الحالة الأولى في الخلاء والكشف. وصريح العقل حاكم بأن هذه المرتبة الثالثة أكمل وأقوى من المرتبة الثانية ، كما أن المرتبة الثانية كانت أقوى وأكمل من المرتبة الأولى .
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : إنا إذا علمنا أن العالم ممكن ، علمنا أن كل ممكن فلا بد له من سبب ، وعلمنا أن الدور والتسلسل باطلان ، فحينئذ نعلم أن هذه الممكنات يجب انتهاؤها إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته . فههنا المعلوم : إثبات موجود ، يستند غيره إليه ولا يستند هو إلى غيره ، والمعلوم ههنا أمور ثلاثة : أحدها : الوجود المطلق . والثاني : وجوب استناد غيره إليه ، وهذا نفس الإضافة . والثالث : امتناع استناده إلى غيره وهذا سلب . فيثبت أن المعلوم ههنا وجود مقيد ، يفيد إضافة ويفيد سلبا ، وكل ذلك مغاير للحقيقة المخصوصة التي لواجب الوجود .
فيثبت : أن الحاصل عندنا من معرفة الله تعالى ليس إلا المرتبة الأولى وأما المرتبة الثانية فهل هي حاصلة لأحد من الخلق ؟ وبتقدير أن لا تكون حاصلة لأحد ، فهل هي ممكنة الحصول للملائكة فقط ، أو لهم وللأنبياء ، أو ممكنة الحصول للكل؟ وهذه المباحث لا بد فيها من البحث. أما المرتبة الثالثة فهل هي ممكنة الحصول لأحد من الخلق أم لا ؟ هذا هو المراد من قولنا : إن رؤية الله تعالى هل هي ممكنة الحصول أم لا ؟ فهذا تمام البحث عن قولنا : إنه هل تصح رؤية الله تعالى أم لا ؟
The Tenth Chapter: On whether it is correct that we see the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) in His essence? Know that great dispute has occurred among the theologians (mutakallimun). It is necessary before entering into it to present preliminaries (muqaddimat), without which the discussion is not complete.
The First Preliminary: On the interpretation of vision (ru'ya). We say: The perception of a thing occurs in three ranks. The first rank: When we see a building, we know that it must have a builder. Here the known is only that it has a builder. As for its specific essence (haqiqatuhu al-makhsusa) and its particular entity (dhatuhu al-mu'ayyana) in itself, it does not become known at all. The second rank: When we know the specific quiddity (mahiyya) called color by way of sight, and we know the specific quiddity called sound by way of hearing, then we close the eye and plug the ear, we are then knowers of the reality of color in that it is it, and of the reality of sound in that it is it. There is no doubt that this rank is stronger in its being known, imaged, and perceived than the first rank. For in the first rank we only knew the thing according to an accidental attribute (sifa 'aradiyya) of its attributes, but in this rank we have known the specific quiddity in that it is it. So this rank is stronger in knowability and imagedness than the first rank. As for the third rank in perception: It is that we look with the eye at color, and we hear with the ear that sound. For when we had closed the eye we were knowers of the specific quiddity of color with knowledge in which there is no doubt, then when we opened the eye and looked at it, an additional state occurred beyond the first state in manifestation and disclosure. Sound reason judges that this third rank is more complete and stronger than the second rank, just as the second rank was stronger and more complete than the first.
When you have known this, we say: When we know that the world is possible (mumkin), we know that every possible must have a cause, and we know that circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul) are invalid. Then we know that these possibles must terminate at an existent that is necessary in its essence (Wajib al-Wujud li-dhatihi). So here the known is: the affirmation of an existent to which other than it is dependent and which is not dependent on other than it. And the known here are three things: One: absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq). Two: the necessity of other's dependence on it, and this is the relation (al-idafa) itself. Three: the impossibility of its dependence on other than it, and this is a negation (salb). So it is established that the known here is a qualified existence (wujud muqayyad) that conveys a relation and conveys a negation, and all of that is other than the specific reality (al-haqiqa al-makhsusa) that belongs to the Necessary Existent.
Thus it is established that what we obtain from knowledge of God (Allah ta'ala) is only the first rank. As for the second rank, is it obtained for any of the creatures? And if it is not obtained for anyone, is it possible of attainment for the angels only, or for them and for the prophets, or possible for all? These investigations must be investigated. As for the third rank, is it possible of attainment for any of the creatures or not? This is the meaning of our statement: Is the vision (ru'ya) of God possible of attainment or not? This completes the discussion concerning our statement: Is the vision of God correct or not?
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a definition and application passage. Al-Razi first defines three ranks of perception. He then applies them to knowledge of God and clarifies that our knowledge is only of the first rank, while the possibility of the second and third ranks corresponds to the question of whether God can be seen.

He begins by noting the great dispute among theologians on whether it is possible to see God. He then presents the first preliminary: the interpretation of vision (ru'ya). Perception occurs in three ranks:
1. First rank: When we see a building, we infer it has a builder. We only know a relational attribute (that it has a builder), not the builder's essence. This is the weakest rank.
2. Second rank: We know the essence of a thing by conceptual knowledge without direct sensation, e.g., knowing the nature of color or sound after experiencing them, even when not perceiving them. This is stronger than the first because we know the quiddity itself.
3. Third rank: Direct sense perception, e.g., seeing color with the eye or hearing sound with the ear. This adds a state of manifestation and disclosure beyond mere conceptual knowledge. It is the most complete rank.

Al-Razi then applies this to knowledge of God. Using the cosmological argument (the world is possible, possibles need a cause, no circularity or infinite regress, so there must be a Necessary Existent), he shows that what we know about God is only that He exists, that other things depend on Him, and that He does not depend on others. This is a qualified existence involving relation and negation. This is only the first rank of perception—we know God through His effects, not His essence.

He then raises the question: Can anyone obtain the second rank (knowledge of God's essence without direct vision) or the third rank (direct vision)? This is the real debate about the possibility of seeing God.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (Wajib al-Wujud)
الدور (dawr)
التسلسل (tasalsul)
الإضافة (idafa)
السلب (salb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The cosmological argument proves God's essence.
Correction: Al-Razi argues it only proves existence, relation, and negation—not the specific reality or essence of God.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi, what are the three things we know from the cosmological argument?
A) God's essence, attributes, and actions
B) Absolute existence, relation, and negation
C) Power, knowledge, and will
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Rational proof of God yields only relational knowledge (first rank), not His essential reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that your knowledge of God is only relational, not essential. Does this humble your intellect or trouble your heart? Will you accept that in this life, you will never comprehend His essence, or will you keep trying to penetrate what is beyond your capacity? Commit to a specific way you will respond when you feel frustrated by the limits of your knowledge.
?
If all your knowledge of God is like seeing a building and inferring a builder, what does that imply about your prayer and supplication? Are you speaking to a being you only know through effects, or do you truly believe He hears you despite the veil? How will you deepen your trust in Him based on this relational knowledge alone?
?
Al-Razi says we know existence, relation, and negation. Which of these three do you most easily forget when you think about God? When you face a trial, do you focus on His existence, His relation to you, or His independence? Which one do you need to meditate on most right now? Write down one concrete change in your daily remembrance that will anchor this truth.

Can We Attain Higher Ranks of Knowledge of God?
Al-Razi raises questions about the possibility of the second and third ranks of perception of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the questions Al-Razi raises about the possibility of attaining the second and third ranks of perception of God.

Core Idea
The debate over seeing God is about whether the second or third rank of perception is possible for creatures.

After establishing that our natural knowledge of God is only the first rank, Al-Razi turns to the crucial theological question: can anyone attain the second rank (conceptual knowledge of God's essence) or the third rank (direct vision of God)? This sets up the controversy over the possibility of seeing God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second rank
Al-Razi asks: Is the second rank (knowledge of essence) possible for any creature? He lists possibilities: only angels, also prophets, or everyone.
2
Read the passage on the third rank
Then he asks: Is direct vision (the third rank) possible? This is the core debate about ru'ya.
3
Understand the stakes
The question is not whether God exists, but whether any creature can perceive His essence directly or conceptually.
This is a major issue between Ash'aris and others.

Is Vision of a Non-Spatial Being Self-Evidently Impossible?
Al-Razi argues that vision of a non-spatial being is not known to be impossible by primary reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's claim that the impossibility of seeing a non-spatial being is not self-evident to primary reason and his initial reasoning involving ranks of knowledge.

Core Idea
Seeing God is not self-evidently impossible; it requires careful reasoning.

Al-Razi continues his preliminaries by addressing a key objection: that it is obviously impossible to see something that has no location. He argues that this is not a self-evident truth (badihah). Instead, we must analyze the concept of vision and the ranks of knowledge. This lesson presents his initial reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the correct statement
Al-Razi states the 'correct statement' about the possibility of seeing a non-spatial being. What is it?
He says it is not known to be impossible by primary reason.
3
Understand the three ranks of knowledge
The passage mentions first-rank, second-rank, and third-rank knowledge. Reflect on how Al-Razi uses these to build his argument.
The ranks are: inferential knowledge (first), conceptual knowledge (second), and direct perception (third).
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
Notice that the passage ends mid-sentence. Consider why Al-Razi raises the question of knowing God in higher ranks while He is transcendent.
The argument continues on the next page.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقدمة الثانية : فهي في بيان أن رؤية الموجود الذي لا يكون مختصا بالمكان أو الحيز ، هل هو معلوم الامتناع في بديهة العقل أم لا ؟ فنقول : القول الصحيح : إن الرؤية بالتفسير الذي ذكرناه ليست معلومة الامتناع في بديهة العقل ، وذلك لأنا في هذه الحياة علمنا الله تعالى علما واقعا في المرتبة الأولى ، وعلمنا أنه منزه عن الجسمية والمكان. فهل يمكن أن نعلم الله تعالى علما واقعا في المرتبة الثانية مع العلم بكونه منزها عن الجسمية والمكان ؟ وإذا ثبت هذا فهل يمكن إدراكه على الوجه الواقع في المرتبة الثالثة ، مع كونه منزها عن الجسمية
And as for the Second Preliminary: It is about explaining whether the vision of the existent that is not specific to place or location is known to be impossible in the primary reason (badihah al-'aql) or not? We say: The correct statement: Vision, according to the interpretation we mentioned, is not known to be impossible in the primary reason. That is because in this life we have known God with knowledge that falls in the first rank, and we know that He is transcendent (munazzah) from corporeality and place. So is it possible that we know God with knowledge that falls in the second rank while knowing that He is transcendent from corporeality and place? And if this is established, then is it possible to perceive Him in the manner that falls in the third rank, while He is transcendent from corporeality...
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage introduces the second preliminary: whether it is self-evidently impossible to see a being that is not in a place or direction. Al-Razi states the correct opinion: vision, as interpreted in the first preliminary, is not known to be impossible by primary reason (badihah al-'aql). He begins his reasoning: we already know God in this life with first-rank knowledge (i.e., inferentially), and we know that He is transcendent (munazzah) from corporeality and place. The question then is whether we can know God with second-rank knowledge (conceptual) while knowing He is transcendent, and if so, whether third-rank perception (direct vision) is possible while He is transcendent. The argument is left incomplete here.

الجسمية (jismiyya)
المكان (makan)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of seeing God is self-evident simply because God has no place or direction.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that this impossibility is not self-evident; it requires further rational demonstration.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi state as the correct opinion regarding the possibility of seeing a non-spatial being?
A) It is known to be impossible by primary reason
B) It is not known to be impossible by primary reason
C) It is impossible because God is not in a place
D) The question is meaningless

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi argues that the impossibility of seeing a non-spatial being is not known by primary reason; it requires further argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a possibility about God (such as seeing Him) simply because it seemed impossible to you, without carefully examining the evidence? What does that reveal about your willingness to submit your assumptions to deeper reasoning? Will you commit to not letting primary reason alone settle matters of God's attributes?
?
Al-Razi says we can have first-rank knowledge of God (knowing Him through creation) while knowing He is non-spatial. But is your knowledge of God limited to that first rank? Do you actively seek second-rank (conceptual) and third-rank (direct) knowledge, or are you content with a distant, inferential relationship? What step can you take today to move from simply knowing about God to truly knowing Him?
?
If the impossibility of seeing God is not self-evident, then your intellect is not the final judge of what God can or cannot do. When you read the Quranic verses about believers seeing their Lord in the afterlife, do you accept them as a hopeful promise, or do you allow your rational assumptions to rob you of that hope? How will you actively train your heart to trust revelation over your own limited reasoning?

The First Proof: If God Could Be Seen, We Would See Him Now
Razi presents the first proof against the possibility of seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of Razi's first argument against the possibility of seeing God, based on the necessary conditions for vision and their inapplicability to God.

Core Idea
If God could be seen, we would see Him now — but we don't, so He cannot be seen.

Razi's first argument for the impossibility of seeing God is a powerful reductio ad absurdum. He starts by establishing a general principle about vision: when all eight necessary conditions are present, vision must occur. Then he shows that only two of those conditions could ever apply to God, since the others involve physical limitations. Since those two conditions are always present for us, if God were visible, we would see Him constantly. But we don't. The only explanation, says Razi, is that God is not the kind of being that can be seen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice how Razi uses a conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage has two main sections: first, the establishment of a general rule about vision (when eight conditions are met, vision is necessary); second, the application of that rule to God, concluding that vision is impossible.
3
Understand the eight conditions
Razi mentions eight conditions for vision, but only names two: sound senses and the object being visible. The other six involve physicality (body, place, direction). Note why these cannot apply to God.
The six physical conditions are not listed separately, but Razi implies they relate to bodies in space.
4
Follow the reductio
Trace the logic: If God's vision were possible, only two conditions are needed; those two are always present; so we would see God now. But we don't. Therefore, God's vision is impossible by essence.
This is a classic 'reductio ad absurdum' — assuming the opposite leads to contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : عند حصول هذه الشرائط الثمانية يجب الإبصار ، إذ لو لم يجب لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا تلال وبوقات ونحن لا نراها ولا نسمعها . وذلك يفضي إلى السفسطة . فيثبت أن عند حصول هذه الشرائط الثمانية يجب الإبصار. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لو كانت رؤية الله جائزة لما اعتبر في حصولها إلا الشرطان الأولان ، أعني كون الحاسة سليمة ، وكون المرئي بحيث تصح رؤيته . فأما الشرائط الستة الباقية فإنه يمتنع اعتبارها في حق الله تعالى ، لأنه إنما يمكن اعتبارها بالنسبة إلى الجسم ، وإلى الشيء الذي يكون حاصلا في الحيز والجهة ، والباري تعالى مقدس عن هذه الحالة، فيثبت أنه بتقدير أن تجوز رؤية الله تعالى ، فإن الشرائط الست لا يمكن اعتبارها في حق الله تعالى ، فلم يبق في حصول رؤية الله إلا سلامة الحواس ، وكون المرئي بحيث تصح رؤيته . وهذا أن الشرطان حاصلان في الحال ، فكان يجب أن نراه الآن ، فيثبت بما ذكرناه : أنه لو صحت رؤيته لوجب أن تراه الآن ، ومعلوم أنا لا نراه الآن ، فوجب أن يقال : إنما لا نراه الآن لكونه في نفسه ، بحيث تمتنع رؤيته ، وذلك هو المطلوب
If you know this, we say: When these eight conditions are present, vision is necessary, because if it were not necessary, it would be possible that there are hills and trumpets before us and we do not see them nor hear them. And that leads to sophistry. So it is established that when these eight conditions are present, vision is necessary. If you know this, we say: If the vision of God were permissible, then for its occurrence nothing would be considered except the first two conditions, namely that the sense is sound and that the visible object is such that its vision is valid. As for the remaining six conditions, it is impossible to consider them with respect to God the Exalted, because they can only be considered with respect to a body and with respect to something that exists in place and direction, and the Creator the Exalted is sanctified from this state. So it is established that, supposing the vision of God the Exalted is permissible, the six conditions cannot be considered with respect to God the Exalted. So nothing remains for the occurrence of the vision of God except soundness of the senses and the visible object being such that its vision is valid. And these two conditions are present at the moment, so we ought to see Him now. So it is established by what we have mentioned that if His vision were valid, we would be obligated to see Him now, and it is known that we do not see Him now. So it must be said that we do not see Him now only because He is in Himself such that His vision is impossible. And that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the impossibility of seeing God. The author begins by establishing a general principle: when the eight conditions for vision are met, vision is necessary. Otherwise, absurd consequences like failing to see obvious objects would lead to sophistry (safsata). Then he applies this to God: if God's vision were possible, only two of the eight conditions could apply to God – a sound sense and the object being visible – because the other six involve physicality (body, place, direction), which God transcends. Since these two conditions are always present (we have sound senses and God is visible in principle), we should see God now. But we do not see Him now. Therefore, the impossibility must be due to God's own nature: He is not the kind of being that can be seen. The proof relies on a reductio ad absurdum: if vision were possible, it would be necessary now; but it is not, so it is impossible.

Key Terms
الشرائط الثمانية
الإبصار
السفسطة
الحاسة
المرئي
الحيز
الجهة
الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument aims to prove that God cannot be seen under any circumstances, even in the afterlife.
Correction: The argument only proves that if vision were possible, it would be necessary now, but since we don't see God now, the impossibility is due to God's essence. It does not address whether God could grant vision in the afterlife; that is a separate discussion.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, how many of the eight conditions for vision are possible for God?
A) Eight
B) Two
C) Six
D) None

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof shows that if God were visible, we would see Him now; since we do not, His essence makes vision impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is invisible by essence, does your heart's awe reflect that He is beyond all physical perception? Or do you unconsciously try to limit Him to what you can imagine? This lesson forces you to confess that you cannot see God now. How does that change the way you approach Him in prayer? Are you willing to worship a God you cannot picture?
?
Razi's argument says that if vision of God were possible, it would have already happened. You know it hasn't. Yet do you live as though God is distant because He is unseen, or does His invisibility make His presence more real to you? When you struggle with doubt, what would it mean to accept that God's transcendence is the very reason you cannot see Him, and that this is a mercy, not a proof of absence?
?
The proof hinges on the fact that we do not see God now. But consider: are there 'gods' you do see—wealth, status, approval—that you worship instead? This lesson calls you to account: if you truly believed that the only real God is utterly beyond sight, would you still chase things that are visible as if they were ultimate? What visible idol do you need to turn away from right now?

The Flour Objection: Why Conditions Do Not Necessitate Vision
Al-Razi critiques the first proof by arguing that even with conditions, vision is not necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's critique of the claim that the eight conditions necessitate vision, through the flour atom example and the logical options he presents.

Core Idea
Conditions do not force vision; seeing the whole does not entail seeing its parts.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi launches his first objection to the classic proof for the necessity of vision. He uses a clever counterexample: a pile of flour. Although we see the pile, we do not see its constituent atoms. By analyzing the logical possibilities of how we perceive parts and wholes, he demonstrates that even if all eight conditions for vision are met, actual vision is not necessitated. This challenges the very foundation of the first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal English translation. Note the structure: a denial, evidence, then three logical divisions.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the three logical possibilities
The objector presents three options for the relationship between seeing individual atoms. Identify them: (1) circular dependence, (2) one-way dependence, (3) independence.
These correspond to dawr, tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ, and the true option.
3
Understand why two are impossible
Option 1 leads to circularity (dawr), impossible. Option 2 leads to preponderance without a preponderant (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ), also impossible. Grasp why each is ruled out.
The third option is then the only remaining possibility.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The third option implies each atom should be visible in isolation, yet we do not see isolated atoms. Therefore, even with all conditions, vision is not necessary. This refutes the original claim.
The conclusion is that the presence of the eight conditions does not guarantee vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن هذه الوجوه خسيسة ضعيفة جدا أما الوجه الأول فالاعتراض عليه من وجوه : الأول : إنا لا نسلم أن عند حصول الشرائط الثمانية يجب حصول الابصار ، ويدل عليه وجوه الأول : إنا إذا وضعنا على الطبق مناً من الدقيق ، فإنا نراه ، ونقول : ذلك الدقيق عبارة عن تلك الذرات الصغيرة المتلاصقة . فرؤية الدقيق عبارة عن رؤية مجموع تلك الذرات ، ورؤية ذلك المجموع عبارة عن رؤية كل واحد من تلك الذرات مع الأخرى ، ونقول : إما أن تكون رؤية كل واحدة من تلك الذرات موقوفة على رؤية الذرة الأخرى وحينئذ يلزم منه الدور ، وإما أن تتوقف رؤية إحدى الذرتين على رؤية الذرة الأخرى ، ولا تتوقف رؤية الذرة الثانية على رؤية الذرة الأولى ، وذلك محال . لأن تلك الذرات متساوية في تمام الماهية ، فتوقيف أحد الجانبين على الجانب الآخر من غير عكس ، يكون حكما بترجيح الممكن من غير مرجح. وهو محال. وإما أن يكون الحق هو أن رؤية كل واحدة من تلك الذرات غير موقوفة على رؤية الذرة الأخرى ، وإذا كان الحق هو هذا القسم وجب الحكم بأنه تصح رؤية كل واحدة منهما حال انفرادها عن كل ما سواها مع أنا لا نراها ، وذلك يوجب القطع بأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء صحيح الرؤية ، مع سائر الشرائط : وجوب رؤيته .
Know that these arguments are base and very weak. As for the first argument, the objection to it is from several aspects: The first: We do not concede that when the eight conditions are present, vision is necessary. Evidence for it: first, if we place a mann of flour on a plate, we see it, and we say: that flour is composed of those small contiguous atoms. The vision of the flour is the vision of the sum of those atoms, and the vision of that sum is the vision of each one of those atoms together with the others. We say: either the vision of each one of those atoms is dependent on the vision of the other atom, in which case a circularity (dawr) follows; or the vision of one of the two atoms depends on the vision of the other atom but the vision of the second atom does not depend on the vision of the first, and that is impossible, because those atoms are equal in complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya), so making one side dependent on the other without reciprocity would be a judgment of giving preponderance to a possible without a preponderant, which is impossible; or the truth is that the vision of each one of those atoms is not dependent on the vision of the other atom. If the truth is this division, then it must be judged that each one of them can be seen when isolated from everything else, yet we do not see them. That leads to the decisive conclusion that it does not follow from a thing being valid for vision, along with all conditions, that its vision is necessary.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is the first objection to the first proof. The objector begins by declaring the four arguments base and weak. The first specific objection targets the necessity claim: he denies that the eight conditions necessitate vision. He gives a counterexample with flour. When we see a pile of flour, we see it as a whole, but we do not see the individual atoms. He analyzes the logical possibilities for the relationship between seeing the atoms: either seeing each atom depends on seeing the other (circular), or one depends on the other without reciprocity (unjustified preference), or they are independent. The first two are impossible. The third (independent) would imply each atom is visible in isolation, but we do not see an isolated atom. Therefore, even if all conditions are met, vision is not necessary. This undermines the first proof's premise that the eight conditions guarantee vision.

Key Terms
الشرائط الثمانية
الدقيق
الذرات
المتلاصقة
الدور
الترجيح بلا مرجح
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The flour objection argues that God cannot be seen at all.
Correction: The objection only refutes the claim that the eight conditions necessitate vision. It does not deny the possibility of vision altogether.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does the objector draw from the flour example?
A) God cannot be seen
B) Conditions do not necessitate vision
C) Atoms are invisible
D) Flour is not truly seen

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's flour example shows that the presence of all conditions for vision does not guarantee that vision actually occurs; the first proof's necessity claim is thus undermined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This passage shows that even if all conditions are present, vision is not necessary. What does that teach you about God's absolute freedom? Are you trying to 'force' or 'demand' His intervention by meeting conditions, or do you trust His will beyond your efforts?
?
We often see the 'whole' (the flour) but ignore the 'parts' (individual atoms). In your spiritual life, what 'wholes' do you focus on while neglecting the 'parts' — the details of your character, hidden sins, or small acts of obedience? How might that affect your relationship with God?

The Essence Difference Objection
Al-Razi objects that the necessity of seeing bodies does not transfer to God due to essence difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's objection that the necessity of seeing bodies does not transfer to God because the essence of seeing God differs from seeing bodies.

Core Idea
A ruling for one essence does not transfer to another differing essence.

Al-Razi concedes the first proof that eight conditions necessitate vision for bodies and accidents. But he raises a powerful objection: the vision of God, if possible, would be different in essence from the vision of bodies. Therefore, the necessity that applies to bodies cannot be assumed to apply to God. This lesson unpacks this logical move.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concession
Notice that the objector begins by conceding the necessity for bodies and accidents when conditions are met. This is not a denial of the premises, but a challenge to the conclusion.
The objector grants (sallamnā) the necessity for bodies.
2
Grasp the essence-difference argument
The key claim: the vision of God, if permissible, would differ in essence (māhiyya) from the vision of bodies. A ruling established for one essence does not automatically apply to a different essence. Hence, the necessity of seeing bodies does not imply the necessity of seeing God.
Essence difference is the core of the objection.
3
See the strength of the objection
Al-Razi himself calls this a 'manifestly strong question' (suhāl ẓāhir qawī). This signals that the objection is not weak and must be addressed seriously.
The phrase 'wa anā shadīd' might indicate the author's endorsement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا هذا الوجوب في رؤية الأجسام والأعراض ، فلم قلتم : بأنه لما وجبت رؤية الأجسام والأعراض عند اجتماع هذه الشرائط الثمانية ، وجب حصول رؤية الله تعالى عند اجتماعها ؟ وبيانه : أن بتقدير جواز رؤية الله تعالى ، فإن رؤيته تكون مخالفة بالماهية لرؤية الأجسام والأعراض ، ولا يلزم من ثبوت حكم في ماهية ، ثبوت مثل ذلك الحكم فيما يخالف تلك الماهية ، فعلى هذا لا يلزم من وجوب رؤية الأجسام والأعراض عند اجتماع هذه الشرائط ، وجوب رؤية الله تعالى عند اجتماع هذه الشرائط . وهذا سؤال ظاهر قوي ، وأنا شديد (٣)
The second question: We concede this necessity in the vision of bodies and accidents. But why do you say that because the vision of bodies and accidents is necessary when these eight conditions are met, then the occurrence of the vision of God the Exalted is necessary when they are met? Its explanation: Supposing the permissibility of the vision of God the Exalted, His vision differs in essence (māhiyya) from the vision of bodies and accidents. And it does not follow from the establishment of a ruling in one essence the establishment of a similar ruling in what differs from that essence. Therefore, it does not follow from the necessity of the vision of bodies and accidents when these conditions are met the necessity of the vision of God the Exalted when these conditions are met. And this is a manifestly strong question, and I am severe (shaḍīd).
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection to the first proof. The objector grants (sallamnā) that for bodies and accidents, the eight conditions necessitate vision. However, he challenges the transfer of this necessity to God. His reasoning: the vision of God, if permissible, would be different in essence (māhiyya) from the vision of bodies and accidents. A ruling established for one essence does not necessarily apply to a different essence. Therefore, the necessity of seeing bodies when conditions are met does not imply the necessity of seeing God when conditions are met. The objector calls this a strong question. The phrase 'I am severe' likely indicates the author's strong endorsement of this objection.

Key Terms
الوجوب
الأجسام
الأعراض
الماهية
الحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objector denies the necessity for bodies altogether.
Correction: The objector actually concedes the necessity for bodies; he only denies its transfer to God based on essence difference.

Checkpoint
Why does the objector argue that the necessity of seeing bodies does not apply to seeing God?
A) Because God is not a body
B) Because the eight conditions are not met for God
C) Because the vision of God differs in essence from the vision of bodies
D) Because we do not see God now

Key Takeaway
The difference in essence between the vision of bodies and the vision of God prevents transferring the ruling of necessity from one to the other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God, do you ever assume that what is true for created things (like needing conditions to be seen) must also be true for Him? How does this objection challenge that assumption, and what in your life is affected by this?
?
In your own reasoning about God, are you sometimes led by analogies from your experience? Reflect on a time you might have transferred a limitation of creation to the Creator. What can you do to guard against this?
?
Al-Razi concedes the necessity for bodies but denies transfer to God. Are there areas in your faith where you cling to general principles without considering God's unique essence? How can you humbly accept that God's ways are not like ours?

The Third Objection: Conditions for Complete Disclosure
Presents the third objection against the first proof for vision, focusing on conditions for complete disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection against the first argument for vision of God uses the distinction between physical and immaterial entities to challenge the necessity of vision.

Core Idea
Complete disclosure is not universal; it depends on conditions that differ for physical and spiritual beings.

In this passage, the author expresses astonishment that opponents overlooked a crucial point: the third rank of 'complete disclosure' applies differently to physical and immaterial entities. For physical objects, it is automatic when senses are sound, but for God, it requires specific conditions not present in this worldly state. This objection, if plausible, undermines the entire argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the author's tone of astonishment.
The passage begins with 'التعجب من أن القوم كيف غفلوا عنه'.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The objection distinguishes between corporeal entities (حسوسات وجسمانيات) and immaterial entities (مجردات ومفارقات). Note the different conditions for each.
For corporeal, vision is necessary when senses are sound; for immaterial, it requires specific conditions.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the conditions are absent in this state, vision of God does not occur, and the inference from the first argument is invalidated.
The author concludes 'وهذا محتمل، ومع تمام هذا الاحتمال يسقط هذا الاستدلال'.
4
Reflect on the objection's strength
Consider why this objection is considered a serious challenge to the first proof for vision. Does it prove impossibility or just lack of necessity?
The objection shows the argument is not conclusive, not necessarily that vision is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعجب من أن القوم كيف غفلوا عنه. والسؤال الثالث: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: هذه المرتبة الثالثة التي هي الانكشاف التام، إن حصلت في حق الحسوسات والجسمانيات، فإنها تكون واجبة الحصول عند سلامة الحاسة الظاهرة، وإن اعتبرت في حق المجردات والمفارقات، فإن تلك الحالة إنما تحصل لجوهر النفس القدسية، وحصول تلك الحالة للجوهر النفس القدسية، مشروط بشرائط هي فائتة في هذه الحالة، ولأجل فواتها لم تحصل هذه الرؤية، وهذا محتمل، ومع تمام هذا الاحتمال يسقط هذا الاستدلال. فهذا تمام الكلام على الحجة الأولى.
Astonishment that the people how they were heedless of it. And the third question: Why is it not permissible to say: This third rank which is complete disclosure, if it occurs with respect to sensed things and corporeal things, then it is necessarily obtained when the external sense is sound, and if it is considered with respect to immaterial and separate entities, then that state is only obtained for the substance of the holy soul, and the obtaining of that state for the substance of the holy soul is conditioned by conditions that are absent in this state, and due to their absence this vision is not obtained, and this is probable, and with the completeness of this probability this inference is invalidated. So this completes the speech on the first proof.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection against the first argument for the vision of God. The author expresses astonishment that opponents overlooked this point. The objection states: The third rank (complete disclosure) is necessarily obtained for physical objects when the senses are sound, but for immaterial entities (e.g., God), it depends on the holy soul's substance and specific conditions that do not exist in this worldly state. Since the objection is plausible, it destroys the argument. The author concludes the refutation of the first proof.

Correction: The objection shows that analogy fails because immaterial entities require different conditions that are not present, so the argument does not prove vision must occur.

Weakness of Both Proofs and the Conclusion of Suspension
The author concludes the ru'ya debate by declaring both proofs weak and leaning toward acceptance based on reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author concludes that the debate on seeing Allah remains suspended and how prophetic and mystical reports strengthen conjecture without achieving certainty.

Core Idea
Both sides' proofs fail, so we suspend judgment—except reports tip the scale toward possibility.

In this concluding passage, the author steps back and assesses the arguments from both sides of the ru'ya debate. He finds them all wanting, yet he does not leave us in complete doubt. Let's see how he navigates between rational weakness and transmitted evidence, ultimately leaning toward acceptance while acknowledging that only Allah knows the full reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's final verdict
Begin by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's decisive language: he calls the negators' proofs 'in extreme weakness and collapse' and says the affirmers' proof 'has strong objections that cannot be repelled.'
The passage is a conclusion, not a detailed argument.
2
Identify the two groups and their fate
The author evaluates two groups: the negators (النفاة) and the affirmers (المثبتون). For each, he declares their proofs weak. Understand why he says the matter should be suspended (التوقف).
3
Explore the meaning of tawaqquf
Suspension (tawaqquf) is a methodological stance where one refrains from judgment due to insufficient evidence. The author explicitly says 'this research remains in the place of suspension' after showing both arguments fail.
Tawaqquf is not agnosticism but a temporary holding position.
4
Consider the role of prophetic and mystical reports
The author then introduces a different type of evidence: reports from prophets and people of unveiling (mukashafat). These strengthen conjecture (zann) about the occurrence of ru'ya, even though rational proof is lacking.
Conjecture (zann) is a degree of belief below certainty but above doubt.
5
Reflect on the final acknowledgment
The passage ends with a humble note: 'the realities of things are not known in their entirety except by Allah the Wise.' This reminds us of the limits of human reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فظهر بهذه البيانات: أن هذه الدلائل التي تمسك بها نفاة الرؤية في غاية الضعف والسقوط وأما مثبتو الرؤية فقد عولوا على أن قالوا : الله تعالى موجود ، وكل موجود فإنه تصح رؤيته ودليلهم في الإثبات : أن كل موجود تصح رؤيته : قد ذكرناه في أحكام الموجودات ، وأوردنا عليه اعتراضات قوية ، لا يمكن دفعها البتة وإذا عرفت ضعف دلائل الفريقين فنقول : بقي هذا البحث في محل التوقف ، إلا أنا رأينا الأنبياء والرسل عليهم السلام تخبرين عن حصول هذه الرؤية ، ورأينا أصحاب المكاشفات يخبرون عن وقوع أحوال كأنها جارية مجرى المقدمات لهذه الرؤية ، فقوي الظن في جواز وقوعها وحقائق الأشياء لا يعرفها بتمامها إلا الله الحكيم
So it became clear from these data that these proofs which the negators of the vision held onto are in extreme weakness and collapse. As for the affirmers of the vision, they relied on saying: Allah Almighty exists, and every existent can be seen. Their proof in affirmation: that every existent can be seen: we have mentioned it in the judgments of existents, and we have raised against it strong objections that cannot be repelled at all. And when you know the weakness of the proofs of the two groups, we say: this research remains in the place of suspension, except that we have seen the prophets and messengers, peace be upon them, inform about the occurrence of this vision, and we have seen the people of unveilings (mukashafat) inform about the occurrence of states that seem to be in the position of premises for this vision. So strong became the conjecture (zann) about its permissibility and occurrence. And the realities of things are not known in their entirety except by Allah the Wise.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents the author's final judgment on the debate about seeing Allah (ru'ya). He first refutes the arguments of those who deny the possibility of ru'ya, finding them extremely weak. Then he examines the proof of those who affirm ru'ya, which is based on the premise that every existent can be seen. The author mentions that he previously discussed this proof and raised strong objections that cannot be refuted. Thus both sides' arguments are flawed. He concludes that the matter should remain suspended (tawaqquf). However, he then adds that he has seen reports from prophets and messengers that inform of the occurrence of ru'ya, and from mystics (ahl al-mukashafat) who describe states that seem to be premises leading to ru'ya. This strengthens the conjecture (zann) that ru'ya is permissible and will occur. He ends by acknowledging that ultimate reality is known only to Allah.

Correction: The author only says conjecture (zann) is strengthened, not certainty. He maintains that the matter is still subject to the limitation that full knowledge belongs to Allah.

Al-Razi's Four Categories and the Inconceivability of God's Essence
Al-Razi classifies concepts into four types and argues that God's essence falls outside them, thus inconceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's classification of all human concepts into four categories and how he argues that God's essence cannot be conceptualized because it falls outside all of them.

Core Idea
God's essence is inconceivable because it does not fall into any of the four types of human conceptualization.

In this second proof, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a systematic classification of all possible human concepts. He then applies this classification to argue that the divine essence is beyond conceptualization. This argument is central to the Ash'ari emphasis on tanzih (transcendence) and the limits of human reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Thoroughly
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Pay attention to the structure of the argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the Four Categories
Al-Razi lists four ways we conceptualize things. Find them in the text and note their descriptions.
The categories are from senses, inner feelings, pure intellect, and composition.
3
Understand the Objection and Response
Read the part where an imaginary opponent brings up 'partner of God'. See how Al-Razi responds by showing it is a composite concept.
This shows that even abstract theological terms are derived from composition.
4
Apply the Argument to God's Essence
Realize that God's essence does not fit any of the four categories. Conclude that it cannot be conceptualized.
This is the main point of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحجة الثانية في إثبات هذا المطلوب : أن نقول : العلم إما تصور وإما تصديق ، والتصديق هو الحكم على أحد المتصورين، بإثباته للمتصور الآخر ، أو سلبه عنه . وهو فرع على التصور. وأما التصورات فإنه لا يمكننا أن نتصور شيئا إلا على أحد وجوه أربعة : أحدها : التصورات التي أدركناها وتصورناها بواسطة الحواس الخمس ، مثل تصورنا الحقيقة السواد والبياض ، ومثل تصورنا الحقيقة الصوت والحرف، وكذلك القول في ما سائر التصورات المستفادة من الحواس الخمسة . وثانيها : التصورات التي أدركناها من وجدانات النفوس ، مثل علمنا بحقيقة الألم واللذة والشهوة والنفرة والفرح والغم . وغيرها . وثالثها : التصورات التي تدركها بمحض العقل مثل تصوراتنا لمعنى الوجود والعدم والوحدة والكثرة والوجوب والامتناع والإمكان . ورابعها : التصورات التي يركبها الخيال والعقل من تلك المدركات البسيطة ، أما تركيبات الخيال فمثل إنسان له ألف رأس ، وذلك لأنا إذا أدركنا صورة الإنسان وصورة الرأس بحواسنا وأدركنا معنى الإنسان بعقولنا ، فالخيال يركب إنسانا له ألف رأس، لأن هذه التصورات كانت حاضرة عند الإنسان، فالخيال ركب بعضها مع البعض . وأما تركيبات العقل فمثل تركيب أحد التصورين بالآخر حتى تتركب منهما مقدمة، وتركيب إحدى المقدمتين بالأخرى حتى يتركب منهما قياسا . إذا عرفت هذه الأقسام الأربعة من التصورات . فنقول : الإنسان لا يمكنه أن يستحضر نوعا من أنواع التصورات، إلا على أحد هذه الأقسام الأربعة ، فأما ما يكون مغايرا لهذه الأقسام الأربعة فإنه لا يمكنه أن يستحضر تصورها البتة . والدليل عليه : أنا إذا رجعنا إلى انفسنا واعتبرنا أحوال إدراكاتنا وتعقلاتنا ، علمنا أنه لا يمكننا أن نستحضر شيئا من التصورات إلا على أحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة. فإن قالوا : إنا نحكم بأن شريك الإله ممتنع ، ولولا أنا تصورنا معنى شريك الإله ، وإلا لما أمكننا أن نحكم عليه بالامتناع، فيثبت أن معنى شريك الإله : متصور لنا ، مع أنه خارج عن الأقسام الأربعة المذكورة . فنقول : بل هذا من باب تركيب العقل ، وذلك لأنه حصل عنده معنى الشريك ، حيث وجد . وحصل عنده معنى الإله . فنقول عند هذا : حصول شيء : الله تعالى نسبته إليه كنسبة أحد الشريكين إلى الآخر فيما بيننا : محال . فيثبت أن هذا من باب التصورات المركبة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : حقيقة الإله : ما وجدناها بحواسنا ، ولا من القسم الثاني وهو الوجدانيات النفسانية ، ولا من التصورات العقلية مثل الوجود والعدم ، ولا من القسم الرابع وهو التصورات المركبة من تلك الأقسام الثلاثة . ولما كانت الحقيقة المخصوصة التي هي ذات الله مخالفة لهذه الأقسام الأربعة وثبت بالاستقراء أنه لا يمكننا تحصيل شيء من التصورات، إلا على أحد تلك الأقسام الأربعة ، وجب الجزم بأن تصور حقيقة تلك الذات المخصوصة غير حاصل للبشر.
And the second proof in establishing this desired [conclusion] is: let us say: knowledge is either conceptualization (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). Assent is the judgment about one of the two conceptualized things, by affirming it to the other conceptualized thing, or negating it from it. And it is dependent on conceptualization. As for conceptualizations, we cannot conceptualize anything except in one of four ways: The first: the conceptualizations that we have perceived and conceptualized by means of the five senses, such as our conceptualization of the reality of blackness and whiteness, and such as our conceptualization of the reality of sound and letter, and the same applies to the rest of the conceptualizations derived from the five senses. The second: the conceptualizations that we have perceived from the inner feelings of the souls, such as our knowledge of the reality of pain, pleasure, appetite, aversion, joy, and grief, and others. The third: the conceptualizations that are perceived by pure intellect, such as our conceptualizations of the meaning of existence, non-existence, unity, multiplicity, necessity, impossibility, and possibility. The fourth: the conceptualizations that the imagination and the intellect compose from those simple perceptions. As for the compositions of the imagination, such as a human with a thousand heads. This is because when we have perceived the image of a human and the image of a head by our senses, and we have perceived the meaning of a human by our intellects, the imagination composes a human with a thousand heads, because these conceptualizations were present with the human, so the imagination composed some of them with others. As for the compositions of the intellect, such as composing one of the two conceptualizations with the other until they compose a premise, and composing one of the two premises with the other until they compose a syllogism. Now that you have recognized these four categories of conceptualizations, let us say: a human being cannot bring to mind any type of conceptualizations except in one of these four categories. As for what is different from these four categories, he cannot bring to mind its conceptualization at all. The proof for it: when we turn to ourselves and consider the states of our perceptions and understandings, we know that we cannot bring to mind any conceptualization except in one of these four ways. Now if they say: we judge that the partner of God (sharik al-ilah) is impossible, and were it not that we have conceptualized the meaning of partner of God, otherwise we could not have judged it as impossible, so it is established that the meaning of partner of God is conceptualized by us, despite it being outside the mentioned four categories. We say: Rather, this falls under the composition of the intellect. This is because he has obtained the meaning of partner, wherever it is found, and he has obtained the meaning of God. Thus we say here: the obtaining of a thing such that its relation to God Almighty is like the relation of one partner to another among us is impossible. So it is established that this falls under composite conceptualizations. And when this is established, we say: the reality of the God: we have not found it by our senses, nor from the second category, which is the psychological inner feelings, nor from the intellectual conceptualizations like existence and non-existence, nor from the fourth category, which is the conceptualizations composed from those three categories. And since the particular reality which is the essence of God is contrary to these four categories, and it is established by induction that we cannot obtain any conceptualization except in one of those four categories, it is necessary to be certain that the conceptualization of the reality of that particular essence is not obtained for humans.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents the second and more detailed proof that God's essence cannot be conceived. It begins by dividing knowledge into two categories: conceptualization (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq). Assent is a judgment about two conceptualized items, and it depends on conceptualization. The focus is then on conceptualization, which the author argues can only occur in four ways: (1) from the five senses (e.g., blackness, sound); (2) from inner feelings (e.g., pain, joy); (3) from pure intellect (e.g., existence, unity); (4) from composition by imagination or intellect (e.g., a human with a thousand heads, or a syllogism). The key point is that any concept must fall into one of these four categories. Then the author applies this to the divine essence: God's essence is not perceived by senses, not an inner feeling, not a purely intellectual concept (since existence etc. are not the essence itself), and not a composition. Therefore, it cannot be conceptualized. An objection is raised: people still speak of 'partner of God' (sharik al-ilah) and judge it impossible, implying they have a concept of it, even though it seems outside the four categories. The answer is that this concept is a composition of the intellect: we already have concepts of 'partner' and 'God', and we combine them; thus it falls under the fourth category. The conclusion is firm: the reality of God's essence is not obtainable by any of these four means, and since all human concepts are limited to these four, we must affirm that we cannot conceive God's essence.

تصديق
الحواس الخمس
وجدانات النفوس
تركيبات الخيال
تركيبات العقل
مقدمة
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we can speak about 'partner of God' and judge it impossible, then we must have a concept of God's essence.
Correction: Al-Razi explains that 'partner of God' is a composite concept derived from separate concepts of 'partner' and 'God', so it does not require a direct concept of God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, into how many categories are all human conceptualizations (tasawwurat) classified?
A) Two: sensory and intellectual.
B) Three: sensory, inner, and intellectual.
C) Four: sensory, inner feelings, pure intellectual, and composite.
D) Five: sensory, inner, intellectual, imaginative, and rational.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's four-category classification shows that God's essence is beyond all human conceptualization, affirming the principle of tanzih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi says we cannot conceptualize God's essence. When you say 'I know God,' what is that knowledge based on? Is it possible that you have substituted a composite image or feeling for the reality? What would change in your worship if you truly internalized that His essence is beyond all conception?
?
The passage categorizes all human concepts into four types. Which type do you rely on most in your thoughts about God? Could that be limiting your understanding? How can you practice tanzih in your daily reflections without falling into the trap of assuming you 'comprehend' Him?
?
The objection about 'partner of God' shows that even false concepts are composed from true ones. Examine your own beliefs: are there any composite concepts you hold about God that are actually derived from worldly experiences? Are you willing to let go of those false composites to embrace the sheer mystery of His essence? What step will you take today to purify your understanding?
