The Self-Refutation of Denying Knowledge of God's Essence
A rebuttal arguing that stating God's essence is unknown implies it is known as the subject of the statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the claim 'God's essence is unknowable' self-destructs because it presupposes knowledge of that essence as the subject of the claim.

Core Idea
Denying knowledge of God's essence requires knowing it as a subject.

In this lesson, we examine a classical kalam argument that exposes a fundamental logical flaw in the claim that God's essence is completely unknowable. The argument uses the basic structure of propositions (subject-predicate) to show that every judgment requires prior conceptualization of its terms. By applying this to the denial of knowledge, the arguer forces the opponent to admit that the denial itself implies some knowledge. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the technical terms and the logical flow.
Pay attention to the terms تصديق and تصور.
2
Identify the main premise
The argument starts with a general premise about every تصديق (affirmative judgment). Understand this premise.
The premise is that every judgment requires conceptualization of subject and predicate.
3
Apply the premise to the claim
See how the premise is applied to the specific claim 'His reality is unintelligible'. Identify the subject and predicate.
The subject is 'His reality', the predicate is 'unintelligible'.
4
Draw the conclusion
From the application, conclude that the one judging must know the subject. Therefore, the claim implies knowledge, leading to contradiction.
The argument says: negation implies affirmation, so the negation is false.
5
Consider the objection and response
The passage then considers an objection: knowledge of the reality by some attributes. The response distinguishes two interpretations of the subject.
The response is incomplete, but it starts to show that if the subject is the essence as such, it must be known as such.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون يكون الخلق عارفين بذاته المخصوصة بوجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن كل تصديق ، فإنه يجب أن يكون مسبوقا بتصور الموضوع والمحمول. فإذا قال القائل : إن حقيقته غير معقولة للخلق ، كان موضوع هذه القضية : هو قولنا حقيقته . ومحمولها : هو قولنا : غير معقولة للخلق ، والحاكم باستناد هذا المحمول إلى هذا الموضوع ، يجب كونه عالما بهذا الموضوع، وبهذا المحمول ، حتى يمكنه هذا الاستناد . فيثبت : أن صدق قولنا : إن حقيقته غير معلومة للخلق ، يقتضي كون حقيقته معقولة للخلق من
حيث إن موضوع القضية يجب أن يكون معلوما ، وما أدى نفيه [ إلى ثبوته . كان نفيه (۱) ] باطلا . فوجب أن يكون قولنا : [ حقيقته (۲) ] غير معقوله للخلق : قولا باطلا . فإن قالوا : إن تلك الحقيقة معقولة بحسب بعض صفاتها وأحوالها . فنقول : إذا قلنا إن حقيقته غير معقولة فموضوع هذه القضية ، إما أن يكون تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي ، أو تلك الحقيقة بحسب بعض صفاتها وأحوالها . فإن كان الأول لزم كون تلك الحقيقة من حيث إنها هي معقولة للخلق . لما بينا أن موضوع القضية من الاعتبار الذي به صار موضوعاً للقضية ، يجب أن يكون متصورا. فإذا كان موضوع هذه القضية هو تلك الحقيقة من حيث إنها هي ، وجب كون تلك الحقيقة من حيث هي هي ،
And those who claim that creatures know His particular essence argued with proofs: The first proof: Every assertion (tasdiq) must be preceded by a conceptualization (tasawwur) of the subject and predicate. So if someone says: 'His reality is unintelligible to creatures,' the subject of this proposition is our saying 'His reality,' and its predicate is our saying 'unintelligible to creatures.' The one who judges that this predicate is attributed to this subject must be knowledgeable of this subject and this predicate in order to make this attribution. So it is established that the truth of our saying 'His reality is unknown to creatures' necessitates that His reality is intelligible to creatures inasmuch as the subject of the proposition must be known. And what leads its negation to its affirmation, its negation is false. So it is necessary that our saying 'His reality is unintelligible to creatures' is a false statement. If they say: 'That reality is intelligible in terms of some of its attributes and states,' then we say: When we say 'His reality is unintelligible,' the subject of this proposition is either that reality as it is in itself, or that reality in terms of some of its attributes and states. If it is the first, then it necessarily follows that that reality as it is in itself is intelligible to creatures, because as we explained, the subject of a proposition from the perspective by which it becomes the subject must be conceptualized. So if the subject of this proposition is that reality as it is in itself, then that reality as it is in itself must be ... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This is the first counter-argument from those who affirm that creatures can know God's particular essence. It points out a logical problem in the claim that His essence is unknowable. It argues that every affirmative judgment (تصديق) requires prior conceptualization (تصور) of the subject and predicate. If one says 'His reality is unintelligible,' the subject 'His reality' must be known in some way for the judgment to be meaningful. Therefore, the very statement that His essence is unknowable implies that it is known. If this leads to a contradiction (negation implies affirmation), then the statement is false. The argument then considers an objection: perhaps the reality is known by some attributes. It responds by distinguishing two possibilities: either the subject is the essence as such, or it is the essence with some attributes. If it is the essence as such, then it must be known as such. The passage ends incomplete, but the argument is clearly attempting to show that the claim of unknowability is self-refuting.

موضوع
محمول
قضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A negative proposition about the unknowability of something does not require knowledge of its subject.
Correction: Every judgment, including negative ones, requires conceptualization of its subject and predicate. Denying knowledge of God's essence still requires knowing what 'His essence' refers to.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical point of the first counter-argument?
A) That God's essence is too great to be known.
B) That the statement 'His essence is unknowable' presupposes knowledge of the essence.
C) That the intellect is too weak.
D) That only God knows Himself.

Key Takeaway
The denial of knowledge of God's essence logically requires that the essence be known as the subject of the denial, making the denial self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself saying 'I cannot know Allah' while at the same time using that very phrase as if you know enough to make that claim? What does this argument reveal about the dishonesty in your own heart?
?
When you say 'God is beyond my understanding,' are you using that as a shield to avoid the effort of seeking deeper knowledge of Him? What is one specific step you can take this week to actually learn more about His attributes?
?
The argument shows that denying knowledge implies some knowledge. How does this challenge your comfort in remaining ignorant about Allah? Will you now commit to studying His names and attributes with the hope that knowing them will transform your worship?

The Objection: Necessity as a Negative Concept
Objector argues necessity is negative and provides two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's challenge that necessity (wujub) may be a negative concept, and how this undermines the composition argument.

Core Idea
If necessity is negative, sharing it does not cause composition.

The author has argued that positing two necessary beings leads to composition in each essence. But this argument assumes necessity is a positive attribute. The objector now raises a critical challenge: what if necessity is merely a negative concept? Then sharing in necessity implies no real composition. This lesson presents the objector's two proofs for the negative nature of wujub.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its setup
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the objector frames the challenge: he denies that composition follows if wujub is negative. Then he states two things that need to be proven.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'sharing' (ishtirak) and 'distinction' (imtiyaz).
2
Understand the first proof from predication on non-existence
The first proof argues that wujub is predicated of non-existence (e.g., 'its non-existence is necessary'). But a predicate of non-existence cannot itself be existent; therefore wujub is not a positive concept.
This relies on the principle that a positive attribute cannot inhere in pure non-existence.
3
Analyze the second proof by elimination
The second proof considers three possibilities if wujub were positive: it would be the whole essence, part of it, or external. All are shown false, so it must be negative.
Consider why each option fails: whole? No, essence is more than necessity. Part? Then composition. External? Then accident, but necessity is essential.
4
Reflect on the implication for the main argument
If the objector succeeds in proving wujub negative, then the earlier proof of composition fails because the shared element is not a real positive attribute. This sets up a deeper debate about the nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : السؤال على ما ذكرتم من وجوه : السؤال الأول : أن نقول : أنتم بنيتم كل هذه الوجوه على حرف واحد ، وهو أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته ، فإنه يلزم وقوع التركيب في ذات كل واحد منهما. فنقول : هذا ممنوع وذلك لأن هذا التركيب ، إنما يلزم إن كان الوجوب الذي به الاشتراك مفهوما ثبوتيا ، وكان التعين الذي به الامتياز مفهوما ثبوتيا ، وحينئذ يلزم التركيب وتستمر الشبه التي ذكرتموها . أما بتقدير أن يكون الوجوب الذي به المشاركة مفهوما عدميا ، فإنه لا يلزم التركيب ، لاحتمال أن يقال : إنه وقع الاشتراك في المفهوم السلبي ، ووقع الامتياز بتمام الماهية ، فنفتقر في تقرير هذا السؤال إلى بيان أمرين : أحدهما : أن الوجوب مفهوم سلبي ، والثاني : أنه إذا كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يلزم وقوع التركيب . أما المقام الأول : فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن كل ما صدق عليه أن وجوده ممتنع ، صدق عليه أن عدمه واجب . وعلى هذا التقدير فالوجوب محمول على العدم والمحمول على العدم أن يكون موجودا [ وإلا لزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالعلم المحض ، والنفي الصرف. وإنه محال (1) ] فيثبت بهذا : أن المفهوم من الوجوب يمتنع أن يكون أمرا موجودا . الثاني : وهو أنه لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثبوتيا ، لكان إما أن يكون تمام تلك الحقيقة أو جزء منها أو أمرا خارجا عنها ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فالقول بكون الوجوب مفهوما ثبوتيا : باطل .
And one may say: The objection to what you have mentioned from the proofs is the first objection: that we say: You have built all these proofs on one point, which is that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary by essence, then composition would occur in the essence of each. So we say: This is denied, because this composition only follows if the necessity by which there is sharing is a positive concept and the individuation by which there is distinction is a positive concept. Then composition would follow and the difficulties you mentioned would continue. But on the assumption that the necessity by which there is sharing is a negative concept, then composition does not follow, because it is possible to say: sharing occurred in the negative concept, and distinction occurred by the complete quiddity. So in establishing this objection we need to clarify two matters: first, that necessity is a negative concept; second, that if this is the case, composition does not follow. As for the first matter, it is indicated by proofs: The first proof: Everything of which it is true that its existence is impossible, it is true that its non-existence is necessary. On this basis, necessity is predicated of non-existence, and what is predicated of non-existence cannot be existent [otherwise it would entail the existence of a positive attribute in pure non-existence, which is impossible]. Thus it is established that the concept of necessity cannot be an existent thing. The second proof: That if necessity were a positive thing, then it would either be the entirety of that reality, or a part of it, or something external to it. And the three divisions are false, so the statement that necessity is a positive concept is false.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents the objector's first challenge to the previous proofs. The objector claims that the author's reasoning assumes that wujub (necessity) is a positive concept (mafhum thubuti). If wujub is instead a negative concept (mafhum 'adami), then sharing in wujub does not entail composition because the shared element is not a real positive attribute. The objector needs to establish two things: that wujub is negative, and that on that basis composition is avoided. He gives two proofs for the first. Proof one: everything whose existence is impossible has necessary non-existence; wujub is predicated of non-existence, but a predicate of non-existence cannot itself be existent, so wujub is not a positive concept. Proof two: if wujub were positive, it would either be the whole essence, a part, or something external; all are false: it cannot be the whole because essence is not just necessity; it cannot be a part because then composition; it cannot be external because then it would be an accident. Therefore wujub is negative.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'necessary' describes a positive attribute of God, similar to power or knowledge.
Correction: The objector argues that necessity is merely the negation of possibility or dependence, i.e., a negative concept.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that necessity (wujub) is a negative concept?
A) If necessity were positive, it would be a part of the essence.
B) Everything whose existence is impossible has necessary non-existence, and necessity is predicated of non-existence, so it cannot be existent.
C) If two necessary beings shared in a positive concept, they would be composites.
D) The objector does not give a proof.

Key Takeaway
The objector argues that necessity is a negative concept, and he provides two proofs to establish this, thereby undermining the earlier composition argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God as 'Necessary Being', do you instinctively imagine a positive attribute added to Him, like a quality that makes Him necessary? After reading this objection, can you see that necessity might instead be the absence of causedness or dependency? Does that make your tawhid purer, or does it feel uncomfortable to think of God's attributes as negations? Examine your heart: is your attachment to positive images of God a form of subtle shirk?
?
The objector argues that if necessity were positive, it would force composition in God's essence. Are you willing to let go of any mental picture that attributes positive 'parts' to God—even conceptually—in order to preserve His absolute simplicity? What is one concrete step you can take today to train your mind to think of God as beyond all composition, even in how you speak about His attributes?

Refuting the Objection: Wujub as a Negative Concept
Sanusi presents the first objection and refutes it with two proofs that wujub is a negative concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the argument against two necessary existents and how Sanusi proves that wujub is a negative concept to avoid composition.

Core Idea
Wujub is not a positive attribute—it is a negative concept predicated of non-existence.

Sanusi anticipates an objection: what if the necessity that two necessary existents share is a negative concept? Then no composition follows. To refute this, he must prove that wujub is indeed negative. He provides two proofs, the first based on the equivalence of impossibility and necessity of non-existence, and the second by showing that wujub cannot be a positive thing. This passage grounds that defense.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Notice that the objector claims composition only follows if wujub is a positive concept. If negative, no composition. Learn the two matters that need clarification.
The objector does not assert wujub is negative; he raises it as a possibility.
2
Follow the first proof
Trace how Sanusi argues that wujub is predicated of non-existence, so it cannot be an existent positive thing.
Key premise: everything impossible has necessary non-existence.
3
Analyze the second proof
Examine the division into three possibilities (essence, part, external) and why each is impossible. Understand the reasons for each impossibility.
This proof shows wujub cannot be a positive attribute at all.
4
See the conclusion
Realize that wujub must be a negative concept, and thus the objection fails. The original proof against two necessary existents stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : السؤال على ما ذكرتم من وجوه : السؤال الأول : أن نقول : أنتم بنيتم كل هذه الوجوه على حرف واحد ، وهو أنا لو فرضنا موجودين يكون كل واحد منهما واجبا لذاته ، فإنه يلزم وقوع التركيب في ذات كل واحد منهما. فنقول : هذا ممنوع وذلك لأن هذا التركيب ، إنما يلزم إن كان الوجوب الذي به الاشتراك مفهوما ثبوتيا ، وكان التعين الذي به الامتياز مفهوما ثبوتيا ، وحينئذ يلزم التركيب وتستمر الشبه التي ذكرتموها . أما بتقدير أن يكون الوجوب الذي به المشاركة مفهوما عدميا ، فإنه لا يلزم التركيب ، لاحتمال أن يقال : إنه وقع الاشتراك في المفهوم السلبي ، ووقع الامتياز بتمام الماهية ، فنفتقر في تقرير هذا السؤال إلى بيان أمرين : أحدهما : أن الوجوب مفهوم سلبي ، والثاني : أنه إذا كان الأمر كذلك ، لم يلزم وقوع التركيب . أما المقام الأول : فيدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إن كل ما صدق عليه أن وجوده ممتنع ، صدق عليه أن عدمه واجب . وعلى هذا التقدير فالوجوب محمول على العدم والمحمول على العدم أن يكون موجودا [ وإلا لزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالعلم المحض ، والنفي الصرف. وإنه محال ] فيثبت بهذا : أن المفهوم من الوجوب يمتنع أن يكون أمرا موجودا . الثاني : وهو أنه لو كان الوجوب أمرا ثبوتيا ، لكان إما أن يكون تمام تلك الحقيقة أو جزء منها أو أمرا خارجا عنها ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة فالقول بكون الوجوب مفهوما ثبوتيا : باطل . أما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون تمام الماهية فلوجوه : أحدها : إنا نصف تلك الماهية بالوجوب . ووصف الشيء بنفسه محال . لأنا ندرك تفرقة بديهية بين قولنا : هذه الذات ، وبين قولنا : هذه الذات واجبة الوجود . وثانيها : إنه لو كان الوجوب هو نفس تلك الماهية ، لكان كل من عرف الوجوب بالذات عرف تلك الماهية المخصوصة ، ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك ، علمنا أن الوجوب ليس نفس الماهية . وثالثها : إن نقيض قولنا : واجب لذاته : أنه ليس بواجب ، ولا يناقضه قولنا : إنه ليس تلك الذات الفلانية ، ولو كان الوجوب نفس تلك الذات لكان نقيض أحدهما ، عين نقيض الآخر . وأما بيان أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الوجوب جزءا للماهية. لأنكم لما ذكرتم أن كل ماله جزء فهو : ممكن ، فلو كان الواجب بالذات له جزء ، لكان ممكنا بالذات . وأما بيان أنه لا يجوز أن يكون خارجا عن الماهية . فلوجهين : الأول : إن كل صفة خارجة عن الماهية فهي مفتقرة إلى تلك الماهية ، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته ، وواجب بغيره ، فيلزم أن يكون الواجب بالذات ممكنا لذاته ويلزم منه الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . وأيضا : فلما كان الوجوب بالذات واجبا بغيره، كان وجوب ذلك الغير سابقا على وجوب هذا الأثر ، فيلزم أن يحصل قبل ذلك الوجوب ، وجوب آخر إلى غير النهاية . وذلك محال . والثاني : إن الصفة مفتقرة إلى الذات، محتاجة إليها ، فلو حكمنا بأن وجوب تلك الذات إنما كان بسبب هذه الصفة لزم كون كل واحد منهما مفتقرا ، إلى الآخر وهو محال ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أنه يمتنع كون الوجوب بالذات نفس الماهية ، ولا جزءا منها ، ولا صفة خارجة عنها فيمتنع كون الوجوب صفة ثبوتية
And one may say: The objection regarding what you have mentioned has several aspects: The first objection: We say: You have built all these arguments on one point, which is that if we suppose two existents each of which is necessary by itself (wājib li-dhātihi), then composition (tarkīb) must occur in the essence of each. So we say: This is denied, because this composition only follows if the necessity (wujūb) by which there is sharing is a positive concept (mafhūm thubūtī), and the individuation (ta‘ayyun) by which there is distinction is a positive concept; then composition follows and the doubts you have mentioned continue. But if we suppose that the necessity by which there is participation is a negative concept (mafhūm ‘adamī), then composition does not follow, because it is possible to say: Participation occurred in the negative concept, and distinction occurred by the complete essence (tamām al-māhiyyah). So in establishing this objection we need to clarify two matters: One: that necessity is a negative concept; the second: that if this is the case, composition does not follow. As for the first station: it is indicated by proofs: The first: Everything of which it is true that its existence is impossible, it is true that its non-existence is necessary. And on this assumption, necessity is predicated of non-existence, and what is predicated of non-existence cannot be existent [otherwise it would entail that an existent attribute subsists in pure non-existence and sheer negation, and that is impossible]. So it is established by this that the concept of necessity cannot be an existent thing. The second: That if necessity were a positive thing, then it would either be the entirety of that reality (tamām tilka al-ḥaqīqah), or a part of it, or something external to it; and the three divisions are false, so the statement that necessity is a positive concept is false. As for the proof that it cannot be the entirety of the essence: due to several reasons: One: We describe that essence with necessity. Describing a thing by itself is impossible, because we perceive an intuitive distinction between saying 'this essence' and saying 'this essence is necessary of existence'. The second: If necessity were that essence itself, then whoever knew necessity by essence would know that particular essence, but since that is not the case, we know that necessity is not the essence itself. The third: The contradictory of our statement 'necessary by itself' is 'it is not necessary', and the statement 'it is not that particular essence' does not contradict it; and if necessity were that essence itself, then the contradictory of one would be the same as the contradictory of the other. And as for the proof that necessity cannot be a part of the essence: because you have stated that everything that has a part is possible; so if the necessary by itself had a part, it would be possible by itself. And as for the proof that it cannot be external to the essence: due to two reasons: The first: Every attribute external to the essence is dependent on that essence, and that which is dependent on another is possible by itself and necessary through another; thus the necessary by itself would be possible by itself, and that entails the combination of two contradictories, which is impossible. Also: Since the necessity by itself would be necessary through another, the necessity of that other would be prior to the necessity of this effect, so another necessity would have to occur before that necessity ad infinitum, and that is impossible. The second: The attribute is dependent on the essence, in need of it; so if we judge that the necessity of that essence is only due to this attribute, then each one would be dependent on the other, which is impossible. So it is established by what we have mentioned: that it is impossible for necessity by itself to be the essence itself, nor a part of it, nor an attribute external to it; therefore it is impossible for necessity to be a positive attribute.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is the presentation and refutation of the first objection to the earlier proof that two necessary existents are impossible. The objector argues that the entire proof rests on the assumption that the concept of necessity (wujūb) is a positive attribute (thubūtī). If instead it is a negative concept (ʿadamī), then when two things share a negative concept, no composition (tarkīb) follows; the distinction comes from the complete essence (tamām al-māhiyyah), not from a positive individuator. To answer this, the author must establish two points: (1) that wujūb is indeed a negative concept, and (2) that if so, composition does not occur. He then tackles the first point (al-maqām al-awwal) with two proofs. First proof: For everything impossible of existence, its non-existence is necessary; thus necessity is predicated of non-existence; but a positive entity cannot be predicated of non-existence without impossibility (as explained in the bracketed note). Therefore the concept of necessity cannot be an existent thing (amr mawjūd). Second proof: If necessity were a positive thing, it must be either the complete essence, a part of it, or external to it. Each is impossible. The author then elaborates each impossibility: it cannot be the essence itself (because describing something by itself is absurd; intuitive distinction; contradictory relationships). It cannot be a part (because anything with a part is possible). It cannot be external (because then it would depend on the essence, making the necessary existent possible by itself, leading to circularity or infinite regress). Thus wujūb cannot be a positive attribute (ṣifa thubūtiyya).

Key Terms
وجوب (wujūb)
مفهوم ثبوتي (positive concept)
مفهوم سلبي (negative concept)
تركيب (composition)
تمام الماهية (complete essence)
واجب لذاته (necessary by itself)
صفة ثبوتية (positive attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wujub (necessity) is a positive attribute that can be shared among necessary existents.
Correction: Wujub is actually a negative concept; it is predicated of non-existence and cannot be a positive attribute, as shown by two proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the first proof that wujub is a negative concept rely on?
A) That wujub is predicated of non-existence because for anything impossible, its non-existence is necessary.
B) That wujub cannot be a part of the essence because then the necessary would be possible.
C) That wujub cannot be external because it would cause infinite regress.
D) That wujub is known intuitively as a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
Wujub is a negative concept, so sharing it does not cause composition in necessary existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that Allah's necessity (wujub) is a negative concept—meaning it is not a positive attribute added to His essence—does that challenge your natural tendency to think of His attributes as 'things' He possesses? How does this realization affect the way you approach His names and attributes in your worship and awe?
?
The objector in this lesson tried to save the possibility of multiple gods by arguing that sharing a negative concept avoids composition. Sanusi refuted this by proving wujub is negative anyway. Have you ever entertained subtle doubts about the uniqueness of Allah? How does this precise logical defense strengthen your certainty in tawheed, and what does that demand from your heart in terms of guarding your faith?
?
If the very foundation of Allah's necessity is beyond positive description, how does that humble you when you try to describe Him with your limited concepts? Can you accept that even the most essential attribute of your Lord is, in a sense, 'nothing' in your own conceptual framework? What does that imply about the need for submission to revelation over unaided reason?

Objection that Necessity is Positive and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation on the nature of wujub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that wujub (necessity) is a positive concept and how the author refutes it using two arguments.

Core Idea
Necessity is a negative concept, not a positive attribute added to existence.

This passage presents a strong objection to the claim that 'necessary by essence' is a negative concept. The objector offers two proofs that wujub must be positive, but the author carefully dismantles each. Understanding this dialectical exchange is key to grasping the subtle kalam terminology about existence and attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and its two proofs
Read the objector's claim and the two proofs: one based on 'affirmation of existence', the other on the logic of contradictories.
The objector is trying to force the conclusion that wujub is a positive concept.
2
Examine the first proof and its weakness
Notice how the objector equates necessity with 'affirmation of existence' and argues it cannot be privative. The author's response shows this phrase is ambiguous.
The author deconstructs the three possible meanings of 'affirmation'.
3
Examine the second proof and its rebuttal
The second proof uses the contradictory pair wujub/non-wujub and argues that non-wujub applies to pure non-existence, so wujub must be positive. The author counters with a parity argument using 'possible with particular possibility'.
This shows the objector's argument proves too much.
4
Grasp the overall conclusion
The objector fails to prove wujub is positive; the author's refutations stand, supporting his position that wujub is a negative concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: الدليل على أنه مفهوم ثبوتي وجهان: الأول: إن وجوب الوجود عبارة عن تأكيد الوجود، فلو كان الوجوب قيدا عدميا، لزم أن يكون الشيء متأكدا بسلب نفسه، وينقيض نفسه، وأنه محال. والثاني: أن نقيض الوجوب هو البلاوجوب، والمفهوم من اللاوجوب يصدق إما على الممتنع وإما على الممكن الخاص. أما الممتنع فإنه عدم محض. فالذي يصدق عليه، يجب أن يكون عدما محضا، وإلا لزم قيام الصفة الموجودة بالعدم المحض. وهو محال. وأما الممكن الخاص فإنه لا يمتنع بقاؤه على العدم، مع أنه في هذه الحالة يصدق عليه أنه غير واجب. فالمفهوم من اللاوجوب يصدق على العدم المحض في هذه الصورة أيضا. فوجب أن يكون المفهوم من اللاوجوب هنا أيضا عدما محضا، فيثبت بهذا البرهان: أن اللاوجوب عدم محض. فوجب أن يكون المفهوم من الوجوب ثبوتا محضا بالضرورة، لأن أحد النقيضين لا بد وأن يكون مفهوما ثابتا. لأنا نقول: أما الوجه الأول: فجوابه: أن نقول: ما المراد من قولكم: الوجوب تأكيد الوجود؟ إن أردتم به أن ما يكون واجب الوجود لذاته، فإنه يفتقر في وجوده إلى سبب منفصل، فهذا معقول، إلا أن هذا المعنى مفهوم سلبي، والأمر فيه ظاهر. وإن عنيتم به كون تلك الماهية مقتضية لذلك الوجود فقد دللنا على اقتضاء الشيء لشيء آخر، يمتنع أن يكون موجودا زائدا، وإلا لزم التسلسل. وإن عنيتم بهذا التأكيد مفهوما ثالثا، فلا بد من بيانه. وأما الوجه الثاني فجوابه: أن هذا معارض بأن قولنا: إنه ليس بممكن بالإمكان الخاص، نقيض لقولنا: إنه ممكن بالإمكان الخاص، لكن قولنا: إنه ليس بممكن بالإمكان الخاص يصدق على الممتنع الذي هو عدم محض فوجب أن يكون قولنا: إنه ليس بممكن بالإمكان الخاص عدما محضا، وإلا لزم قيام الموجود بالمعدوم. وإذا كان كذلك كان قولنا: إنه ممكن بالإمكان الخاص، مفهوما ثبوتيا.
It should not be said: The proof that it is a positive concept has two aspects: First, that the necessity of existence is an expression of the affirmation of existence. If necessity were a privative condition, then a thing would be affirmed by its own negation and contradiction, which is impossible. Second, that the contradictory of necessity is non-necessity, and the concept of non-necessity applies either to the impossible or to the particular possible. As for the impossible, it is pure non-existence. That which applies to it must be pure non-existence, otherwise an existent attribute would inhere in pure non-existence, which is impossible. As for the particular possible, its remaining in non-existence is not impossible, while in this state it is described as not necessary. Thus the concept of non-necessity applies to pure non-existence also in this case. So it is necessary that the concept of non-necessity here is also pure non-existence, and by this proof it is established that non-necessity is pure non-existence. Consequently, the concept of necessity must be pure existence necessarily, because one of the two contradictories must be a fixed concept. So we say: As for the first aspect, its answer is that we say: What do you mean by saying that necessity is an affirmation of existence? If you mean by it that that which is necessary in itself is dependent in its existence on a separate cause, then this is intelligible, except that this meaning is a negative concept, and the matter is clear. If you mean by it that that essence necessitates that existence, then we have proven that the necessitation of one thing for another cannot be an additional existent, otherwise there would be an infinite regress. If you mean by this affirmation a third concept, then it must be explained. As for the second aspect, its answer is that this is rebutted by the fact that our statement: 'It is not possible with particular possibility' is the contradictory of our statement: 'It is possible with particular possibility.' But our statement: 'It is not possible with particular possibility' applies to the impossible, which is pure non-existence. So it is necessary that our statement: 'It is not possible with particular possibility' be pure non-existence, otherwise an existent would inhere in a non-existent. If this is so, then our statement: 'It is possible with particular possibility' is a positive concept.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the author's claim that the concept of 'necessary by essence' is negative, followed by the author's refutation. The objector provides two proofs that the concept must be positive. First, necessity as an 'affirmation of existence' cannot be a privative term, because that would be self-contradictory. Second, since the contradictory of necessity (non-necessity) applies to pure non-existence (impossible and possible-in-potency), then by the principle that of two contradictories one must be positive, necessity must be positive. The author responds to each: to the first, he shows the term 'affirmation of existence' is ambiguous—if it means dependence on a cause, that is negative; if it means self-necessitation, that leads to regress; if a third meaning, it is unproven. To the second, he uses a parity argument: the contradictory of 'possible with particular possibility' is 'not possible with particular possibility', which also applies to pure non-existence, so by the same logic 'possible with particular possibility' would be positive; but this does not prove necessity is positive. Thus the objection fails.

ثبوتي
عدمي
إمكان خاص
امتناع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity (wujub) is a positive attribute, like existence itself, that is added to the essence.
Correction: The passage argues that wujub is a negative concept, not a positive added attribute. The objector's proofs fail, and the author shows that wujub can be understood as the absence of dependence on a cause or as self-necessitation without being an additional existent.

Checkpoint
What is the objector's first argument for wujub being a positive concept?
A) It is an affirmation of existence, so it cannot be privative.
B) It is derived from the concept of the possible.
C) It is a necessary attribute of God.
D) It is a relational concept.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection that wujub is a positive concept by showing the ambiguity of 'affirmation of existence' and exposing a flaw in the argument from contradictories.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is contingent—it depends entirely on God. Do you live each day with the constant awareness that you could cease to exist at any moment, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change in your life if you truly internalized that you are not a necessary being?
?
This lesson teaches that necessity is a negative concept: it is the absence of the possibility of non-existence. Only God possesses this. How often do you treat other things—money, health, relationships—as if they must exist, as if they are necessary? How does this misplacement affect your reliance on the One who alone is necessary?
?
The objector's argument seemed logical but was flawed. In your own spiritual life, where might you be holding onto a seemingly logical but flawed belief about God or yourself? Will you let this passage expose that error and surrender to the truth, even if it is uncomfortable?

The Privative Nature of Necessity by Essence
The author proves that wujub bi al-dhat is negative by analyzing its relationship to imkan and imtina'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why necessity by essence must be a privative concept, based on its relationship to possibility and impossibility.

Core Idea
Necessity is positive only at the cost of contradiction.

This lesson examines a crisp argument from the author showing that necessity by essence cannot be a positive attribute. By analyzing how necessity relates to possibility and impossibility—two concepts with opposite metaphysical statuses—the author demonstrates that any attempt to classify necessity as positive leads to an inevitable contradiction. Understanding this argument deepens your grasp of the foundational terms used in rational theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once to absorb its structure. Note the conditional 'if...then' reasoning and the repeated terms.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the definitions and premises
Notice that the author defines necessity as the negation of both particular possibility and impossibility. Also note that particular possibility is positive and impossibility is privative.
These are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Follow the parity argument
The author uses a parity (symmetry) argument: if removing a privative makes necessity positive, then by the same logic, removing a positive should make necessity privative. This creates a contradiction if one conclusion is accepted and the other rejected.
The argument is designed to show that the claim 'necessity is positive' is untenable.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that necessity cannot be positive; it must be privative. Reflect on why this matters for understanding divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: الوجوب بالذات عبارة عن نفي الإمكان الخاص ومن نفي الامتناع وثبت أن الإمكان الخاص مفهوم ثبوتي، وأن الامتناع مفهوم عدمي، فإن لزم من كون الوجوب بالذات رافعا للامتناع، كونه ثبوتيا لأن رافع العدم ثبوت لزم من كون هذا الوجوب بالذات رافعا للإمكان الخاص كونه عدميا، لأن رافع الثبوت عدم، وإن لم يلزم هذا، لم يلزم ذاك أيضا. فهذا مجموع المباحث الدالة على أن المفهوم من الوجوب بالذات [ يمتنع كونه ثبوتيا ].
If this is established, then we say: Necessity by essence is an expression of the negation of particular possibility and the negation of impossibility. It has been established that particular possibility is a positive concept and that impossibility is a privative concept. If it is necessary that because necessity by essence removes impossibility, it is positive—since the remover of non-existence is existence—then it would be necessary that because this necessity by essence removes particular possibility, it is privative—since the remover of existence is non-existence. If this does not follow, then that does not follow either. This is the sum of the investigations indicating that the concept of necessity by essence [cannot be positive].
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a deductive argument showing the impossibility of necessity being a positive concept. The author first restates that wujub bi al-dhat is defined as the negation of both particular possibility (imkan khas) and impossibility (imtina'). He then notes that imkan khas is a positive concept (thubuti) and imtina' is a privative concept (adami). The argument proceeds by parity: if necessity being the remover of impossibility (a privative) makes it positive because it removes non-existence, then by the same logic necessity being the remover of particular possibility (a positive) would make it privative because it removes existence. Therefore, if the conclusion is not drawn in the second case, it should not be drawn in the first either. This refutes the claim that necessity is positive.

Key Terms

الإمكان الخاص
الامتناع
ثبوتي
عدمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that necessity by essence is a positive attribute (like existence) without further analysis.
Correction: The argument shows that if necessity were positive, then it would also have to be privative, which is contradictory. Therefore necessity is not positive but privative.

Checkpoint

A) Because it removes both impossibility and possibility, but if it were positive for one reason, it would be negative for the other.
B) Because positive concepts cannot be attributes of God.
C) Because necessity is a relational concept.
D) Because it is a simple concept.

Key Takeaway
Necessity by essence is a privative concept because treating it as positive leads to an unavoidable logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How often do you reflect on the meaning of 'there is no god but Allah' in light of necessity being a negation? Can you recall a moment when this understanding deepened your tawhid? If not, what will you do to make it a reality?

The Second Station: Necessity as a Negative Concept Does Not Entail Composition
The author argues that a shared negative concept does not cause composition in the simple essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the concept of necessity by essence being negative does not imply that God is composite, using the example of simple quiddities.

Core Idea
Shared negatives do not cause composition; simple quiddities prove this.

This passage tackles a subtle concern: if God is necessary by essence, and necessity is a negative concept, does that make God's essence composite? The author argues no, because sharing a negative concept does not entail composition. He provides a clear proof using the nature of simple quiddities to illustrate his point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the claim, the exposition, and the proof structure.
The passage is incomplete but the main argument is self-contained.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that a shared negative concept does not make the essence composite. Underline this claim.
3
Follow the proof
The proof uses simple quiddities: any two simple quiddities share in the negation of everything else, yet they remain simple. If shared negations caused composition, simples would be composite—a contradiction.
This shows that shared negatives are harmless.
4
Apply to God
Since necessity by essence is a negative concept, and the example shows shared negatives don't cause composition, God remains simple.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author establishes that the concept of necessity by essence is negative and that shared negatives do not cause composition. Therefore, God's simplicity is preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الثاني: في بيان أنه لما كان المفهوم من الوجوب بالذات أمرا سلبيا، لم يلزم منه كون الواجب لذاته مركبا. وتقريره: أنه على هذا التقدير يكون الاشتراك حاصلا في هذا المفهوم السلبي، ويكون الامتياز حاصلا بتمام تلك الحقيقة، وهذا لا يوجب وقوع التركيب في الحقيقة. والدليل عليه: أنه لا شك في وجود ماهية من الماهيات وتلك الماهيات إما أن تكون بسيطة أو مركبة، فإن كانت بسيطة فقد حصلت الماهية البسيطة، وإن كانت مركبة فالمركب إنما يتولد من البسائط. فالقول بإثبات الماهية البسيطة أمر لابد من الاعتراف به، على كل حال ثم إن كل بسيطين، فإنه لابد وأن يشتركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنها، فإن كان الاشتراك في السلوب يوجب وقوع التركيب في الماهية، لزم كون البسيط مركبا، وذلك محال. فيثبت بما ذكرنا: أن المفهوم من الوجوب بالذات مفهوم سلبي، وثبت أنه متى وقع الاشتراك في المفهومات
The second station: in explaining that since the concept of necessity by essence is a negative matter, it does not entail that the necessary by essence is composite. Its exposition: On this assumption, the shared element occurs in this negative concept, and the differentiation occurs by the entirety of that essence, and this does not necessitate the occurrence of composition in the essence. The proof of this: There is no doubt about the existence of a quiddity among quiddities. Those quiddities are either simple or composite. If simple, then the simple quiddity has been attained; if composite, then the composite only arises from simples. So the affirmation of a simple quiddity is something that must be acknowledged in any case. Then, any two simples must share in the negation of everything else from them. If sharing in negations necessitates the occurrence of composition in the quiddity, then it would be necessary that the simple be composite, which is impossible. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that the concept of necessity by essence is a negative concept, and it is established that whenever sharing occurs in concepts...
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage is a proof that wujub bi al-dhat being a negative concept does not imply composition in God. The author first states the claim: a shared negative concept does not make the essence composite, because the shared element is negative and the distinguishing factor is the entire essence. He then provides a proof: quiddities are either simple or composite; simple quiddities must be acknowledged. Any two simple quiddities share in negating everything other than themselves. If this sharing in negations caused composition, then every simple would be composite—a contradiction. Therefore, shared negatives do not cause composition. Hence, the necessity concept being negative does not make God composite.

Fourth Argument: Determination Cannot Be Additional
Fourth argument refuting determination as additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument that determination cannot be an additional attribute, and how it uses a reductio ad absurdum to show that both possible outcomes lead to impossibility.

Core Idea
Determination cannot be additional—it would entail an impossible chain of absurdities.

In this fourth argument, the author deploys a powerful logical tool: assuming the opponent's position leads to two impossible outcomes. Either multiplicity of necessary beings or universal contingency. Both are unacceptable. Let's trace this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the assumption
Read the opening line carefully: 'If determination were an additional fixed concept.' This is the hypothesis the argument seeks to disprove.
The opponent claims determination is a real, additional attribute to the essence.
2
Follow the two branches
Notice the bifurcation: either each part of this composite is necessary in itself, or one part is contingent. Trace each branch to its consequence.
The first branch leads to multiple necessary beings; the second leads to universal contingency.
3
Recognize the contradiction
Both consequences are impossible in the author's framework. Therefore, the original assumption must be false.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opponent's view leads to absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إن التعين لو كان مفهوماً زائداً ثابتاً ، لكان هذا التعين مركباً من جزئين. وكل واحد من هذين الجزئين ، إن كان واجباً لذاته ، صار واجب الوجود أكثر من واحد ، وأنتم إنما تذكرون هذه الدلائل لإبطاله ، وإن لم يكن كذلك ، فحينئذ يلزم القول يكون أحد هذين الجزئين ممكناً لذاته [ وما يكون أحد أجزائه ممكناً لذاته ] كان ذلك المركب أولى بالإمكان ، فيلزم أن يكون كل ذات معينة مشخصة في الوجود أن تكون ممكنة لذاتها . وهذا يقتضي أن لا يحصل في الوجود موجود معين، يكون هو في نفسه واجب الوجود ، وذلك محال
The fourth argument: If determination were an additional concept that is fixed, then this determination would be composed of two parts. If each of these two parts is necessary in itself, then the necessary of existence becomes more than one—and you only mention these proofs to invalidate that—and if it is not like that, then it would necessitate saying that one of these two parts is contingent in itself, and whatever has one of its parts contingent in itself, that composite is more deserving of contingency. Thus it would necessitate that every determined individual being in existence is contingent in itself. And this requires that there not exist in existence any determined existent that is in itself necessary of existence, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (al-wajh al-rabi') in a refutation of the view that determination (al-ta'ayyun) is an additional attribute to the essence (al-dhat). The author uses a reductio ad absurdum. He assumes for argument's sake that determination is an additional fixed concept. If so, it would be composite of two parts. He then examines the status of those parts: if each part is necessary in itself (wajib li-dhatihi), then there would be more than one necessary existent—a consequence the opponent (you) seeks to avoid. If, on the other hand, one part is contingent (mumkin li-dhatihi), then the composite is even more contingent, implying that every determinate entity is contingent. This would preclude the existence of a necessary being. Both outcomes are impossible, so the initial assumption that determination is additional is false.

Key Terms
التعين
زائد
ماهية
واجب الوجود
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Determination is a real, additional attribute to the divine essence.
Correction: The argument shows that if determination were additional, it would lead to absurd consequences (multiple necessary beings or universal contingency), so determination cannot be additional.

Checkpoint
In the fourth argument, if both parts of determination are necessary in themselves, what follows?
A) There would be more than one necessary being.
B) The composite would be contingent.
C) The essence would be composite.
D) Determination would be negative.

Key Takeaway
Determination cannot be an additional attribute, because that would lead to either multiple necessary beings or universal contingency, both of which are impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This argument exposes that even one unnecessary part leads to universal contingency—a world without a Necessary Being. How does this refine your understanding of Allah's absolute simplicity?
?
Did you ever, even subconsciously, imagine Allah's attributes as separate 'add-ons' to His essence? This argument shows that such a notion destroys the very concept of a Necessary Being. In your heart, do you affirm that Allah's essence is one, without any addition, even in how you conceive His determination?
?
If this argument is true, then every determinate being—including you—is contingent. Do you live each day with the weight of that contingency, or do you act as if your existence is self-sufficient? How will this lesson change your reliance on Allah alone for every moment of your existence?

Fifth Argument: Determination as Pure Negation
Fifth argument that determination is purely negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth argument that determination is purely a negative concept, not a positive attribute.

Core Idea
Determination is nothing but the negation of identity with another.

The fifth argument tackles the nature of determination (al-ta'ayyun). The author reduces it to a simple relational concept: 'this is not that.' This seemingly trivial idea has profound implications for whether determination is a positive attribute or not. Let's examine the concise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the words stand on their own before diving into commentary.
Notice the brevity: only two sentences carry the entire argument.
2
Grasp the meaning of determination
Determination (al-ta'ayyun) is what makes a thing distinct from others. The author defines it purely as the fact that 'this thing is not that other thing'.
3
Identify the claim of pure negation
The author states that the concept of determination is a pure negation (salb maḥḍ). This means it does not add any positive content but merely denies identity.
This directly challenges views that consider determination an additional attribute.
4
Reflect on the implication
If determination is purely negative, it cannot be a real positive attribute. This supports the broader theological position that many apparent attributes are relational or negative.
Think about how this connects to discussions of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : إنه لا معنى للتعين إلا أن هذا الشيء ليس هو ذلك الآخر . ومعلوم أن هذا المفهوم سلب محض .
The fifth argument: There is no meaning for determination except that this thing is not that other. And it is known that this concept is a pure negation.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-wajh al-khamis). The author argues that determination (al-ta'ayyun) has no meaning beyond the fact that 'this thing is not that other thing'. In other words, it is a relational concept of distinctness. The author then asserts that this concept is a pure negation (salb mahd) — it does not affirm any positive attribute. This directly contradicts the view that determination is an additional positive attribute.

Correction: The argument shows determination is merely the negative notion of being distinct from another, not a positive quality.

Key Takeaway

Three Proofs for Determination as Additional
Three arguments for determination being additional to quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs the opponent uses to argue that determination is an additional concept to the essence.

Core Idea
Determination adds a restrictive element that prevents sharing.

The opponent begins by trying to prove that determination is additional to the quiddity. They present three distinct arguments, each based on logical differences between general and particular concepts. This lesson unpacks those arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage for the three proofs, then the translation. Focus on the structure: each proof starts with an assumption and leads to a conclusion.
The passage is from 'أما الذي يدل' to 'زائد على الماهية'.
2
Examine the first proof
Notice the contrast between 'blackness' and 'this blackness'. The proof argues that if there were no difference, every blackness would be this blackness—false, so 'this' adds a restriction.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof uses the concept of shareability: 'blackness' can be shared, 'this blackness' cannot. This difference in judgment indicates an added restriction.
4
Examine the third proof
The third proof appeals to intuition: 'this blackness' is composite of 'this' and 'blackness', which are distinct concepts, so determination is additional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقال : الدليل على أن التعين مفهوم ثبوتي : هو أنا نبين أنه مفهوم زائد على أصل الماهية ، ثم نبين أن ذلك الزائد يجب أن يكون أمراً ثابتاً
أما الذي يدل على أنه مفهوم زائد على أصل الماهية وجوه :
الأول : إنه لو كان لا فرق بين الواد وبين هذا السواد [ لكان متى حصل السواد فقد حصل هذا السواد ] ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل فيثبت أن قولنا : هذا السواد قد اشتمل على قيد ، لم يحصل في قولنا : السواد .
الثاني : إن قولنا : السواد ماهية لا يمنع نفس مفهومها عند وقوع الشركة فيها [ وقولنا : هذا السواد يمنع عن وقوع الشركة فيها ] ولولا التفاوت في الحقيقة ، وإلا لما حصل هذا التفاوت في هذا الحكم ، ولا شك أن المفهوم من السواد حاصل في المفهوم من قولنا : هذا السواد . فوجب أن يكون قولنا : هذا السواد قد اختص بقيد زائد على المفهوم من مجرد قولنا : السواد 1 وذلك هو المطلوب :
الثالث : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن المفهوم من قولنا : هذا السواد مفهوم مركب من قولنا هذا، ومن قولنا السواد. وحاكمة أيضاً بأن المفهوم من قولنا : هذا مغاير للمفهوم من قولنا : السواد . وذلك يدل على أن تعين المتعينات أمر زائد على الماهية .
وجهان :
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا المفهوم الزائد: أمر ثبوتي . ويدل عليه
الأول : إن هذا السواد من حيث إنه هذا السواد موجود . وقولنا : هذا ، جزء من مفهوم قولنا : هذا السواد والمعدوم يمتنع أن يكون جزءاً من . الموجود ، فوجب أن يكون المفهوم من قولنا : هذا : موجوداً .
والثاني : إن بتقدير أن يكون تعين هذا المتعين مفهوماً عدمياً ، فإنه لا يكون عبارة عن عدم أي شيء كان ، وإنما هو عبارة عن عدم تعين المتعين الآخر ، وذلك التعين الآخر إن كان عدماً ، كان هذا التعين عدماً للعـدم وعدم العدم ثبوت . فهذا التعين ثبوت . فيكون التعين الآخر ثبوتاً ، ضرورة أن التعينات أفراد ماهية واحدة فيكون حكمها بأسرها حكماً واحداً . وإن كان التعين الآخر ثبوتاً وجب أن يكون هذا التعين أيضاً ثبوتاً لعين ما ذكرناه
فثبت بهذه البيانات : أن تعين كل متعين : صفة موجودة زائدة على الذات ، وحيثنذ ينتظم الدليل المذكور ، لانا نقول : هذه الدلائل التي ذكرتم تفيد أن التعين صفة زائدة على الماهية . والدلائل التي ذكرناها تفيد أن الأمر ليس كذلك ، فحصل التعارض ، وبقي الشك [ ومع قيام هذا الشك
And let it not be said: The proof that determination is a positive concept is that we show that it is an additional concept to the essence, then we show that that addition must be a fixed thing. As for what indicates that it is an additional concept to the essence, there are aspects: The first: If there were no difference between 'blackness' and 'this blackness', then whenever blackness occurs, this blackness would occur, and it is known that that is false, so it is established that our saying 'this blackness' includes a restriction not present in our saying 'blackness'. The second: Our saying 'blackness' is a quiddity that does not prevent its concept from being shared, while our saying 'this blackness' prevents it from being shared. If there were no difference in reality, this difference in judgment would not occur. There is no doubt that the concept of blackness is present in the concept of our saying 'this blackness', so it is necessary that our saying 'this blackness' is distinguished by an additional restriction beyond the concept of mere 'blackness', and that is what is sought. The third: The intellectual intuition judges that the concept of our saying 'this blackness' is a composite concept of 'this' and 'blackness', and also judges that the concept of 'this' is different from the concept of 'blackness', and that indicates that the determination of determinate things is additional to the quiddity. Two proofs: If this is established, we say: This additional concept is a positive thing. Two proofs indicate it: The first: This blackness, insofar as it is this blackness, exists. Our saying 'this' is a part of the concept of our saying 'this blackness', and the non-existent cannot be part of the existent, so it is necessary that the concept of our saying 'this' is existent. The second: If it is supposed that the determination of this determinate thing is a negative concept, then it is not an expression of the negation of just any thing, but rather it is an expression of the negation of the determination of the other determinate thing. If that other determination is a negation, then this determination is a negation of negation, and the negation of negation is affirmation. So this determination is affirmative. Then the other determination must be affirmative, since determinations are individuals of one quiddity, so their ruling is one and the same. And if the other determination is affirmative, then this determination must also be affirmative for the very same reason. So by these evidences it is established that the determination of every determinate thing is an existent attribute additional to the essence. At that point the mentioned proof becomes coherent, for we say: These proofs that you have mentioned indicate that determination is an additional attribute to the quiddity, while the proofs we have mentioned indicate that it is not so, so conflict occurs and doubt remains. And with this doubt
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a structured objection-and-answer. The author raises the opponent's claim (via 'ولا يقال' meaning one should not say, i.e., the opponent might say) that determination is a positive concept (thubuti) that is additional. The opponent's full argument requires two steps: (1) proving determination is additional to the essence, and (2) proving that addition is positive. The author then presents the opponent's three proofs for step one: the first uses the difference between 'blackness' and 'this blackness' to show 'this blackness' includes an extra restrictive element; the second argues that 'this blackness' prevents shareability unlike 'blackness'; the third appeals to common sense that 'this' and 'blackness' are distinct concepts. Then the author presents the opponent's two proofs for step two: the first argues that since 'this blackness' exists, the part 'this' must exist (as non-existence cannot be part of existence); the second uses a negation-of-negation argument to show determination is affirmative. After presenting these, the author concludes that the opponent's evidences would establish determination as an additional existing attribute. However, the author then delivers the refutation: the opponent's proofs conflict with the author's earlier proofs (al-wajh al-rabi' and al-khamis), resulting in a contradiction and leaving the matter in doubt (shakk). The passage ends on this note of unresolved doubt, effectively undermining the opponent's position.

Key Terms
التعين
زائد
ماهية
سواد
قيد

Checkpoint
Which proof argues that 'this blackness' prevents shareability while 'blackness' does not?
A) The first proof
B) The second proof
C) The third proof
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The opponent presents three proofs—based on occurrence, shareability, and composition—to argue that determination is an additional concept to the quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I am just being myself' to excuse flaws. But this lesson shows the quiddity 'you' is shareable, yet your unique determination involves specific restrictions. What one restriction in your character have you been excusing as 'just me', and how will you actively remove it starting today?
?
The first proof implies that if determination were not added, every occurrence would be identical. In your worship, do you treat each prayer as a unique 'this prayer' or as a mere instance of 'prayer'? Commit to being fully present in one specific prayer tomorrow—which one?
?
The third proof calls 'this blackness' composite. Are you letting one determination—like your job, family role, or nationality—overshadow your essence as a servant of Allah? Write down one determination that has become your identity, and a concrete step to realign your identity with your essence.

Proofs that Determination is Positive and the Conflict
Two proofs for positivity and the resulting conflict.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's two proofs that determination is positive, and how this leads to a contradiction with earlier proofs, leaving the matter in doubt.

Core Idea
The opponent's argument collapses because it contradicts sound premises.

Having argued that determination is additional, the opponent now tries to prove that this addition is positive (thubuti). Two arguments are given, but the author then points out that these conclusions conflict with earlier proofs, resulting in a stalemate and doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read from 'إذا ثبت هذا فنقول' to the end. First, the opponent states the goal: to prove the addition is positive. Then two proofs follow.
The passage ends with 'فحصل التعارض وبقي الشك'.
2
Understand the first positivity proof
The argument: 'this blackness' exists; 'this' is part of its concept; the non-existent cannot be part of the existent, so 'this' must exist (i.e., be positive).
3
Understand the second positivity proof
Assume determination is negative (non-existence). Then it would be the negation of another determination. If that other is negative, then this determination is negation of negation, hence positive. By symmetry, all determinations are positive.
4
See the conclusion: conflict
The author states that these proofs conflict with earlier proofs (not shown here), leading to doubt. The opponent's argument is not accepted.

Individuation is an Additional Positive Attribute: Conceptual Proof
Author analyzes 'this blackness' to prove individuation is extra and positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analysis of 'this blackness' demonstrates that individuation (ta'ayyun) is a positive attribute added to the essence.

Core Idea
The concept of 'this blackness' proves that individuation is real and extra.

This lesson examines a key kalam argument about individuation. The author uses the simple phrase 'this blackness' to show that when we say 'this blackness', we are adding something beyond the mere concept of blackness. This addition, called ta'ayyun, is not merely verbal but a real positive attribute. We will walk through the conceptual analysis and the two proofs that this addition is existent (thubūtī).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the author's use of 'this blackness' as a starting point.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage begins by analyzing the concept of 'this blackness' to argue that 'this-ness' is an extra restriction beyond blackness.
3
Understand the two proofs
The passage gives two proofs (wajhān) that this extra concept is positive (thubūtī), not negative. Follow each proof step by step.
First proof: from existence. Second proof: from the negation of non-being.
4
See the dialectical conclusion
The author applies this to the unity proof, showing that if individuation is additional, it creates a contradiction that makes the unity argument doubtful.
This is a dialectical move to show the opponent's assumptions lead to uncertainty.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how the argument uses linguistic/conceptual analysis to prove an ontological point.
This method is common in kalam for establishing attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفهوم من السواد حاصل في المفهوم من قولنا : هذا السواد . فوجب أن يكون قولنا : هذا السواد قد اختص بقيد زائد على المفهوم من مجرد قولنا : السواد وذلك هو المطلوب : الثالث : إن بديهة العقل حاكمة بأن المفهوم من قولنا : هذا السواد مفهوم مركب من قولنا هذا، ومن قولنا السواد. وحاكمة أيضاً بأن المفهوم من قولنا : هذا مغاير للمفهوم من قولنا : السواد . وذلك يدل على أن تعين المتعينات أمر زائد على الماهية . وجهان : إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : هذا المفهوم الزائد: أمر ثبوتي . ويدل عليه الأول : إن هذا السواد من حيث إنه هذا السواد موجود . وقولنا : هذا ، جزء من مفهوم قولنا : هذا السواد والمعدوم يمتنع أن يكون جزءاً من الموجود ، فوجب أن يكون المفهوم من قولنا : هذا : موجوداً . والثاني : إن بتقدير أن يكون تعين هذا المتعين مفهوماً عدمياً ، فإنه لا يكون عبارة عن عدم أي شيء كان ، وإنما هو عبارة عن عدم تعين المتعين الآخر ، وذلك التعين الآخر إن كان عدماً ، كان هذا التعين عدماً للعدم وعدم العدم ثبوت . فهذا التعين ثبوت . فيكون التعين الآخر ثبوتاً ، ضرورة أن التعينات أفراد ماهية واحدة فيكون حكمها بأسرها حكماً واحداً . وإن كان التعين الآخر ثبوتاً وجب أن يكون هذا التعين أيضاً ثبوتاً لعين ما ذكرناه فثبت بهذه البيانات : أن تعين كل متعين : صفة موجودة زائدة على الذات ، وحيثنذ ينتظم الدليل المذكور ، لانا نقول : هذه الدلائل التي ذكرتم تفيد أن التعين صفة زائدة على الماهية . والدلائل التي ذكرناها تفيد أن الأمر ليس كذلك ، فحصل التعارض ، وبقي الشك [ ومع قيام هذا الشك صار الدليل المذكور في توحيد واجب الوجود مشكوكا ، لأن الموقوف على المشكوك مشكوك
The concept of blackness is contained in the concept of our saying 'this blackness'. So it follows that our saying 'this blackness' has been distinguished by an additional restriction (qayd zā'id) beyond the concept from merely our saying 'blackness'. That is what is sought. Third: The intuition of reason (badīhat al-'aql) judges that the concept of our saying 'this blackness' is a compound concept from our saying 'this' and from our saying 'blackness'. And it also judges that the concept of our saying 'this' is different from the concept of our saying 'blackness'. And that indicates that the individuation (ta'ayyun) of individuated things is something additional to the essence (māhiyya). Two aspects: If this is established, we say: this additional concept is a positive thing (amr thubūtī). And it is indicated by: First: This blackness, inasmuch as it is this blackness, exists. And our saying 'this' is a part of the concept of our saying 'this blackness', and the non-existent cannot be a part of the existent. So it follows that the concept of our saying 'this' exists. Second: If it is supposed that the individuation of this individuated thing is a non-being concept (mafhūm 'adamī), then it is not an expression of the non-being of anything whatsoever, but rather it is an expression of the non-being of the individuation of the other individuated thing. And that other individuation, if it is non-being, then this individuation is non-being of non-being, and non-being of non-being is positive (thubūt). So this individuation is positive. Then the other individuation would be positive, necessarily because individuations are individuals of one essence, so their ruling is entirely one ruling. And if the other individuation is positive, then it is necessary that this individuation also be positive, for the same reason we mentioned. So it is established by these proofs that the individuation of every individuated thing is an existent attribute (ṣifa mawjūda) additional to the essence. And then the mentioned proof is invalidated, because we say: these proofs which you mentioned indicate that individuation is an attribute additional to the essence. And the proofs we mentioned indicate that the matter is not so. So contradiction occurs, and doubt remains. And with the existence of this doubt, the mentioned proof for the unity of the Necessary Being becomes doubtful, because that which depends on the doubtful is doubtful.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a proof that individuation (ta'ayyun) is an additional positive attribute beyond the essence. The author begins by analyzing the concept of 'this blackness' (hādhā al-sawād). He argues that the concept of 'blackness' is already contained in 'this blackness', but 'this blackness' adds an extra restriction (qayd zā'id) — the 'this-ness' (al-hādhā). This shows that individuation is something over and above the essence. Then he presents two proofs (wajhān) that this additional concept is positive (thubūtī), not merely negative. The first proof: since 'this blackness' exists, and 'this' is part of its concept, the part cannot be non-existent; hence 'this' exists. The second proof: supposing individuation were a non-being concept (mafhūm 'adamī), it would be the negation of another individuation; but if that other individuation were non-being, then this individuation would be the negation of non-being, which is positive; if the other is positive, then this must also be positive because all individuations of a single essence share the same ontological status. Therefore, every individuation is an existent attribute (ṣifa mawjūda) added to the essence. The author then applies this to the earlier proof for God's unity: if individuation is additional, the opponent's proofs say it is extra; our proofs say it is not; a contradiction arises, making the unity argument doubtful. This is a dialectical move to show that the opponent's own assumptions lead to uncertainty.

Key Terms
تعين (ta'ayyun)
قيد زائد (qayd zā'id)
ثبوتي (thubūtī)
عدم العدم ('adam al-'adam)
صفة موجودة (ṣifa mawjūda)
ماهية (māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'this-ness' is merely a verbal distinction.
Correction: The text argues it is an ontological addition, proven by conceptual analysis and the impossibility of a non-existent part.

Checkpoint
What does the concept of 'this blackness' (هذا السواد) prove about individuation?
A) That it is identical to the essence of blackness.
B) That it is an additional concept beyond blackness.
C) That it is a purely negative concept.
D) That it is a subjective mental construct.

Key Takeaway
Individuation is a real positive attribute added to the essence, not merely a mental concept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that even a simple phrase like 'this blackness' contains profound ontological structure. How often do you overlook the deep realities behind everyday concepts? What would change if you habitually thought to yourself, 'What makes this thing *this* one?' — especially when it comes to your own existence?
?
The lesson ends by noting that if a proof leads to contradiction, the whole argument for God's unity becomes doubtful. Have you ever held a belief that, upon deeper analysis, turned out to rely on assumptions you can't defend? Are you willing to revisit your foundational convictions, even if it feels unsettling?
?
The second proof shows that even if we try to reduce individuation to a negation, we end up with a positive reality. This means there is something about you that cannot be reduced to what you are not. In what ways do you define yourself by negations (e.g., 'I am not like them')? How might you instead affirm your identity as a positive gift from God?

Second Argument: Impossibility of Disagreement in Will
Presents the second proof that two gods cannot have conflicting wills.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second rational proof for why two gods cannot have conflicting wills.

Core Idea
Perfect knowledge prevents conflicting wills.

This passage presents a second argument against the possibility of multiple gods. It uses a dichotomy: either one side is more beneficial or not. In both cases, the gods' perfect knowledge leads them to the same will, making disagreement impossible. Read on to see the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: a dichotomy with two cases.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the initial dichotomy
Find the two possibilities: either one side is more preponderant in benefit, or not. This sets up the whole argument.
3
Trace the logic for each case
Follow the reasoning for both 'preponderant' and 'not preponderant'. Notice how knowledge leads to will in each.
In the second case, equality prevents willing because an act without a preponderant factor is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that on all estimations, disagreement is impossible. Ask yourself: why does perfect knowledge force agreement?
This highlights a key attribute of divinity: omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : وهو أنه إما أن يكون أحد الجانبين أرجح في كونه مصلحة من الجانب الآخر، أو لا يكون. فإن كان أحد الجانيين أرجح كان كل واحد من الألمين عالماً بذلك الرجحان ، فوجب أن يكون كل واحد منهما مريداً لذلك الجانب. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يمتنع اختلافهما في الإرادة ، وأما إن لم يكن أحد الجانبين أرجح ، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد منهما. عالماً بحصول المساواة في الطرفين ، والفعل بدون المرجح محال ، فحينئذ يكون كل واحد منها عالماً بأن الترجيح محال ههنا . ومع هذا العلم يمتنع أن يريد ترجيحه : فيثبت أن على جميع التقديرات يمتنع أن يخالف أحد الإلهين الثاني في القصد والإرادة
The second aspect: That either one of the two sides is more preponderant in being a benefit than the other, or not. If one of the two sides is more preponderant, then each of the two gods knows that preponderance, so each of them must will that side. On this estimation, their disagreement in will is impossible. But if neither side is more preponderant, then each of them knows that equality obtains between the two sides, and an act without a preponderant factor is impossible, so each of them knows that preponderance is impossible here. With this knowledge, it is impossible for him to will preponderance. Thus it is established that on all estimations, it is impossible for one of the two gods to oppose the other in intention and will.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the impossibility of two gods having conflicting wills. It begins by stating a dichotomy: either one side is preponderant in benefit over the other, or not. If it is preponderant, both gods know that preponderance and therefore both will that side, so no conflict can arise. If neither side is preponderant, then both gods know that the two sides are equal, and since an act without a preponderant factor is impossible, neither can will preponderance. Thus, given their perfect knowledge, they cannot will differently. The conclusion is that in all cases, disagreement in intention and will is impossible.

Key Terms
الرجحان
المرجح
العلم
الإرادة
المخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if the sides are equal, a god could arbitrarily will one side without a reason.
Correction: The argument states that an act without a preponderant factor is impossible (especially for a perfect being), so willing arbitrarily is not possible.

Checkpoint
According to the second argument, if one side is more preponderant, what follows?
A) Both gods will that side.
B) Each god wills a different side.
C) Neither god wills anything.
D) One god wills that side and the other opposes.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that whether a side is preponderant or not, the gods' perfect knowledge and rational nature force them to have the same will, making disagreement impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God's perfect knowledge leaves no room for conflict or confusion in His will, would you still hesitate to surrender your plans to Him when they clash with His commands?
?
In what specific area of your life have you been acting as if God's will might be divided or uncertain, and what change does this argument demand of you starting today?
?
This proof shows that a being with all knowledge cannot will arbitrarily. How often do you pray as if you need to persuade God or catch His attention — and how would your prayer change if you fully trusted that His will is already single and purposeful?
