Third Argument: Knowledge Prevents Willing Opposition
Third argument: knowledge prevents willing opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational argument that two gods cannot oppose each other: each god's knowledge of the impossibility of successful opposition prevents him from even willing it.

Core Idea
Knowledge of impossibility prevents the very will to oppose.

This lesson explains the third and final argument in a series of proofs that two equally powerful gods cannot be in conflict. Unlike arguments based on power or will, this one focuses on the role of knowledge: knowing that opposition is futile eliminates the desire to oppose. The passage is concise but reveals a deep insight into divine psychology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the argument's structure.
The passage is brief but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the key premise
What does each god know? How does that knowledge affect his will?
The knowledge is that if he tried to oppose, executing his will would be impossible.
3
Trace the logical flow
From the premise of knowledge to the prevention of willing opposition. Then see how it contributes to the overall conclusion.
This is the third of three arguments.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider whether knowledge truly prevents willing. This is a psychological claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن علم كل واحد منهما بأنه لو حاول المخالفة لتعذر عليه تنفيذ مراده يمنعه من إرادة المخالفة. فهذه الوجوه الثلاثة دالة على امتناع حصول المخالفة بينهما
The third: Each one's knowledge that if he attempted opposition, executing his will would be impossible for him, prevents him from willing opposition. Thus these three aspects indicate the impossibility of opposition occurring between them.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This is the third, brief argument. Each god knows that if he tried to oppose the other, he would be unable to carry out his will because the other is equally powerful. This knowledge itself prevents him from even willing opposition. So, together with the previous two arguments, these three aspects prove that opposition between two gods cannot occur.

Correction: The argument holds that knowledge of impossibility prevents the very act of willing opposition.

Checkpoint

B) It prevents him from willing opposition.
C) It prevents him from knowing the other's will.
D) It prevents him from existing.

Key Takeaway
A god's knowledge that opposition is impossible prevents him from even willing it, reinforcing the conclusion that two gods cannot be in conflict.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Fourth Objection: The Preventer as Knowledge
Objection and answer about why each god cannot achieve his will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth objection against the existence of two gods is answered by positing each god's knowledge as the preventer of simultaneous willing, avoiding contradiction.

Core Idea
Each god's own knowledge prevents the conflict, not the other's will.

The fourth objection attempts to sidestep the earlier reasoning by asking why each god could not simply be incapable of achieving his will. The answer reveals a deeper point: the true preventer is not the other's will but each god's knowledge that the other would intervene. This subtle shift preserves the argument against multiple gods without falling into logical contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the question posed by the objector carefully. Note that it asks why each god cannot simply be prevented from achieving his will.
2
Identify the opponent's reply
Notice how the opponent (the defender of tawheed) first states a potential difficulty: if the preventer were the other's will, then both wills being impossible would force both wills to occur, which is absurd.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer rejects that the preventer is the other's will. Instead, it asserts that each god's knowledge that the other would prevent him is the actual preventer. Understand why this avoids the contradiction and strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع : لم لا يجوز أن يتعذر على كل واحد منهما تحصيل مراده ؟ قوله : ( المانع لكل واحد منهما عن تحصيل مراده ، هو حصول مراد الثاني ، فلو امتنع المرادان معا ، لوجب حصول (۱) المرادين معاً . وأنه باطل ، قلنا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المانع لكل واحد منهما عن تحصيل مراده ، علمه بأنه لو حال تحصيل مراده ، يمنعه الآخر منه. فهذا العلم هو المانع فقط ، وحينئذ لا يلزم المحذور المذكور ؟
The fourth question: Why is it not permissible that it be impossible for each of them to achieve his will? His statement: 'The preventer for each of them from achieving his will is the occurrence of the will of the second. So if both wills were impossible, then the occurrence of both wills would be necessary, and that is invalid.' We say: Why is it not permissible to say: The preventer for each of them from achieving his will is his knowledge that if he attempted to achieve his will, the other would prevent him? So this knowledge alone is the preventer, and then the mentioned difficulty does not arise.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This is the fourth objection and its answer. The objector asks: Why can't each god simply find it impossible to achieve his will? The opponent points out that if the preventer were the other's will occurring, then if both wills were impossible (neither occurs), the wills would have to occur simultaneously—a contradiction. The answer rejects that premise: the true preventer is each god's knowledge that the other would prevent him. This knowledge alone prevents him from willing, so no contradiction arises. The objector's difficulty is thus avoided.

Key Terms
المانع
تحصيل المراد
حصول مراد
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the preventer is the other god's will or an external obstacle.
Correction: The correct view is that the preventer is each god's own knowledge that the other would prevent him, making the obstacle internal.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fourth objection, what is the actual preventer for each god?
A) The occurrence of the other's will.
B) Each god's knowledge that the other would prevent him.
C) The impossibility of both wills occurring.
D) The lack of a preponderant factor.

Key Takeaway
The preventer in the fourth objection is each god's knowledge of the other's potential interference, not the other's will itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your knowledge tells you that a certain action will displease Allah, does that knowledge prevent you from pursuing it as effectively as the hypothetical gods' knowledge prevented them? What stops you from letting your knowledge govern your will?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's knowledge of your actions is complete and that He will hold you accountable, would you still commit sins in private? How can you make this knowledge the actual preventer in your life starting today?
?
The hypothetical gods acted consistently with their perfect knowledge. Where in your life do you act inconsistently with what you know to be true about Allah, His attributes, and His commands? What concrete step will you take today to align your will with your knowledge?

The Time Analogy: Preponderance Without a Preponderant
Objection and extended answer using analogy of creation at a specific time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector's claim is refuted using the analogy of God's choice of a specific time for creation.

Core Idea
If God can choose a time without a reason, He can prefer one god's act without a preponderant.

The fifth objection tries to force the opponent into accepting that a free agent can choose without a preponderant factor. The answer cleverly turns the tables: the same logic applies to God's creation at a specific time. This lesson unpacks the layered response, including the infinite regress argument that closes the door on alternative explanations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening question: why can't one god's will occur without the other? The opponent says this requires preponderance without a preponderant. Identify the core tension.
The objector uses the principle of sufficient reason.
2
Examine the time analogy
The answer cites God's creation of the world at a particular time without any property of that time being the cause. This is accepted as a free choice. The same should apply to the gods.
The analogy is the heart of the refutation.
3
Follow the counter-argument and refutation
The opponent might say the time had a beneficial property. The text then divides possibilities: if times are equal, the problem remains; if unequal, the property comes from the time itself (making time a creator), from an external agent, or by free choice. Each leads to a dead end or infinite regress.
Trace the logical divisions carefully.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
If the agent's choice is free without a preponderant, then the objector's original scenario is permissible: one god's act occurs without a reason, and the objection fails.
The regress argument forces acceptance of free choice sans preponderant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : يقع مراد أحدهما دون الآخر ؟ قوله : وإن هذا يقتضى حصول الرجحان لا المرجح ، وأنه باطل ، قلنا : أليس أن العالم لما كان محدثاً ، وجب الاعتراف بأنه تعالى خصص إحداثيه بوقت معين دون سائر الأوقات من غير مخصص اختص به ذلك الوقت ؟ فإذا جاز هذا ، فلم لا يجوز رجحان أحدهما على الآخر لا المرجح ؟ فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه تعالى خصص إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين لأجل أن ذلك الوقت أقرب إلى إصلاح المكلفين ؟ فنقول : الدليل على فساد هذا القسم ، أن نقول : الأوقات إما أن تكون متساوية في جميع الأمور المعتبرة في الخلق ، وإما أن لا تكون متساوية جميع فإذا كان الأول فالإشكال لازم ، وإن كان الثاني فنقول : اختصاص ذلك الوقت المعين بتلك الخاصية ، إما أن يكون لذاته، أو لشيء من لوازم ذاته ، أو للفاعل المنفصل . فإذا كان الأول أو الثاني فقد جوزتم أن يكون للوقت المعين أثر في اقتضاء حصول تلك الخاصية ، وإذا عقل ذلك ، فلم لا يعقل أن يقال : إن ذلك الوقت هو الذي اقتضى حدوث العالم ؟ وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يمكن الاستدلال بحدوث العالم على إثبات الصانع، وإن كان الثالث عاد التقسيم المذكور في أن ذلك الفاعل [ المباين ، لم (۱) ] خصص ذلك الوقت المعين بتلك الخاصية ؟ فإن كان ذلك لأجل اختصاص ذلك الوقت بخاصية أخرى ، عاد التقسيم فيه ، ولزم التسلسل . وإن لم يكن لأجل اختصاص ذلك الوقت بشيء من الخواص، فحينئذ قد جوزتم في الفاعل المختار أن يرجع أحد مقدوريه على الآخر لمرجح ، فإذا جوزتم ذلك [ فلم لا يجوز أيضاً أن أحد الألمين يقع مقدوره ، والإله الثاني لا يقع مقدوره (۲) ] مع أنه لا يكون أحدهما مخصوصاً بما لأجله حصل الرجحان ؟
The fifth question: Why is it not permissible to say: The will of one of them occurs without the other? His statement: 'This requires the occurrence of preponderance without a preponderant factor, and that is invalid.' We say: Is it not that since the world is originated, we must acknowledge that He, exalted, specified its origination at a particular time to the exclusion of all other times without a specific attribute distinguishing that time? If this is permissible, then why is it not permissible for one of them to be preponderant over the other without a preponderant factor? If they say: Why is it not permissible to say that He specified the origination of the world at the particular time because that time is closer to the reform of those obliged? Then we say: The proof of the corruption of this type is that we say: Times are either equal in all matters considered in creation, or they are not equal. If the first, the problem remains. If the second, we say: The specification of that particular time with that characteristic is either due to its essence, or to something from its essential accidents, or due to a separate agent. If the first or second, then you have permitted that the particular time has an effect in necessitating the occurrence of that characteristic, and if that is conceivable, why is it not conceivable to say that that time necessitated the occurrence of the world? On this estimation, it is not possible to infer from the occurrence of the world the proof of the Maker. And if the third, the aforementioned division returns concerning that agent: did he specify that particular time with that characteristic? If that was due to the time's specification with another characteristic, the division recurs and an infinite regress follows. And if not due to the time's specification with any characteristic, then you have permitted in the free agent to prefer one of his possibilities over another without a preponderant factor. So if you permit that, then why do you not also permit that one of the gods has his possibility occur and the second does not, while neither is specially distinguished by something for which preponderance occurred?
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This is the fifth objection and its lengthy answer. The objector asks: Why can't one god's will occur and not the other's? The opponent (original argument) says that would require preponderance without a preponderant factor, which is impossible. The answer first uses an analogy: God created the world at a specific time without any inherent property of that time making it special. If God's choice without a preponderant factor is accepted there, then it should be accepted here. The answer then considers a counter-argument that the time might have some beneficial property (closer to reform). It disproves this by dividing possibilities: if times are equal, the problem remains; if unequal, the property must come from the time's essence, its accidents, or an external agent. If from the time itself, then the time would be the creator—which negates the need for a divine maker. If from an external agent, we ask what specified that agent's choice, leading to either an infinite regress or an admission that a free agent can prefer without a preponderant factor. In the latter case, the same principle applies to the gods: one can occur without the other, without a special distinguishing attribute. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
الرجحان
المرجح
المخصص
الفاعل المختار
الوقت المعين
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That preponderance always requires a preponderant factor (tarijih bi la murajjih is absolutely impossible).
Correction: The answer shows that a free agent's choice can be without a preponderant, as illustrated by God's selection of a specific time for creation.

Checkpoint
What is the main analogy used in the answer to the fifth objection?
A) The creation of the world at a specific time.
B) The equality of all times.
C) The impossibility of two gods.
D) The nature of eternal beings.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because God's own free choice of creation time without a preponderant serves as a precedent for allowing preponderance without a preponderant among gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you assumed that God's actions must have a reason you could understand. How does this lesson challenge that assumption? What would it mean for you to accept that God's choices are free and not bound by your logic?
?
If God's creation at a specific time had no preponderant factor, what does that imply about your own expectations of God's justice or mercy? Are you demanding reasons where God has simply chosen freely?
?
This argument shows that preponderance can occur without a preponderant. In your own life, where do you seek external 'preponderant factors' to justify your beliefs or actions? Could you instead rely on God's free choice as sufficient?

The Three-Premise Proof for Human Agency as Necessary Knowledge
Author enumerates three premises to prove agency knowledge is necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses three premises to demonstrate that knowledge of the servant as a creator of his actions is necessary (ḍarūrī) and not merely theoretical.

Core Idea
Self-evident moral judgments prove that human agency is known necessarily.

In this passage, the author constructs a rigorous argument to prove that the servant's role as creator of his own actions is known necessary knowledge. He begins by establishing a self-evident premise about praise and blame, then shows that this premise depends on recognizing the agent as a true agent, and finally argues that because the branch (moral knowledge) is necessary, the root (knowledge of agency) must also be necessary. This reasoning counters claims that human agency is merely theoretical.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three premises
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. Try to identify the three distinct premises the author lists. Notice how each premise is introduced with 'As for the first premise...' etc.
The first line has a garbled word; focus on the overall argument.
2
Understand the logic of each premise with the example
Focus on the example of the stone-thrower in the second premise. How does it illustrate that praise and blame depend on agency? How does the third premise use the idea that if the root were theoretical, doubt could arise in the branch?
The third premise uses a conditional: if the root is not necessary, doubt occurs in the branch, but the branch is certain, so the root must be necessary.
3
Follow the conclusion and see how it refutes the opposing view
The final sentences show that the argument defeats an opposing division that would deny the servant's creative role. See how the conclusion follows from the three premises.
The conclusion is that the division (which likely claimed agency is not necessary) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العبد فاهلا وموحداً، ومتى كان الفرع معلوماً بالضرورة ، كان الأصل أيضاً معلوماً بالضرورة ، فوجب أن يكون العلم بكون العبد موجداً علماً ضرورياً .
وهذه مقدمات لا بد من تقريرها
أما المقدمة الأولى : وهي قولنا : إن العلم الضروري حاصل بحسن المدح والذم . فتقريره : أن كل من أحسن إلى إنسان ، وأوصل إليه نوعاً من أنواع الخير ، فإن صريح عقله يحكم بحسن الشكر والمدح والثناء له ، وكل من آذاه وأوصل إليه نوعاً من أنواع الضرر ، فإن صريح عقله يحمله على أن يقول : لم فعلت هذا ؟ وبئس ما فعلت . والعلم بحسن هذا المدح والذم من أجلى العلوم الضرورية، والمنازع فيه مكابر في أجلى العلوم البديهية
·
وأما المقدمة الثانية : وهي في بيان أن العلم بحسن المدح والذم فرع على العلم بكونه فاعلاً. فالدليل عليه : أن من رمى وجه إنسان بحجر حتى شجّه، فإنه يجد من قبله ذمّ ذلك الرامي ، ولا يجد البنة ذم ذلك الحجر ولو قيل له : فرقت بينهما في هذا الحكم ؟ فكل عاقل يفرق بينهما بقوله : إن الرامي فعل هذا الفعل باختياره ، فأستحق المدح والذم . وأما الحجر فلا قدرة له على الفعل البتة . فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن صريح العقل حاكم بأن حسن المدح والذم فرع على العلم بكون الفاعل فاعلاً
وأما المقدمة الثالثة : وهي في بيان أن العلم بالفرع لما كان ضرورياً ، وجب أيضاً أن يكون العلم بالأصل ضرورياً . فالدليل عليه : أن الأصل إذا لم يكن معلوماً بالضرورة أمكن وقوع الشك فيه . وعند وقوع الشك في الأصل يلزم وقوع الشك في الفرع ، فيثبت : أن كون الأصل نظرياً ، يقتضى جواز وقوع الشك في الفرع ، ولما وللنا على أن وقوع الشك في هذا الفرع محال ، أعني العلم باستحقاق المدح والذم ، وجب أن يكون العلم بذلك الأصل ، أعني العلم بكون العبد موجداً غير قابل للشك . ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، كان ذلك معلوماً بالضرورة. فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن التقسيم الذي ذكرتم لوضح ، لزم القدح في كون العبد موجدا لأفعال نفسه ، وثبت أن هذا باطل ، فوجب أن يكون ذلك التقسيم باطلاً
The servant is [؟] and [؟], and whenever the branch is known necessarily, the root is also known necessarily, so it must be that knowledge that the servant is a creator is necessary knowledge. These are premises that must be established. As for the first premise: our statement that necessary knowledge exists regarding the goodness of praise and blame. Its establishment: every one who does good to a human and brings him some kind of good, his pure intellect judges the goodness of gratitude, praise, and commendation for him; and every one who harms him and brings him some kind of harm, his pure intellect impels him to say: Why did you do this? How bad is what you did. And knowledge of the goodness of this praise and blame is among the clearest of necessary sciences, and the one who disputes it is obstinate regarding the clearest of self-evident sciences. As for the second premise: it is in explaining that knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame is derivative upon knowledge that he is an agent. The proof for it: that one who throws a stone at a person's face and wounds him, he finds from himself blame of that thrower, and he does not find at all blame of that stone. If it were said to him: Did you distinguish between them in this judgment? Every rational person distinguishes between them by saying: The thrower did this act by his choice, so he deserves praise and blame. As for the stone, it has no ability to act at all. So it is established by what we mentioned that pure reason judges that the goodness of praise and blame is derivative upon knowledge that the agent is an agent. As for the third premise: it is in explaining that since knowledge of the branch is necessary, it must also be that knowledge of the root is necessary. The proof for it: that if the root is not known necessarily, then doubt could occur in it. And when doubt occurs in the root, doubt occurs in the branch necessarily. So it is established that the root being theoretical necessitates the possibility of doubt in the branch. And since we have argued that the occurrence of doubt in this branch is impossible, namely knowledge of deserving praise and blame, it must be that knowledge of that root, namely knowledge that the servant is a creator, is not susceptible to doubt. And when the matter is thus, it is known necessarily. So it is established by what we mentioned that the division you mentioned [if it were correct], would impugn the fact that the servant is a creator of his own actions, and it is established that this is false, so it must be that that division is false.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents a proof composed of three premises to establish that knowledge of the servant as the creator of his own actions is necessary knowledge. The author begins by stating the conclusion: if the branch (the effect) is known necessarily, then the root (the cause) must also be known necessarily, so knowledge that the servant is a creator is necessary. He then says that these premises must be established. The first premise: necessary knowledge exists regarding the goodness of praise and blame. He explains that every rational person naturally judges that doing good deserves praise and doing harm deserves blame, and this is self-evident; anyone who denies it is obstinate. The second premise: this knowledge of praise and blame is derivative upon knowledge that the agent is truly an agent. He provides an example: if someone throws a stone and wounds another, we blame the thrower, not the stone, because the stone has no ability to act. Thus reason judges that praise and blame depend on the knowledge that the agent is acting by choice. The third premise: if the branch (knowledge of praise/blame) is necessary, then the root (knowledge of the servant as creator) must also be necessary. He argues: if the root were theoretical (open to doubt), then doubt could arise in the branch; but since we have argued that doubt in the branch is impossible (the goodness of praise and blame is self-evident), the root cannot be doubtful and must be necessary. He concludes that any division that would deny the servant's role as creator is false.

Key Terms

حسن المدح والذم
فرع
أصل
فاعل
موجد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is circular because it uses the conclusion to prove itself.
Correction: The argument is not circular. It starts from a self-evident moral judgment (praise and blame) which is independent of the conclusion. Then it shows that this judgment logically implies that the agent is a true agent (second premise) and that this agency must be known necessarily (third premise). The conclusion follows from the premises, not the other way around.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise of the proof?
A) Necessary knowledge of the goodness of praise and blame exists.
B) Knowledge of praise and blame is derivative upon knowledge that the agent is an agent.
C) If the root is theoretical, doubt occurs in the branch.
D) The servant is a creator of his own actions.

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of moral praise and blame necessitates necessary knowledge of human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof argues that denying human agency would collapse moral accountability. In your own life, where do you find yourself subtly denying your own agency to avoid blame? Examine one instance where you excused your sin by saying 'I had no choice.' How does this lesson challenge that excuse?
?
You claim to believe that humans are responsible for their actions. Yet, do you live as if that is necessary knowledge, certain and undeniable? Or do you sometimes act as if your actions are determined by circumstances? What would it mean for you to truly internalize this truth?

Possibility Suffices: The Key Premise
The author answers the third question by proving that possibility does not entail impossibility when assumed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses the principle that assuming the occurrence of a possible thing does not lead to impossibility to refute the objection that the proof against two gods requires actual disagreement.

Core Idea
Possibility alone suffices; actual occurrence need not be proven.

This lesson tackles a key objection in the proof against two gods. The objector demands proof that disagreement actually occurs between them. But the author shows that if disagreement is possible, assuming its occurrence does not yield an impossibility, so the proof holds without proving actual occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection.
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objector's claim: consequences only follow from actual occurrence, not mere possibility. This sets up the author's response.
The objection is the starting point of the argument.
2
Understand the key premise.
The author introduces a certain premise: for anything possible, assuming its occurrence does not entail impossibility. Reflect on why this is a rational principle.
This premise is foundational for the rest of the argument.
3
See how the premise is proved.
The author proves the premise by contradiction: if assuming the occurrence of a possible thing led to impossibility, the thing would be impossible to occur, contradicting its possibility. This establishes the premise.
The proof is a simple contradiction argument.
4
Apply the premise to the two gods case.
Since disagreement between two gods is possible (not impossible in itself), assuming its occurrence does not lead to impossibility. Therefore, the consequences (contradictions) follow from the assumption, and the proof is valid without needing actual occurrence.
This shows that possibility alone is sufficient for the proof.
5
Reflect on the significance.
Understand why this strengthens the overall argument: it removes the burden of proving actual disagreement, making the proof more robust.
This is a common move in kalam: using rational necessity instead of empirical evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله في السؤال الثالث : وإن هذه المحاولات إنما لزمت من وقوع المخالفة ، لا من إمكان حصول المخالفة. فما الدليل على وقوع المخالفة ؟ فنقول في الجواب : ههنا مقدمة يقينية وهي أن كل ما كان ممكناً ، فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال ، إذ لو لزم من فرض وقوعه محال ، لوجب أن يكون محال الوقوع ، مع أنا قد فرضناه ممكن الوقوع ، وهذا يفضي إلى الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . فيثبت : أن كل ما كان ممكناً فإنه لا يلزم من فرض وقوعه محال البتة. إذا عرفت هذا . فنقول : إنا إذا فرضنا وجود الإلهين، وفرضنا حصول هذه المخالفة بعد أن أقمنا الدلالة على أن هذه المخالفة ليست ممتنعة لذاتها وعينها ، ثم رأينا أن المحاولات قد لزمت . فنقول : هذه ...
And as for his statement in the third question: 'These consequences only follow from the actual occurrence of disagreement, not from the possibility of the occurrence of disagreement. So what is the proof that disagreement actually occurs?' We say in answer: Here is a certain premise, namely that everything that is possible, it does not follow from assuming its occurrence that it is impossible. For if from assuming its occurrence an impossibility followed, it would have to be impossible of occurrence, whereas we have assumed it possible of occurrence, and this leads to the combination of two contradictories, which is impossible. So it is established that everything that is possible is such that no impossibility at all follows from assuming its occurrence. If you understand this, we say: When we assume the existence of two gods, and we assume the occurrence of this disagreement after we have established the proof that this disagreement is not impossible in itself and in its essence, then we see that the consequences have followed. We say: these...
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage addresses the third question. The objector argues that the consequences (the impossible results) only follow from the actual occurrence of disagreement, not merely from the possibility of disagreement. So the proponent must prove that disagreement actually occurs between the two gods. The author replies by stating a certain premise: if something is possible in itself, then assuming it occurs does not lead to an impossibility. Why? Because if assuming its occurrence led to an impossibility, that would mean the thing itself is impossible to occur, contradicting the assumption that it is possible—a contradiction. Therefore, for any possible thing, assuming it occurs does not yield impossibility. Applying this: disagreement between two gods is possible (as proven earlier, it is not impossible in itself). So assuming its occurrence does not entail impossibility. Therefore, the consequences (the contradictions) that follow from the assumption of two gods and disagreement are valid, proving that the assumption itself is impossible. Thus the proof stands without needing to demonstrate actual occurrence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof against two gods requires demonstrating that disagreement actually occurs between them.
Correction: The author shows that if disagreement is possible (not impossible in itself), then assuming its occurrence is valid and leads to the consequences, so actual occurrence does not need to be proven.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise used to refute the third question?
A) Disagreement between two gods is impossible in itself.
B) If something is possible, assuming it occurs cannot lead to impossibility.
C) The actual occurrence of disagreement must be proven for the proof to work.
D) The consequences only follow if disagreement is actual, not possible.

Key Takeaway
For any possible thing, assuming its occurrence does not entail impossibility; thus the proof against two gods does not require actual disagreement.

Refutation of the Preponderance Objection
Razi refutes the first two objections regarding preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that a single agent can choose without a preponderant does not apply to the case of two gods.

Core Idea
A single agent's choice without preponderant is possible because he can do the opposite; two gods cannot.

The objector raises two interconnected challenges against the proof of divine unity. First, why cannot one god's will occur without the other? Second, does not a single powerful agent sometimes choose without an external preponderant? The response shows why these analogies fail. This lesson unpacks the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic and translation for the first objection and its answer. Notice the two parts of the objection.
2
Identify the logical flaw
The objector assumes that the same rule applies to a single agent and to two agents. The answer explains the difference: a single agent can do the opposite, but two gods cannot.
The key is the concept of doing the opposite (فعل ضده).
3
Reflect on the concept of preponderant
Understand why an event without a preponderant (murjīḥ) is impossible. This is a basic principle of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله : « لم لا يجوز وقوع مراد أحدهما دون الثاني ، ؟ قلنا : لأنه يقتضي وقوع أحد الجائزين من غير مرجح وقوعاً على سبيل الوجوب ، وأنه محال وقوله : ( أليس أن القادر قد يرجح أحد الجانبين على الآخر لا المرجح ؟ فلم لا يجوز مثله ههنا : ؟ قلنا : الذين يقولون بجواز ذلك ، قالوا : منه فعل ضده . وههنا لا يصح منه ذلك يصح . إن ذلك إلما يجوز بشرط أن فيكون ذلك قدحاً في كون القادر قادراً . أما قوله : « إن هذا التقسيم يقدح في كون العبد موجدا لفعل نفسه ، فنقول : هذا السؤال يتعلق بمسألة الجبر والقدر. والكلام المستقصى فيه سيأتي إن شاء الله. أما قوله : « إن هذا الدليل [ لا يدل ] على امتناع وجود [ الإلهين اللذين يكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على بعض المقدورات دون البعض ، ولا يدل على وجود ] موجود ثان ، لا يقدر على شيء أصلاً ، سواء قيل : إنه حي ، أو ليس بحي ، قلنا : مقصودنا من هذا الدليل ليس إلا بيان امتناع القول بوجود الإلهين كاملي القدرة فقط . [ والله أعلم] )
As for his statement: 'Why is it not permissible for the will of one of them to occur without the second?' We said: Because it would necessitate the occurrence of one of two possibilities without a preponderant, and that is impossible. And his statement: 'Does not the powerful agent (al-qādir) sometimes preponderate one of the two sides without a preponderant? So why is it not permissible like that here?' We said: Those who deem that permissible said: because he does the opposite of it. And here it is not valid for him to do that. That is [not?] permissible on condition that it would be a defect in the powerful agent's being powerful. As for his statement: 'This division (taqsīm) impugns the servant's being the originator of his own action,' we say: This question pertains to the issue of predestination (jabr) and free will (qadar). The thorough discussion of it will come, God willing. As for his statement: 'This proof does not indicate the impossibility of existence of two gods such that each of them is powerful over some of the possibles without others, nor does it indicate the existence of a second existing thing that has no power over anything at all, whether it is said to be alive or not alive,' we said: Our purpose in this proof is nothing but to demonstrate the impossibility of asserting the existence of two fully capable gods. [And God knows best]
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a set of objections and answers pertaining to the first proof of God's oneness. The author addresses three distinct objections raised against the earlier demonstration that two omnipotent gods cannot coexist.

First objection: Why cannot one god's will be realized without the other's? The answer is that such an occurrence would require a preponderant (murjīḥ) to tilt the scales between two equally possible events, but by hypothesis there is no preponderant, so it is impossible.

Second objection: A single powerful agent (qādir) sometimes chooses one of two alternatives without any external preponderant; why cannot the same happen with two gods? The answer distinguishes: those who allow such preponderance in a single agent do so because the agent can do the opposite action, which is not possible here (since both are acting on the same thing). If it were allowed, it would impugn the agent's being truly powerful.

Third objection: The proof against two omnipotent gods does not rule out a lesser deity with limited power. The author concedes the point: the proof was intended only to refute the existence of two fully capable gods, not all conceptions of secondary deities.

The author also defers discussion of human free will to a later section, noting that the objection about predestination is separate from the present argument.

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
مرجح (murjīḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a single agent can choose without a preponderant, the same applies to two gods.
Correction: The single agent can do the opposite of the chosen action, which preserves the possibility of the alternative. In the case of two gods acting on the same object, the opposite action is not possible because both agents are acting simultaneously on the same thing, making it impossible for one to do the opposite without conflict.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation of the first objection, why is it impossible for one god's will to occur without the other?
A) Because both gods are equal in power, so they must act together.
B) Because it would require a preponderant (murjīḥ) to choose one possibility over another, which is absent.
C) Because the second god would necessarily prevent the first from acting.
D) Because the first god cannot act without the permission of the second.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two omnipotent gods is grounded in the necessity of a preponderant for any contingent event; the analogy with a single agent's choice fails because a single agent can do the opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If it is impossible for two gods to exist, then what does that mean for the things you rely on besides Allah—your wealth, status, or plans? Are you living as if they have independent power, or do you truly see them as subordinate to His will? How can you realign your heart today?
?
The analogy fails because a single agent can do the opposite of his choice. Does this description of true power resonate with your understanding of Allah? In what areas of your life do you still doubt that He alone is fully capable, and that nothing can counter His will?
?
The principle that every event needs a preponderant points to Allah as the sole cause. Think of a recent event that upset or delighted you. Did you attribute it to a secondary cause as if it were independent, or did you see Allah's hand behind it? What practical step can you take to deepen your reliance on Him alone?

Refutation of the Objection Regarding Human Free Will
Razi defers the objection about human free will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the proof of divine unity does not directly address the issue of human free will, which is a separate theological question.

Core Idea
The proof of tawhid and the question of free will are distinct inquiries.

The objector raises a concern: does not the argument against two gods imply something about human agency? Razi acknowledges this is a different issue, belonging to the debate on predestination (jabr) and free will (qadar), and defers its discussion. This lesson clarifies why the proof is not affected.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
The objector says the proof's division (taqsīm) impugns the servant's being the originator of his own action. The response is a brief deferral.
2
Understand the deferral
Razi states this question pertains to the issue of jabr and qadar, which will be discussed later. This indicates that the proof of tawhid does not directly engage with free will.
It is important to keep theological discussions separate.
3
Reflect on the scope of the proof
The proof only addresses the impossibility of two fully capable gods; it does not deny human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله : « لم لا يجوز وقوع مراد أحدهما دون الثاني ، ؟ قلنا : لأنه يقتضي وقوع أحد الجائزين من غير مرجح وقوعاً على سبيل الوجوب ، وأنه محال وقوله : ( أليس أن القادر قد يرجح أحد الجانبين على الآخر لا المرجح ؟ فلم لا يجوز مثله ههنا : ؟ قلنا : الذين يقولون بجواز ذلك ، قالوا : منه فعل ضده . وههنا لا يصح منه ذلك يصح . إن ذلك إلما يجوز بشرط أن فيكون ذلك قدحاً في كون القادر قادراً . أما قوله : « إن هذا التقسيم يقدح في كون العبد موجدا لفعل نفسه ، فنقول : هذا السؤال يتعلق بمسألة الجبر والقدر. والكلام المستقصى فيه سيأتي إن شاء الله. أما قوله : « إن هذا الدليل [ لا يدل ] على امتناع وجود [ الإلهين اللذين يكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على بعض المقدورات دون البعض ، ولا يدل على وجود ] موجود ثان ، لا يقدر على شيء أصلاً ، سواء قيل : إنه حي ، أو ليس بحي ، قلنا : مقصودنا من هذا الدليل ليس إلا بيان امتناع القول بوجود الإلهين كاملي القدرة فقط . [ والله أعلم] )
As for his statement: 'Why is it not permissible for the will of one of them to occur without the second?' We said: Because it would necessitate the occurrence of one of two possibilities without a preponderant, and that is impossible. And his statement: 'Does not the powerful agent (al-qādir) sometimes preponderate one of the two sides without a preponderant? So why is it not permissible like that here?' We said: Those who deem that permissible said: because he does the opposite of it. And here it is not valid for him to do that. That is [not?] permissible on condition that it would be a defect in the powerful agent's being powerful. As for his statement: 'This division (taqsīm) impugns the servant's being the originator of his own action,' we say: This question pertains to the issue of predestination (jabr) and free will (qadar). The thorough discussion of it will come, God willing. As for his statement: 'This proof does not indicate the impossibility of existence of two gods such that each of them is powerful over some of the possibles without others, nor does it indicate the existence of a second existing thing that has no power over anything at all, whether it is said to be alive or not alive,' we said: Our purpose in this proof is nothing but to demonstrate the impossibility of asserting the existence of two fully capable gods. [And God knows best]
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a set of objections and answers pertaining to the first proof of God's oneness. The author addresses three distinct objections raised against the earlier demonstration that two omnipotent gods cannot coexist.

First objection: Why cannot one god's will be realized without the other's? The answer is that such an occurrence would require a preponderant (murjīḥ) to tilt the scales between two equally possible events, but by hypothesis there is no preponderant, so it is impossible.

Second objection: A single powerful agent (qādir) sometimes chooses one of two alternatives without any external preponderant; why cannot the same happen with two gods? The answer distinguishes: those who allow such preponderance in a single agent do so because the agent can do the opposite action, which is not possible here (since both are acting on the same thing). If it were allowed, it would impugn the agent's being truly powerful.

Third objection: The proof against two omnipotent gods does not rule out a lesser deity with limited power. The author concedes the point: the proof was intended only to refute the existence of two fully capable gods, not all conceptions of secondary deities.

The author also defers discussion of human free will to a later section, noting that the objection about predestination is separate from the present argument.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr)
قدر (qadar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof against two gods denies human free will because it emphasizes divine causality.
Correction: Razi explicitly separates the two issues: the proof of tawhid is about divine unity, while free will is a separate topic discussed under jabr and qadar.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the objection that the proof impugns human free will?
A) He argues that the proof actually supports free will.
B) He says the question is unrelated and defers discussion.
C) He concedes that the proof weakens human agency.
D) He offers a detailed refutation of the objection.

Key Takeaway
The objection about human free will is acknowledged but set aside for separate treatment; the proof of tawhid does not engage that topic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a compelling argument for tawhid, do you ever quickly think of the free will issue to avoid fully accepting the proof? What would it take for you to examine each issue on its own terms instead of conflating them?
?
The objector in this passage raised a related but separate concern. Think of a time you used a valid point about something else to dismiss a truth you were uncomfortable with. Will you commit to facing each argument directly, even if it demands change?
?
Razi deferred the free will question for later. Is there a difficult question about your own life—like personal responsibility or divine decree—that you keep deferring because you fear where the answer might lead? What would it mean to actually seek the answer?

Refutation of the Objection Regarding Limited Deities
Razi limits his proof to fully capable gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first proof of tawhid only refutes the existence of two fully omnipotent gods, not any lesser deity.

Core Idea
The proof is specific to two fully capable gods; it does not rule out a limited secondary being.

A final objection argues that the proof does not eliminate the possibility of a god with limited power or a mere existing thing without power. Razi's response clarifies the precise scope of his argument, showing that theological proofs must be carefully defined.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector says the proof does not disprove the existence of two gods with limited power, nor the existence of a powerless second entity.
2
Read the response
Razi explicitly states that his proof only aims to show the impossibility of two fully capable gods. It does not address other conceptions.
3
Reflect on the precision of theological arguments
This shows that proofs in kalam are often targeted at specific claims; they do not overthrow all alternatives.

Structure of Razi's Second Proof
Razi states the proof hypothesis and enumerates three possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditional structure and the three possibilities that Razi uses to disprove the existence of two omnipotent gods.

Core Idea
Two omnipotent gods lead to a contradiction by any possible effect.

This lesson introduces the second proof from al-Matalib al-Aliyah, a classic kalam argument against polytheism. The proof proceeds by examining three logical possibilities for a single effect under two omnipotent agents, each of which is shown to be impossible. We start with the overall framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It sets up the hypothesis and lists three possibilities for any effect.
Notice the conditional starting with 'law qaddarnā'.
2
Identify the conditional
The hypothesis is: if two omnipotent gods exist, then each has power over the same objects. The consequent is the impossibility of this situation.
3
Find the three possibilities
Locate the enumeration: by both, by one, by the other. These are the only logical options.
The author says all three are false; later lessons prove each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات أن إله العالم واحد بناء على أصول المتكلمين : أن نقول : لو قدرنا وجود إلهين ، قادرين على جميع الممكنات ، لزم القطع بكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على عين مقدور الآخر . وهذا محال . فالقول بوجود الإلهين محال . أما الشرطية فظاهرة ، وأما أن هذه الملازمة ممتنعة . فتقريره : أنه لما كان كل واحد منهما [ قادراً ] على جميع الممكنات ، فكل ما كان مقدوراً لهذا ، فهو بعينه مقدور لذاك . وإن وقع ذلك المقدور ، فإما أن يقع بهما معاً ، وإما أن يقع [ بواحد منهما ، واما أن يقع ] بأحدهما دون الثاني . والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فالقول بوجود الإلهين باطل. إنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع وقوعه بهما معاً. وذلك لأن هذا القادر لما كان مستقلاً بالإيقاع وصار ذلك الفعل مع هذا الفاعل واجب الوقوع به ، ووجوب وقوعه به يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الثاني ، ووجوب وقوعه بالثاني يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الأول . فإذا اجتمعا عليه ، لزم أن يكون وجوب وقوعه بكل واحد منها مانعاً من وقوعه بالثاني ، فيلزم أن يكون واقعاً بهما ، حال ما يمتنع كونه واقعاً بكل واحد منهما [ وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن لا يقع بواحد منهما ] لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذا ليس إلا وقوعه بذاك وبالضد . والأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر ، فلو امتنع بهما معاً ، لزم أن يقع بكل واحد منها حتى يكون وقوعه بهذا مانعاً من وقوعه بذاك وبالضد . وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون واقعاً بكل واحد منهما . فالحاصل : أن في القسم الأول : وقوعه بكل واحد منهما ، يمنع من وقوعه بهما . وفي الثاني عدم وقوعه بهما [ يقتضي وقوعه بهما ] من حيث أن الأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر ، والمؤثر في امتناع وقوعه بكل واحد منها هو وقوعـه بالآخر . وإنما يمتنع وقوعه بأحدهما دون الثاني ، لأنهما لما استويا في التأثير وفي القوة ، كان وقوعه ، بأحدهما دون الآخر رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وهو محال .
The second proof for establishing that the God of the world is one, based on the principles of the theologians (al-mutakallimīn): We say: If we suppose the existence of two gods, both powerful over all possibles, then it necessarily follows that each of them is powerful over exactly the same object of power (maqdūr) as the other. And this is impossible. So the assertion of the existence of two gods is impossible. As for the conditional, it is obvious. As for the consequent being impossible, its elucidation: Since each of them is powerful over all possibles, then everything that is within the power of this one is exactly the same object of power for that one. And if that object of power occurs, then either it occurs by both together, or it occurs by one of them, or it occurs by one without the second. The three divisions are all false, so the assertion of two gods is false. We say: It is impossible for it to occur by both together, because when this powerful agent is independent in bringing about (īqā‘), and that act with this agent becomes necessarily occurring by him, the necessity of its occurrence by him prevents it from being attributed to the second, and the necessity of its occurrence by the second prevents it from being attributed to the first. So if they both concur upon it, then the necessity of its occurrence by each of them would prevent its occurrence by the second, so it would follow that it occurs by both while simultaneously it is impossible for it to occur by each of them, and that necessitates the combination of two contradictories (jam‘ bayn al-naqīḍayn), which is impossible. And we say: It is impossible for it not to occur by either of them, because the preventer of its occurrence by this one is nothing other than its occurrence by that one and the opposite. And the effect (athar) only exists upon the existence of the cause (mu’aththir). So if it were impossible by both together, it would necessitate that it occur by each of them, so that its occurrence by this one prevents its occurrence by that one and the opposite. Then it would follow that it occurs by each of them. The conclusion: In the first division, its occurrence by each of them prevents its occurrence by both. In the second, its non-occurrence by both necessitates its occurrence by both, because the effect only exists upon the existence of the cause, and the cause of the impossibility of its occurrence by each of them is its occurrence by the other. And it is impossible for it to occur by one without the second, because since they are equal in effect (ta’thīr) and in power, its occurrence by one without the other would be a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderant, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for God's oneness based on kalam principles. It is a structured disproof by contradiction.

The proof begins with a conditional: If there were two omnipotent gods, then each would have power over exactly the same objects as the other. The impossibility is shown by considering three possibilities for any given effect (maqdūr): (1) it occurs by both together, (2) it occurs by neither, (3) it occurs by one alone. Each is shown to be impossible.

For (1): If the effect occurs by both together, then each agent independently necessitates it. The necessity of its occurrence by one prevents its attribution to the other, leading to the contradiction that it both occurs and does not occur by each. Hence a combination of contradictories.

For (2): If it occurs by neither, then there must be a preventer (māni‘) for each. The only possible preventer for one is its occurrence by the other or its opposite. But if it does not occur by either, there is no such preventer, and the effect would still require a cause. So non-occurrence by both forces occurrence by each, a contradiction.

For (3): If it occurs by one alone without the other, then since both are equal in power and effect, this would be a preponderance without a preponderant (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ), which is impossible.

All three logical possibilities are impossible, so the hypothesis of two omnipotent gods is impossible. This proof is more detailed than the first and examines the coordination of two agents on a single effect.

Key Terms
قادر (qādir)
مقدور (maqdūr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that two gods could cooperate harmoniously, so the second proof is unnecessary.
Correction: The proof shows that cooperation leads to logical contradictions because each agent's independent necessity conflicts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many possibilities are there for an effect occurring under two omnipotent gods?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The second proof for God's oneness begins by enumerating three logical possibilities for a single effect under two gods, all of which must be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is one and that no other being shares His power, does every moment of your life reflect that belief? Or do you sometimes act as if other forces (people, money, status) have ultimate control over your destiny? Examine your anxieties and ambitions—do they betray a hidden polytheism in your heart?
?
The proof shows that two omnipotent beings cannot even act in concert—their coexistence leads to logical impossibility. How does this truth deepen your reliance on Allah alone? In what areas of your life have you been seeking help or security from others as if they had independent power? Repent and reaffirm your tawheed.

First Possibility: Both Agents Together
Razi demonstrates the impossibility of a single effect produced by both gods together.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for a single effect to be produced by two omnipotent agents simultaneously.

Core Idea
Independent omnipotent agents cannot both cause the same effect without contradiction.

In this lesson, we examine the first of the three possibilities: that the effect occurs by both gods together. Razi argues that this leads to a logical impossibility because each agent's independent causation excludes the other, resulting in a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage that argues against the two acting together. It is a single continuous argument.
Focus on the terms 'istiglāl' and 'wujūb al-wuqū‘'.
2
Understand independent causation
Each agent, being omnipotent, would necessitate the effect independently. This necessity prevents attribution to the other.
3
Identify the contradiction
If both concur, the effect would be caused by both, but each necessity excludes the other, so it occurs by both while being impossible by each – a contradiction.
The phrase 'jam‘ bayn al-naqīḍayn' is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية في إثبات أن إله العالم واحد بناء على أصول المتكلمين : أن نقول : لو قدرنا وجود إلهين ، قادرين على جميع الممكنات ، لزم القطع بكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على عين مقدور الآخر . وهذا محال . فالقول بوجود الإلهين محال . أما الشرطية فظاهرة ، وأما أن هذه الملازمة ممتنعة . فتقريره : أنه لما كان كل واحد منهما [ قادراً ] على جميع الممكنات ، فكل ما كان مقدوراً لهذا ، فهو بعينه مقدور لذاك . وإن وقع ذلك المقدور ، فإما أن يقع بهما معاً ، وإما أن يقع [ بواحد منهما ، واما أن يقع ] بأحدهما دون الثاني . والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فالقول بوجود الإلهين باطل. إنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع وقوعه بهما معاً. وذلك لأن هذا القادر لما كان مستقلاً بالإيقاع وصار ذلك الفعل مع هذا الفاعل واجب الوقوع به ، ووجوب وقوعه به يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الثاني ، ووجوب وقوعه بالثاني يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الأول . فإذا اجتمعا عليه ، لزم أن يكون وجوب وقوعه بكل واحد منها مانعاً من وقوعه بالثاني ، فيلزم أن يكون واقعاً بهما ، حال ما يمتنع كونه واقعاً بكل واحد منهما [ وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن لا يقع بواحد منهما ] لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذا ليس إلا وقوعه بذاك وبالضد . والأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر ، فلو امتنع بهما معاً ، لزم أن يقع بكل واحد منها حتى يكون وقوعه بهذا مانعاً من وقوعه بذاك وبالضد . وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون واقعاً بكل واحد منهما . فالحاصل : أن في القسم الأول : وقوعه بكل واحد منهما ، يمنع من وقوعه بهما . وفي الثاني عدم وقوعه بهما [ يقتضي وقوعه بهما ] من حيث أن الأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر ، والمؤثر في امتناع وقوعه بكل واحد منها هو وقوعـه بالآخر . وإنما يمتنع وقوعه بأحدهما دون الثاني ، لأنهما لما استويا في التأثير وفي القوة ، كان وقوعه ، بأحدهما دون الآخر رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وهو محال .
The second proof for establishing that the God of the world is one, based on the principles of the theologians (al-mutakallimīn): We say: If we suppose the existence of two gods, both powerful over all possibles, then it necessarily follows that each of them is powerful over exactly the same object of power (maqdūr) as the other. And this is impossible. So the assertion of the existence of two gods is impossible. As for the conditional, it is obvious. As for the consequent being impossible, its elucidation: Since each of them is powerful over all possibles, then everything that is within the power of this one is exactly the same object of power for that one. And if that object of power occurs, then either it occurs by both together, or it occurs by one of them, or it occurs by one without the second. The three divisions are all false, so the assertion of two gods is false. We say: It is impossible for it to occur by both together, because when this powerful agent is independent in bringing about (īqā‘), and that act with this agent becomes necessarily occurring by him, the necessity of its occurrence by him prevents it from being attributed to the second, and the necessity of its occurrence by the second prevents it from being attributed to the first. So if they both concur upon it, then the necessity of its occurrence by each of them would prevent its occurrence by the second, so it would follow that it occurs by both while simultaneously it is impossible for it to occur by each of them, and that necessitates the combination of two contradictories (jam‘ bayn al-naqīḍayn), which is impossible. And we say: It is impossible for it not to occur by either of them, because the preventer of its occurrence by this one is nothing other than its occurrence by that one and the opposite. And the effect (athar) only exists upon the existence of the cause (mu’aththir). So if it were impossible by both together, it would necessitate that it occur by each of them, so that its occurrence by this one prevents its occurrence by that one and the opposite. Then it would follow that it occurs by each of them. The conclusion: In the first division, its occurrence by each of them prevents its occurrence by both. In the second, its non-occurrence by both necessitates its occurrence by both, because the effect only exists upon the existence of the cause, and the cause of the impossibility of its occurrence by each of them is its occurrence by the other. And it is impossible for it to occur by one without the second, because since they are equal in effect (ta’thīr) and in power, its occurrence by one without the other would be a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderant, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for God's oneness based on kalam principles. It is a structured disproof by contradiction.

The proof begins with a conditional: If there were two omnipotent gods, then each would have power over exactly the same objects as the other. The impossibility is shown by considering three possibilities for any given effect (maqdūr): (1) it occurs by both together, (2) it occurs by neither, (3) it occurs by one alone. Each is shown to be impossible.

For (1): If the effect occurs by both together, then each agent independently necessitates it. The necessity of its occurrence by one prevents its attribution to the other, leading to the contradiction that it both occurs and does not occur by each. Hence a combination of contradictories.

For (2): If it occurs by neither, then there must be a preventer (māni‘) for each. The only possible preventer for one is its occurrence by the other or its opposite. But if it does not occur by either, there is no such preventer, and the effect would still require a cause. So non-occurrence by both forces occurrence by each, a contradiction.

For (3): If it occurs by one alone without the other, then since both are equal in power and effect, this would be a preponderance without a preponderant (tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ), which is impossible.

All three logical possibilities are impossible, so the hypothesis of two omnipotent gods is impossible. This proof is more detailed than the first and examines the coordination of two agents on a single effect.

Key Terms
إيقاع (īqā‘)
مانع (māni‘)
نقيض (naqīḍ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction that arises from the first possibility?
A) The effect occurs by both but is impossible by each
B) The effect occurs by neither but is necessary
C) The effect occurs by one alone without reason
D) The effect occurs by both without necessity

Key Takeaway
Two independent omnipotent agents cannot both produce the same effect because their independent necessities contradict each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
This proof strips away any notion of cooperation in divinity. How does that affect your understanding of reliance on created causes? Are you willing to see every secondary cause as a mere instrument, not an autonomous agent?

Second Possibility: Neither Agent
Razi proves the impossibility of a single effect being produced by neither god.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for an effect to occur by neither of two omnipotent agents.

Core Idea
An effect cannot fail to occur by either agent because a preventer would be required but absent.

This lesson examines the second possibility: that the effect occurs by neither of the two gods. Razi argues that this leads to a paradox because the only conceivable preventer for each agent is the effect's occurrence by the other or its opposite, leading to a forced occurrence by both.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text on the second possibility. Note the logical chain.
The argument is subtle; take your time.
2
Identify the preventer concept
The preventer (māni‘) of occurrence by one is the occurrence by the other or its opposite (al-ḍidd).
3
Follow the reductio
Assuming non-occurrence by both leads to no preventer, but an effect must have a cause, forcing occurrence by both – a contradiction.
This shows the impossibility of the second case.

الحجة الثانية في إثبات أن إله العالم واحد بناء على أصول المتكلمين : أن نقول : لو قدرنا وجود إلهين ، قادرين على جميع الممكنات ، لزم القطع بكون كل واحد منهما قادراً على عين مقدور الآخر . وهذا محال . فالقول بوجود الإلهين محال . أما الشرطية فظاهرة ، وأما أن هذه الملازمة ممتنعة . فتقريره : أنه لما كان كل واحد منهما [ قادراً ] على جميع الممكنات ، فكل ما كان مقدوراً لهذا ، فهو بعينه مقدور لذاك . وإن وقع ذلك المقدور ، فإما أن يقع بهما معاً ، وإما أن يقع [ بواحد منهما ، واما أن يقع ] بأحدهما دون الثاني . والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فالقول بوجود الإلهين باطل. إنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع وقوعه بهما معاً. وذلك لأن هذا القادر لما كان مستقلاً بالإيقاع وصار ذلك الفعل مع هذا الفاعل واجب الوقوع به ، ووجوب وقوعه به يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الثاني ، ووجوب وقوعه بالثاني يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الأول . فإذا اجتمعا عليه ، لزم أن يكون وجوب وقوعه بكل واحد منها مانعاً من وقوعه بالثاني ، فيلزم أن يكون واقعاً بهما ، حال ما يمتنع كونه واقعاً بكل واحد منهما [ وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين ، وهو محال . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن لا يقع بواحد منهما ] لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذا ليس إلا وقوعه بذاك وبالضد . والأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر ، فلو امتنع بهما معاً ، لزم أن يقع بكل واحد منها حتى يكون وقوعه بهذا مانعاً من وقوعه بذاك وبالضد . وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون واقعاً بكل واحد منهما . فالحاصل : أن في القسم الأول : وقوعه بكل واحد منهما ، يمنع من وقوعه بهما . وفي الثاني عدم وقوعه بهما [ يقتضي وقوعه بهما ] من حيث أن الأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر ، والمؤثر في امتناع وقوعه بكل واحد منها هو وقوعـه بالآخر . وإنما يمتنع وقوعه بأحدهما دون الثاني ، لأنهما لما استويا في التأثير وفي القوة ، كان وقوعه ، بأحدهما دون الآخر رجحانا لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا المرجح ، وهو محال .

Third Possibility and Conclusion
Razi refutes the third possibility and concludes the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a single effect cannot be produced by only one of two equally powerful gods, completing the proof for God's oneness.

Core Idea
Choosing one equal agent over another without a preponderant is rationally impossible.

In this final lesson, we examine the third possibility: that the effect occurs by one god alone. Razi argues that since both gods are equal in power and influence, singling one out would be an arbitrary preponderance without a cause—an impossibility in rational theology. With all three possibilities refuted, the hypothesis of two gods is proven false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the brief passage on the third possibility. It is a single argument.
Note the terms 'istiwa' and 'tarjīḥ bilā murjīḥ'.
2
Understand equality
Both gods are equal in influence (ta’thīr) and power (quwwa). There is no distinction.
3
Recognize the impossibility
Selecting one over the other would be a preponderance without a preponderant, which is rationally impossible.
This principle is a cornerstone of kalam.
4
Reflect on the overall proof
All three possibilities are impossible, so the conditional leads to a false consequent, disproving the existence of two gods.
This completes the second proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's will could arbitrarily choose without a reason, but in kalam, actions must have a preponderant.
Correction: The principle of preponderance without a preponderant is considered impossible by reason, so even God's will is not arbitrary in this sense.

Key Takeaway
When two agents are equal, an effect cannot be attributed to one without a preponderant, so the third possibility is impossible, completing the proof.

Refutation of Three Possibilities for Two Agents on One Effect
Refutation of three possible attributions of a single effect to two agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the impossibility of two gods is proven by refuting all possible ways a single effect could be attributed to two agents.

Core Idea
A single effect cannot have two independent agents; each possibility leads to a contradiction.

This passage presents a powerful logical refutation of polytheism by examining every conceivable way two gods could produce a single effect. The author, a master of kalam, systematically shows that each scenario is rationally impossible, thereby proving that the existence of two gods is false. As you read, pay attention to how he builds his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Let the full argument sink in without analyzing yet.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage first lists three possible ways a single effect could come from two agents. Find them and write them down.
These are: (1) by both together, (2) by one of them, (3) by one without the other.
3
Understand the refutation of each
The author refutes each division in order. For each, identify the key reason why it is impossible.
The first leads to a contradiction of necessary occurrence. The second leads to a paradox of non-occurrence requiring occurrence. The third leads to an arbitrary preponderance without a preponderator.
4
Connect to the conclusion
After refuting all three, the author concludes that the existence of two gods is false. Reflect on how this argument strengthens tawhid.
5
Self-check
Test your understanding with the checkpoint question.

فهو بعينه مقدور لذاك. وإن وقع ذلك المقدور، فإما أن يقع بهما معاً، وإما أن يقع بواحد منهما، وإما أن يقع بأحدهما دون الثاني. والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة، فالقول بوجود الإلهين باطل. إنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع وقوعه بهما معاً. وذلك لأن هذا القادر لما كان مستقلاً بالإيقاع وصار ذلك الفعل مع هذا الفاعل واجب الوقوع به، ووجوب وقوعه به يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الثاني، ووجوب وقوعه بالثاني يمنعه من الاستناد إلى الأول. فإذا اجتمعا عليه، لزم أن يكون وجوب وقوعه بكل واحد منها مانعاً من وقوعه بالثاني، فيلزم أن يكون واقعاً بهما، حال ما يمتنع كونه واقعاً بكل واحد منهما وذلك يوجب الجمع بين النقيضين، وهو محال. وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع أن لا يقع بواحد منهما لأن المانع من وقوعه بهذا ليس إلا وقوعه بذاك وبالضد. والأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر، فلو امتنع بهما معاً، لزم أن يقع بكل واحد منها حتى يكون وقوعه بهذا مانعاً من وقوعه بذاك وبالضد. وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون واقعاً بكل واحد منهما. فالحاصل: أن في القسم الأول: وقوعه بكل واحد منهما، يمنع من وقوعه بهما. وفي الثاني عدم وقوعه بهما يقتضي وقوعه بهما من حيث أن الأثر لا يوجد إلا عند حصول المؤثر، والمؤثر في امتناع وقوعه بكل واحد منها هو وقوعه بالآخر. وإنما يمتنع وقوعه بأحدهما دون الثاني، لأنهما لما استويا في التأثير وفي القوة، كان وقوعه بأحدهما دون الآخر رجحاناً لأحد طرفي الممكن على الآخر لا مرجح، وهو محال.
So it is precisely the same possible thing (maqdur) for that one. And if that possible thing occurs, then either it occurs by both together, or it occurs by one of them, or it occurs by one of them without the other. And the three divisions are false, so the statement of the existence of two gods is false. We say: it is impossible for it to occur by both together. That is because when this power (qadir) is independent in bringing about and that act becomes with this agent necessarily occurring by it, and the necessity of its occurrence by it prevents it from being attributed to the second, and the necessity of its occurrence by the second prevents it from being attributed to the first. So if they both come together upon it, it would necessitate that the necessity of its occurrence by each one is a prevention from its occurrence by the second, so it would necessitate that it is occurring by both, while it is impossible for it to be occurring by each one of them, and that necessitates combining two contradictories, which is impossible. And we say: it is impossible that it not occur by one of them, because the preventing factor from its occurrence by this one is only its occurrence by that one and by the opposite. And the effect does not exist except when the cause is present. So if it were prevented from occurring by both together, it would necessitate that it occur by each one of them so that its occurrence by this one is a prevention from its occurrence by that one and by the opposite. Then it would necessitate that it be occurring by each one of them. The conclusion: in the first division, its occurrence by each one of them prevents its occurrence by both. In the second division, its non-occurrence by both necessitates its occurrence by both, given that the effect does not exist except when the cause is present, and the cause in preventing its occurrence by each one is its occurrence by the other. And it is impossible for it to occur by one without the other, because when they are equal in influence and in power, its occurrence by one without the other would be a preponderance of one side of the possible over the other without a preponderator (murajjih), and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the three possible ways two agents could produce a single effect, demonstrating that the existence of two gods is impossible. The author first enumerates three divisions: (1) the effect occurs by both together, (2) by one of them, or (3) by one without the other. Each is shown to be impossible. For the first, if each agent is independent and the act becomes necessary with each, then the necessity of occurrence by each prevents attribution to the other, leading to a contradiction when combined. For the second, if it does not occur by either, then the prevention from each is due to the other's occurrence, but the effect requires a cause, so it must occur by both, which contradicts non-occurrence. For the third, if the agents are equal in influence and power, occurrence by one without the other would be an arbitrary preponderance without a preponderator, which is impossible. The argument follows the principle that effects require causes and equal possibilities need a preponderator to determine one side. The conclusion is that all three divisions are false, so the existence of two gods is false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could cooperate to bring about a single effect.
Correction: Cooperation implies each agent is not fully independent, and the argument shows that if both are fully capable, it leads to contradictions. If they are not fully independent, they are not gods.

Checkpoint
What is the third division of possible ways a single effect could occur from two agents according to the author?
A) It occurs by both together
B) It occurs by one of them
C) It occurs by one without the other
D) It occurs by neither

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of two gods is proven by showing that a single effect cannot be attributed to two independent agents without logical contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen a logical proof that only Allah can be the ultimate cause of all effects. Yet, in your daily life, you often act as if your own efforts or the actions of others are independent sources of outcomes. How can you remind yourself hourly that every effect—from your next breath to your success in work—is directly from Allah? What specific change will you make today to depend on Him alone?
?
The argument requires a preponderator (murajjih) for any choice between equals. When you face a difficult decision, do you actively seek the murajjih of divine guidance, or do you rely on your own reasoning? What is one step you can take next time to ask Allah to tip the scales toward what pleases Him?
?
If the existence of two gods is logically impossible, then your heart should have no room for any 'god' besides Allah—no love, fear, or hope that rivals Him. Is there anything in your life that you treat as an ultimate authority or source of security? Will you confess it now and repent, freeing your heart for true tawhid?
